THE BED PAGE .
The seventh volume of the transactions of the
Australasian Association for the Advancement of
Science is sent for review. .It covers 1200 pages,
and is printed and cloth-bound by N.S.W. Govt.
Printer, who supplies numerous maps and illustra-
tions. The book is a taxpayers’ gift to the
A.A.A.S., which sells it for 10s. 6d. to the indivi-
dual taxpayer. The number of copies printed,
and the cost, do not appear: one surmises 1000,
and £750. The book consists chiefly of papers
read at the Association’s seventh meeting, held at
Sydney last year; and these papers are printed
separately and a number of copies presented by
the taxpayers to each paper-contributor, who
usually re-presents them to individual taxpayers,
his friends. The process of printing and presen-
tation is part of N.S.W. contribution to “ Science ”
—which is a vague entity, with occasionally some
astonishing attributes.
• * * *
The Association meets biennially in different
provinces ; and it appears that all the provinces
excepting Westralia have in turn given liberal
assistance in money and printing. Meetings were
for several years held annually; but latterly the
zeal at the Association’s disposal has only sufficed
for a meeting every two years. The A.A.A.S.
was founded in 1888. Its first meeting was
attended by 850 members; then the numbers run
1162, 550, 600, 488, 524, to 685 at last meeting.
Public interest in the Association’s operations is
not increasing, it seems. The last balance-sheet
shows three years’ receipts as £886; made up of
subscriptions £679, a profit from previous meeting
of <£7o, interest on mortgage £123, and sales of
vols. of proceedings £l2 odd—from which it ap-
pears that the individual taxpayer con tents him-
self with purchasing less than a dozen volumes a
year. With the exception of £43 carried to
investment account, the income went in office
expenses, printing, advertising, expenses of meet-
ings, and so on. A L-Wlertafel concert cost the
Association £35.
• * * *
The Association's total inoome during its ten
years’ existence was some £6750. It has £l4OO
invested on mortgage; £lBO loose cash; and it
has devoted £9O to “assisting science.” The rest
has gone to grease the wheels. But there is no
bread-and-sack implication ; for pan-Australasian
machinery needs a lot of grease, and much labour
is given gratis. Professor Liversidge, for example,
who lives at Sydney University and edits the 1200-
page volume, gets nothing for the work. He is
permanent secretary, and occasionally president as
well—and both are honorary positions. Beside
the traditional reward of virtue, the only remune-
ration attached to his office by the 1200-page
volume is the pleasure of forwarding an address to
Queen Victoria, expressing the A.A.A.S.’s “deep
affection and loyalty,” and congratulating
the _ lady on her goodness in permitting
“Science” to advance during a 60-years’
For this address the President received
Queen Victoria’s deputy’s “warm thanks,” and
there is apparently some obscure attribute of
“ Science ” which feels better for the “thanks.”
• • * •
A considerable portion of the Association’s re-
port is taken up in printing the names and ad-
dresses of cometary gentlemen who fly through
‘Science” with long tails of letters attached to
their names. This letter-phenomenon is scientifi-
cally interesting, for it represents the survival in
“civilised ” times of the savage’s habit of adorning
himself with shells and gaudy stones. Brown-
ascends from South-Sea Chief
vv obblegobble with three feathers in his wool.
Smith - Robinson, M.A., L.L.D., F.R.A.S.,
stalks as proudly through the 1200-page volume
as the distinguished Papuan who walks in
ceremonial procession with nine streaks of paint
and an elegant nose-ring. Labels, as labor-saving
devices, have their uses. When you are intro-
duced to “ Tomkins, geologist, ” you are given a
clue by which to unravel Tomkins, and are saved
from wasting upon him your pet ideas regarding
the functions of the thyroid gland. But letters
are such inefficient labels,, and are really not deco-
rative. A reversion to the paint-and-feathers
method of adornment need make the meetings of
the A.A.A.S. no less scientific, and would cer-
tainly make them more aesthetically attractive.
* * * *
Passing through the cometary belt, one comes
upon the large space occupied by scientific stars of
various magnitudes. Not all the papers read to
the Association are printed ; but the selection
ranges over many fields of valuable research, and
testifies that the objects of the A.A.A.S. are being
assiduously chased over Australasia. These objects
are—
To give a stronger impulse and a more systematic direc-
tion to scientific enquiry; to promote the intercourse of
those who cultivate science in different parts of the Aus-
tralasian colonies and in other countries ; to obtain more
general attention to the objeots of science, and a removal
Of any disadvantages of a public kind which may impede
its progress.
And it is fair to say that the Association seems to
be catching its objects as well as chasing them.
* * * •
The seventh volume of transactions suggests
more comments than can be here set down. One
obvious reflection after survey of the papers is
that the faculty of generalisation tne most
valuable faculty of the scientific mind—seems to be
rara avis in terris. Nearly all the authors are
occupied with detail-observations of phenomena ;
and in many branches of science detail-observa-
tions have _ accumulated past reckoning. As
Literature is being buried beneath books, so
Science is entombed under “ records ” —often made
with the greatest care, and involving lifetimes of
scientific labour, yet useless for want of collation,
classification and reduction to principles. The
earthquake-observations of the A.A.A.S. are in
point. There is a special seismological committee,
and much labour is being expended in observing
and recording; but it is doubtful whether the
observations so far have a corresponding scientific
value. All over the world observers are reporting,
but it is not clear that the British or other central
authority is profiting by the enormous mass of
records.’ While the little minds are counting
shocks, we want in Australia a few big minds to
deduce the lesson of the shocks, and add to our
knowledge why, when, and how seismic dis-
turbances are caused, and how they are likely
to proceed in the future. The A.A.A.S. should
set some of its members to consolidating and codi-
fying and deducing unity from variety and con-
trariety—-just as S. W. Griffith has tried to do
with the Queensland laws.
* * * *
Apart from its knavish tricks in the political
field, S. W. Griffith’s is a mind that does one good
m contact: it is so subtle, so clear, and moves
with such a power and sweep. Yet even S. W.
Griffith seems often at fault when there is question
of educing a principle from a mass of details. He
gathers data splendidly, but does not always
appear to appreciate relative values : apparently
his mind cannot rise far enough from the facts to
gain the broadest vision. Griffith’s contribution
to the volume under notice is a discussion of
criminal responsibility, with special reference to
his own view as incorporated in the draft Q
criminal code (has it not been shelved by Q.
Parliament ?) The section dealing with the vexed
question of insanity may be quoted—
-29. A person is not criminally responsible for an act
or omission if at the time of doing or making the
omission he is in such a state of mental disease or natural
mental infirmity as to deprive him of capacity to under-
stand what he is doing, or of capacity to control his
actions, or of capacity to know that he ought not to do
the act or make the omission.
A person whose mind, at the time of his doing or omit-
ting to do an act, is affected by delusions on some specific
matter or matters, but who is not otherwise entitled to
the benefit, of the foregoing provisions of this section, is
criminally responsible for the act or omission to the same
extent as if the real state of things had been such as he
was induced by the delusions to believe to exist.
This may be fairly held an improvement on exist-
ing law as laid down in the MaoNaghten case;
but it is still very far from the root of the matter.
For example, it may be held that a criminal act is
any act which is at variance with the rules made by
the community for the conduct of the individual,
and called laws.
The measure of the criminality of such an act
is determined solely by the magnitude of the
offence against the community, as shown by the
extent of the breach of law.
The object of the community is to prevent
crime: so-called “punishment ”is mere vengeance
—which breeds more crime. The commission of
any criminal act makes it necessary for the com-
munity to devise the best means of preventing its
repetition by the agent or by others.
The sanity or insanity of the agent makes no
difference in the criminality of the act, so far as it
concerns the community. If either a sane or an
insane man is a danger to the oommunity, the
community has just as much right to destroy one
as the other.
From the community’s point of view, therefore,
insanity excuses no one ; and the question whether,
for example, a homicidal maniac shall be hanged
or asylumed is a matter (1) of the community’s
convenience, and (2) of the satisfaction of the com-
munity’s desire to prevent further homicides.
In other words, so far as the community is con-
cerned, the guilt is in the criminal aot, not in the
agent. The community should entirely dissociate
criminal acts from the personality of the agent,
and should view such acts wholly in relation to
the future, and not at all in relation to the past:
This is as good a theory of jurisprudence as has
yet been formulated, and it easily ends the
otherwise unsolvable problem of criminal respon-
sibility. Since we are all more or less insane, the
measure of a sane responsibility under existing
law can only refer to an approximate standard,
and any individual’s measure of another indi-
vidual’s insanity will always be more or less un-
satisfactory guesswork.
And, though the theory is dogmatio, Law is
dogmatic. It can never be logically proved that the
community has any more ethical right to impose its
standards of conduct upon the individual than the
individual has to impose his standards upon the
community ; and all attempts at proof are either
“ sacred ” myth or argumentative moonshine.
And the rest of the 1200-page volume is
waiting. A 1200-page volume !
MY PROPERTIES.
I own no park, I keep no horse,
I can’t afford a stable,
I have no cellar stored with wine,
I set a frugal table ;
But still some property is mine,
Enough to suit my notion :
I own a mountain toward the west,
And toward the east an ocean.
Just this one mountain and one sea
Are property enough for me.
A man of moderate circumstance,
A frugal man, like me,
With one good mountain has enough,
Enough with one good sea.
My mountain stretches high enough,
Up where the clouds are curled;
My ocean puts its arms around
The bottom of the world.
I do not fear my sea will dry;
My hill will last as long as I. . . .
And so I’m glad the mountain’s mine,
I’m glad I own the sea,
That they have special privacies
Which they impart to me.
It took eternity to learn
The tales they know so well,
And I am glad these tales will take
Eternity to tell.
I do not fear my sea will dry;
My hill will last as long as I.
S. W. Foss.
A New American Humourist.
In Peter Dunne, one of the editors of Chicago
Journal , America has found a new and veritable
humourist in the class of Artemus Ward and
Mark Twain. “Mr. Dooley,” a mythical Irish -
American, is the protagonist of Dunne’s-dramatic
commentaries on men and things, and he makes
the happiest hits! The Dooley book, just pub-
lished over the water, has not yet reached Aus-
tralia,, and this account of Rostand’s famous play,
“Cyrano de Bergerac,” is transferred from S.F.
Argonaut :—
“ Iv’ry winter Hogan’s la-ad gives a show with
what he calls th’ Sixth Wa-rd Shakespeare an’
Willum J. Bryan club, an’ I was. sayjooced into
goin’to wan las’ night at Finucane’s Hall,” said
Mr. Dooley.
“ Th’ girls was goin’,’ said Mr. Hennessy, “ but
the shtovepipe come down on th’ pianny an’ we
had a minsthrel show iv our own. What was it
about, I dinnaw.”
“ Well, sir,” said Mr. Dooley, “I ain’t much on
th’ theayter. I niver went to wan that I didn’t
have to stand where I cud see a man in blue over-
alls scratchin’ his leg just beyant where th’
heeroyne was prayin’ on th’ palace stairs, an’ I
don’t know much about it; but it seemed to me,
an’ it seemed to Hartigan, th’ plumber, that was
with me, that ’twas a good play if they’d been a
fire in th’ first act. They was a lot iv people there,
an’ if it cud’ve been arranged f’r to have injine
company fifteen, with Cap’n Duffy at th’ head iv
thim, come in through a window an’ carry off th*
cr-owd, ’twud’ve med a hit with me.
_ “ ’Tis not like anny play I iver seen befure or
since. In ‘ Tur-r’ble Tom ; or, Th’ Boys iv
Ninety-Eight,’ that I see wanst, th’ man that’s
the main guy iv th’ thing he waits till ivry wan
has said what he wants to say an’ he has a clean
field, an’ thin he jumps in as th’ man that plays
th’ big dhrum gives it an uppercut. But with this
here play iv ‘ Cyrus O’Bergerac ’ ’tis far diff’rent.
Tli’ curtain goes up an’ shows Bill Delaney an’
little Tim Scanlan an’ Mark Toolan an’ Paeky
Dugan, that wurruks in th’ shoe store, an’ Molly
Donohue an’ th’ Casey sisters, thim that scandal-
ised th’ parish be doin’ a skirt-dance at th’ fair, all
walkin’ up an’ down talkin’. ‘Tin to wan on
Sharkey,’ says Toolan. ‘I go ye an’ make it a
hunderd,’ says Tim Scanlan. ‘Was ye at th’
cake-walk?’ ‘Who stole me hat?’ “Cudden’t
ye die waltzin’? ’ ‘ They say Murphy has gone on
th’ force.’ ‘Hivins, there goes th’ las’ carl’
‘ Pass th’ butther, please, I’m far fr’m hom. ’ All
iv them talkin’ away at wanst, niver carin’ f’r no
wan, whin all at wanst up shteps me bold Hogan
with a nose on him—glory be, such a nose ! I
niver see th’ like on a man or an illyphant!
“Well, sir, Hogan is Cy in th’ play, an’ th’
beak is par-rt iv him. What does he do ?
He goes up to Toolan, an’ says he: ‘Ye
don’t like me nose. It’s a ilicthric-light globe.
Blow it out. It’s a Swiss cheese. Cut it
off if ye want to. It’s a brick in a hat.
Kick it. Tt’s a balloon. Hang a basket on
it, an’ we’ll have an asoinsion. It’s a dure-bell
knob. Ring it. It’s a punchin’-bag. Hit it if ye
dare. F’r two pins I’d push in th’ face iv ye.’
An’ mind ye, Hinnissy, Toolan had said not wan
wurrud about th’ beak—not wan wurrud. An’
ivery wan in th’ house was talkin’ about it and
wondhrin’ whin it’d come off an’ smash someone’s
fut. I looked f’r a fight there an’ thin. But
Tool an’s a poor-spirited thing, an’ he wint away.
At that up comes Scanlan, an’ says he: ‘Look
here, young fellow,’ he says, ‘don’t get gay,’he
says. ‘Don’t get gay,’he says. ‘ What’s that?’
says Hogan. Whin a man says ‘What’s that?’
in a bar-room, it manes a fight. If he says it
wanst. If he says it twict, it manes a fut-race.
‘I say,’ says Scanlan, ‘ that if ye make anny more
funny cracks, I’ll hitch a horse to that basket-
fender, ’he says, 1 an’ drag it fr’m ye, ’he says. At
that Hogan drew his soord, an’ says he: ‘ Come
on, ’ he says, ‘ come on an’ take a lickin’, ’ he says.
An’ Scanlan drew his soord, too. ‘Wait,’ says
Hogan. ‘Wait a minyit,’ he says. ‘I must
think,’ he says. ‘I must think a pome,’ he
says. ‘Whiniver I fight,’ he says, ‘I always
have a pome, ’he says. ‘ G-lory be, ’ says I, ‘ there’s
Scanlan’s chanst to give it to him,’ I says. But
Scanlan was as slow as a dhray, an’ befure he
oud get action Hogan was at him, l’adin’ with th’
pome an’ counthrin’ with th’ soord. ‘ I’ll call this
pome, ’ he says, ‘ a pome about a gazabo I wanst had
a dool with in Finucane’s Hall, ’ he says. ‘ I’ll
threat ye r-right, ’ he says, ‘ an’ at th’ last line I’ll
hand ye. wan, ’he says. An’ he done it. ‘Go in, ’
he says in th’ pome, ‘go in an’ do ye’er worst, ’ he
says. ‘ I make a pass at ye’er stomak,’ he says ;
‘ I cross ye with me r-right,’ he says, ‘ an’, he says,
‘at the last line,’ he says, ‘I soak ye,’he says.
An’ he done it. _ Th’ minyit ’twas over with th’
Eome ’twas off with Scanlan. Th’ soord wint into
im an’ he sunk down to th’ flure an’ they had to
carry him off. Well, sir, Hogan was that
proud ye cudden’t hold him f’r th’ r-rest
iv th’ night. He wint ar-round ivr.ywhcre
stickin’ people an’ soakin’ thim with pothry.
He’s a gr-reat pote is this here Hogan, an’ a
gr-reat fighter. He done thim all at both, but like
me ol’ frind Jawn L., he come to th’ end. A man
dhropped a two-be-four on his head wan day an’
he died. Honoria Casey was with him as he
passed away, an’ she says: ‘ How d’ye feel ?
‘ All r-right, ’ says Hogan ; ‘ but wan thing I’ll tel
ye has made life worth livin’, ’he says. ‘ What's
that ? ’ says Miss Casey. ‘ I know, ’ says I: * anny-
wan cud guess it. He manes his nose,’ I says.
But ivrywan on th’ stage give it up. ‘Ye don’t
know,’ says Hogan,; ‘’tis me hat,’ he says, an
maltin’ a low bow to th’ audjence he fell to th’ flure
so hard that his nose fell off an’ rowled down on
Mike Finnegan. ‘ I don’t like th’ play, ’ says Fin-
negan, ‘an’l’ll break ye’er nose,’he says, an’he
done it. He’s a wild divvle. Hogan thried to
rayturn th’ compliment on th’ sidewalk afther-
ward, but he cudden’t think iv a pome, an’ Finne-
gan done him. ”
“Well,” said Mr. Hennessy, “I’d like to’ve
been there to see th’ fightin’.”
“ In th’ play ? ” asked Mr. Dooley.
“ No,” said Mr. Hennessy; “on the sidewalk.”

Frank Harris’s Saturday Review papers on
Shakespeare are to be republished. He concludes
that Shakespeare has painted himself as the pro-
tagonist in thirteen of his chief plays :
In Love’s Labour’s Lost he is Biron.
„ Two Gentlemen of Verona „ Valentine.
~ Romeo and Juliet ~ Borneo.
~ As You Like It „ Jaques.
„ Twelfth Night „ The Duke.
„ Hamlet „ Hamlet.
„ Measure for Measure „ The Duke.
„ Julius Ccesar „ Brutus.
~ Macbeth „ Macbeth.
„ Troilus and Cressida „ Troilus.
„ Timon „ Timon.
~ Gymbeline „ Posthumus.
~ The Tempest „ Prospero.
These characters can easily be separated into two
types. The heart of the man and his poet’s tem-
perament are embodied in Biron, Romeo, Troilus,
and the Duke of “Twelfth Night ”; the intellect
and character of the man are incorporated in
Valentine, Jaques, Brutus, Hamlet, Macbeth,
Posthumus, and Prospero.
Some of these identifications seem debatable. But
Harris’s main idea undoubtedly deserves more
stress than it has hitherto received. Shakespeare’s
plays should be considered and criticised not as
detached achievements, but as related products of
Shakespeare the man.
PETER, THE ORTHODOX.
“Pete, you’re a common laughing-stock,
You are the village butt,
Your hair is so outrageous long—
Why don’t you have it cut? ”
“ Bekase dere ain’t no barber, sah,
Dat’s goot ernuff foh me;
Dere ain’t no barber in dis town
Dat’s up to my idee.”
“Why, there is ’Rastus Graham, Pete,
A barber up to par.”
“La ! yes ; but then I kain’t hev him,
Foh he’s a Baptis’, sah.
No low-down Baptis’ heretic
So bigotty ez he
Shall never cut de ha’r upon
A Meferdis like me.”
“But Pratt’s a barber just as good
As any on the list;
A splendid barber, and besides
An earnest Methodist.”
“He am a Meferdis, I know,
But I kain’t train wiv Pratt
Bekase I am a ’Publican
An’ he’s a Dimmerkrat.”
“Butthere is Bangs, a Methodist,
A very righteous man,
A Methodist in high repute,
A good Republican.”
“But he’s a homerpaff, the wretch,
Ez bad ez he can be,
An’ he kain’t cut de wool on sich
An aliopaff ez me.
“ I stall’s foh righteousness, I does,
Foh troof an’ nuffin less;
No Baptis’ trash an’ homerpaffs
Can suit iny piousness.
W’en some good barber comes to town,
A Meferdis fair an’ squar’,
An aliopaff an’ ’Publican,
W’y, he can cut my ha’r.”
S. W. Foss.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ Australia is the one place in the world
where the towns are more beautiful than the
country,” said Sidney Webb in a recent London
address. Webb found the Botanical Gardens in
the Australian capitals “dreams of loveliness,”
but got tired of gum-trees. In the same address
he advises skilled artisans to “transfer themselves
to Australia,” but declares that there is no room
here for the unskilled laborer. But it is the un-
skilled laborer that Dreary Dickson is going to
shovel into Leperland—to compete with Kanakas.
The Man from Cog-hill’s Creek.
He ordered first a whisky, then he drank a pint of
beer.
And gazed on those around him with a happy sort
of leer;
He said: “You coves wot’s willin’ give the
blanky thing a name ”
Like soldiers at the bugle-call before the bar they
came.
“You’ll stop to-night, old party, eh ! ” said the
barman, “ Bill the Sneak.”
“ Yes, rather tired of padding, see, I came from
Coghill’s Creek. ”
TI ie pints were ranged in order fair along the
grimy bar,
“Whose beer is that ?” enquired the man who’d
trodden tracks afar.
“M'Cracken’s,” said the publican, “and good it
is at that;
It has a head that can’t be beat, you bet your
bloomin’ hat.”
“ Two rounds of drinks for all the crowd—l know
things when I speak—
I’ll bet it ain’t M'Cracken’s,” said the man from
Coghill’s Creek.
The publican in pity looked upon the “bloomin’
mug ”;
And “spot the cash” demanded, as he wiped a
mouldy jug.
Then called him in behind the bar ; “ you see the
barrels there,
M'Cracken’s name is on thim, and ye’ve lost the
wager fair.”
“ Not I. You’ve bought and paid for them. Now
chew' the bloomin’ leek,
And pay the blessed wager,” said the man from
Coghill’s Creek.
“Not quite so fast, me hearty,” said the publican ;
“y’ know
M’Cracken’s agent kem last week and bought this
blessed show.
There’s the dupe-lee-kate agreemint, and, me
friend, y’re nicely jammed ”
The betting-man he whistled, then he muttered,
“Well, I’m damned ”
“ They keep me here to sell the beer at thirty bob
a week ”
He hastened for the setting sun, the man from
Coghill’s Creek.
BEN SUN.
The last Shift.
Our last romance is ended,
That had red gold for theme ;
The glamour of the sinking
Is but a dim, red dream,
Stored with a vague remembrance
Of creek and camp and claim,
And mates who worked beside us
Before the parting came.
Our hut alone left standing,
Its rotten bark awry ;
Our smoke the last wreaths curling
Up to the aching sky,
Staring at desolation
Where once worked toiling hordes,
And laughter rose at sunset,
And drifted western-wards.
But shift and shift have vanished,
Fresh fields to seek afar ;
We’ve fossicked out their leavings
From mullock-bar to bar ;
We two left lone together—
Grey mates grown old in toil;
We work the last shift, weary
With years of dreary moil.
Grey mates grown old together—
What fields our young days knew !
We followed fortune’s footprints
With “colors ” for a clue,
Spell out our tale of roving
By camp and tented town ;
And, hark !—the rising water
That frets the loose drift down.
For water finds its level
As we have found our fate,
After a life of roving
To duffer out, old mate;
To end our days in dreaming,
In musing and regrets—
And drive and shaft are crushing
The rotten timber-sets.
Oh, once we followed fortune,
But we have lost the trail;
Old eyes grow weak with watching,
And fate was, we should fail.
We’ll work our shift together,
The last the field will know;
Last shift —grey Death to follow—
’Tis luck we end it so.
W.A.

Uankee poodle
I IN AMERICA.
The Legend.
IT was in the summer of 1778, Wash-
ington, with his army ever active,
determined to oppose the British
general, Sir Henry Clinton, who, having
evacuated Philadelphia, was retreating
through New Jersey on New York. The
forces met on 28th June at Monmouth.
La Payette conducted the attack. The
heat was so intense that some refused to
fight, and many fell dead from exposure
to the sun’s rays. Both sides fought
desperately. As the day waned, and the
flagging spirits of La Fayette’s soldiers
J were at a low ebb, a strange sight aroused
i them. Marching with drum and fife
was an old man, Jeremiah Wynton, and
his son, and grandson. The old father,
with silvery flowing locks, sturdily strode
through the field. The son with ban-
daged head fifing lustily, the youth,
catching the spirit of his grandsire, rolled
out with him the soul-inspiring rattle of
the drum. Father, son, and grandson
had accompanied the troops as volunteers,
and were ever in the forefront of the
fight. The fierce light, of battle was in
their eyes, they knew no fear, and many
a wild cheer arose, as on that fateful
day Yankee Hoodie was heard as never
was heard before or since.

SATURDAY, APRIL 1, 1899.
The Unpublished Manifesto of Anti-Bill.
The Bulletin had a most remarkable dream
last night. It dreamt that the provincialist
party in New South Wales had published a
perfectly frank and honest manifesto con-
taining a full statement of its position. The
document read as follows :
We, the old Cabbage-Tree Mob of New South Wales,
led by George Dibbs, George Dibbs’s brother, the two
Heydons, and 18 other assorted fossils from the unre-
formed Leg. Council, also Sydney Daily Telegraph,
George Pile’s dog, S. A. Rosa and Kohen with a K, do
fake our solemn oath as follows :
(1) In the days that were before Genesis the first Tory
rushed to and fro with nothing on, calling out, “ Oh,
Lord, preserve Chaos.” He had no money invested in
chaos, but he rushed to and fro merely out of his innate
objection to all kinds of progress. ' We mention this
chestnut to show that we are acting on old-established
precedent. We want to preserve chaos.
(2) Some of us want the capital located in Sydney
because we have property there, or trade there. Some of
us want to be sure that our free newspaper postage won’t
be interfered with. Some of us are against Federation be-
cause it means an inevitable reduction in the size, and pro-
bably also in the salary of thelocal Parliament, and we may
lose our billets. Some of us hate the proposed Federal
Constitution because it proposes to build both Houses of
Parliament on that highly objectionable thimr—manhood
suffrage. Some of us object to the referendum. Some
of us feel that we are too small potatoes to have any
chance of distinction in the Parliament of all Australia,
yet don’t want the ignominy of being left behind in a
mere local Legislature. And most of us are old men who
have spent all our lives in the little narrow rut of our
own province, till we have come to believe that this
great busy world all revolves round New South Wales,
and that it is the only place worth mentioning on earth,
and that all the virtues are inside it, and everybody out-
side it is a thief at heart, and that no other province
should have a voice in anything or an opinion about any-
thing.
(3) We have talked froth and bunkum about the un-
paralleled greatness of ourselves and our little place until
we are capable of going quite mad whenever it is
suggested that any other province should have any voice
in the making of the Federal compact.
(4) And some of us, like our naked ancestor in Genesis,
merely want to preserve chaos on general principles.
* » * *
(5) We are aware that all the invitations to federate of
late years have been issued by New South Wales to the
other provinces. Parkes issued one nine years ago, and
a Federal scheme was drawn up. It came to nothing,
because Neiv South Wales dropped it. Dibbs issued an-
other and dropped it. Reid issued another, and wrecked
it by his 80,000 minimum ; and then he issued a second
one. It is because it has always been a case of New South
Wales asking the other provinces to come in and federate
w, W> h er tha,t we represent them as a crowd Of grasping'
and dishonest harpies, who are always stri vine- to federate
with New South YVales for their OWn profit.
(6) When New South Wales Parliament rejected the
Bill last year by the simple process of disqualifying the
pro-Bill majority, none of the other provinces asked us to
move any further in the matter. If New- South Wales
hadn’t insisted on going on with it, and asked its neigh-
bors to make a new start, the matter would apparently
have been dropped. This proves that the other provinces
are all craving to federate with us from the basest motives
of plunder.
(7) The fact that New South Wales hks so constantly
asked the other provinces to conie in and federate with it
shows that New South Wales’s own 'motives are as pure
as the sriowball.on the top of Kosciusko.
(8) In this and Other matters we speak confidently as
representing the people of New South Wales. We were
horribly licked and left at the bottom of the poll in the
election of Federal delegates, which shows that the
people are with us. We were licked by 5367 votes at the
referendum, which further proves that the people are
with us.
(9) The old Bill was “ contemptuously rejected ’’ On Srd
June, 1898. There were 71,595 votes for it, and <36,228
against it, and 170,000 people thought provincialism so
little worth preserving that the-V didn’t vote at all. We
show that we won bv assuming that 'these 170,000 who
didn t vote for provincialism were all provincialists. We
don t bring- forward .any evidence in support of this state-
ment, but just say It. Our capacity for saying things is
something- enormous.
(1°) We say that we have a majority against the new
Bill, and we ha,ve got up all the petitions we could im-
.plonng' the Legislature not to give us a chance of proving
it by letting the people have a straight-out vote on the
new- Bill. We are a fighting crowd, hut w-e prefer to
conduct the scrimmage through the telephone.
• • * *
(11) Our strong point is that New South Wales is the
only province that can be trusted. The first Federal
Conference started w'ith an agreement that the minimum
vote should be 50,000 each for New South Wales and Vic-
toria and 6000 each for Tasmania and West Australia.
We were the only crowd that broke the agreement, which
shows clearly that all the others are liars and can’t be
depended upon for an instant.
(12) A year ago we were strongly demanding that the
capital should be in New South Wales. We have grot it,
but we fro on cursing just the same.
(13) Under the present agreement the capital must be
at least 100 miles from Sydney, 160 miles from Melbourne,
160 miles from Adelaide, 75 miles from Brisbane, 400 miles
from Hobart or 1500 miles from Perth. The language
we use against the Melbourne, Adelaide, Perth and
Hobart people about the insult put upon Sydney in this
matter is something awful. As for the insult which we
put upon all the other cities when w'e demanded that the
capital should be in New- South Wales, and thereby
imposed the above distance limit on them, it doesn't
matter. We have a right to insult anybody as much as
w'e like on account of our superior wisdom and extra
gentlemaniiness of demeanor.
(14) We started the Federal campaign over a year ago
by publicly denouncing the Other provinces (Victoria
especially) as “thieves," “wolves,” “robbers," “five
drunkards,” &c. This shows distinctly the undvin»-
hatred which these other provinces have towards us. *
(l'<) Ayearago we were busy pointing out that Victoria
"'“<* going to enact a whole lot of new bounties (under the
Bill new bounties had to be legalised before 30th June,
1898, else the Commonwealth wouldn’t permit them) in
order to gain for its industries a mean advantage over
those of New South Wales. In reply Victoria prorogued
its Parliament to such a late date that it couldn’t effect
its alleged base purpose within the time limit We
didn’t apologise for the lie and slander. Our finer in-
stincts aren’t built that way.
(16) Also we invented a beautiful fiction to the effect
that the Commonwealth would have to pay Victoria’s
bounties. We didn’t ask that this dreadful thing should
be taken out of the Bill at the Premiers’ Conference in
fact, we couldn’t, for we knew it wasn't In the Bill any-
where. The lie got worn to rags at last, and then dropped
by the wayside somewhere. Anybody finding it will
please return it at once.

(17) The other provinces agreed that we should have
certain concessions over and above the original bargain
the bargain which the people of New South Wales
endorsed by a majority of 5367 votes. Their willingness
to give us these concessions shows that they are thieves
and ruffians of the very worst type. Some people say
“ Thank you ’’ when they get a concession like that. We
said “ Blast! ” and “ Damn !’’ and “ You’re a liar ! ”
(15) The fact that the other provinces agreed that the
capital should be in New South Wales proves that they
are going to have it iri Victoria.
(19) If the other provinces hadn’t agreed to have the
capital in New South Wales that would also have proved
that it was going to be in Victoria.
(20) In fact, anything they could have done or left un-
done would have proved that they were going to have the
capital in Victoria.
(21) As matters stand the agreement is that each pro-
vince is to keep its own lands, its railways (unless it
chooses to hand them over), to pay the same taxation,
and to get back its share of the surplus on the same prin-
ciples. We don’t agree to pay a single copper for any of
the other provinces, and we are to have the capital.
Consequently we all yell at the top of our voices about
the great special “sacrifices” which New South Wales is
making for the other provinces. Full details of these
sacrifices couldn’t be got out of us with a steam-crane, for
we haven’t the faintest idea what they are, but we yell
just the same.
(22) We particularly want sole control of the Darling
and Murray. A great deal of the Darling water conies
down from Queensland, and much of the Murray water
from the Victorian Alps, but they are “ our rivers ’’ just
the same. Our terms are that we should have absolute
power to cut off South Australia’s water-supply in dry
seasons, and South Australia should have no claim for
compensation against us if we choose to let our exclusive
rivers do damage in her territory in flood times.
(23) Anybody who objects to any of the above proposi-
tions is a traitor, and is evidently in the pay of Victoria.
(24) Our general attitude is that Victo'ria is a poor-
miserable place without a cent, and that the amount of
money it spends in trying to buy votes on behalf of the
Federal Bill in New South Wales is something incredible
(25) Also, that New South Wales is the greatest and
richest and most progressive and intelligent province in
Australasia, and that its people, if they federate, will be
robbed and kicked with impunity by everybody, being
too imbecile to do anything in their own defence.' ’
(26) The fact that Victoria was quite willing that New
South Wales should have certain concessions over and
above the terms of the bargain, and didn’t ask for any
new concessions herself, shows that she is arrogantly at-
tempting to dictate the terms of the partnership. This
is further evidenced by the fact that Victoria never asked
for any partnership, but simply agreed to come in at the

ally, G. Dibbs,. puts it, we are expected “to bow down
at the feet of our youngest child ” and accept any condi-

ever any arrangement is suggested we say that it isn’t
fair to New South Wales. Sometimes we ‘state that we
could draw up a fair and equitable scheme in half-an-
hour, but we never do it.

Should Briggs he Hanged? `
F. J. Broomfield to The Bulletin
J n . view of the fact that the present N. S. W.
Ministry MS hever disregarded a jury’s recom-
mendation fed mercy of a condamne, it is to be
tegretted that ,hohe such was made in the case of
BriggS, recently death-sentenced in Sydney for
the killing of his sweetheart and her grandmother.
Ihe public are notoriously apathetic in all cases
involving the infliction of the death penalty, and
cannot be prevailed upon to take even a moderate
interest in petitioning for a reprieve unless the
extenuating circumstances are of the most patent
and sensational character. Nevertheless, 2400
persons petitioned for the commutation of the
death-sentence on the unhappy young fellow now
awaiting his doom in Darlinghurst.
* ♦ * •
The chief extenuating circumstances in the
Briggs case are not sensational; nor are they
patent. They appeal to the professional doctor
and to the lawyer who is also a philosopher; and
furnish another instance of the pressing need for
a, more intimate judicial discrimination between
the diverse mental conditions which may have the
same specific result in homicide or other violence.
The commission of the Petersham tragedy illus-
trates the old logical conflict between law and
medicine; between the legal definition which de-
clares that insanity is the inability to distinguish
the right from the wrong, and the medical defini-
tion which declares that insanity is a mental con-
dition creating inability to resist impulse.
• » * *
Surveying the evidence in the light of the in-
vestigations and Conclusions of the great modern
experts in mental diseases, there can be no hesita-
tion in proclaiming the opinion that Briggs was
the victim of hopelessly-diseased perception and of
irresistible impulse. Now, logically, it is not a
question whether the individual criminal is in-
sane, say the advanced alienists, at the exact
period of the commission of a certain type of
crime, so much as whether every man who com-
mits that type of crime is not prima facie insane.
When Briggs committed the double murder, the
obvious outcome of jealous insanity, exasperated
though he may have been to the extremest, he
was just as insane as any other man or Woman
who has committed, or who may commit, a crime
of like nature. _ The present legal definition of in-
sanity is illogical, moreover, inasmuch as pre-
meditation is excluded. Medical experts with
great experience of lunatics bear witness that
mad crimes can be both premeditated and methodi-
cally carried out.
• * * »
It may be admitted that it was in evidence that
Briggs, while in a state of frenzied jealousy,
similar to that of the hanged man Johns, had blis
revolver repaired; and there may be, further,
small reason to doubt that he went to the girl’s
residence with the intention of killing both her
and himself. These circumstances certainly
amounted to premeditation, which the law, as it
stands, makes the distinguishing characteristic of
wilful murder; so that, waiving the question of
the accused’s sanity, the jury could not well, upon
the facts, have found otherwise than it did.
But that it should not have also recommended this
man to mercy is a serious reflection upon its col-
lective head or its collective heart. And, assum-
ing that the head was in fault, let it now be
pointed out that Briggs’s acts—though construc-
tively “deliberate ” —were, nevertheless, the out-
come of a mental state wholly removed from that
of a person, say, who should methodically poison
another by slow degrees, or otherwise do that
which should alone be treated by the law as
‘ ‘ deliberate ” murder.
• * » *
Briggs, it was amply proved, had been for over
a week preceding the killing in a state of such
emotional disorganisation that, even if his plight
did not amount to actual and technical mania, it
would be manifestly absurd to put his irresponsi-
bility upon a level with that of the cdld-blooded
inurderer-for-the-sake-of-gain, such as, say, Frank
Butler or Louisa Collins. Such premeditation
as that evinced by the twain mentioned consti-
tutes “wilful murder ”in the fullest signification
of the term; whereas Briggs, though his final
act occupied but a few seconds, may logically be
said to have been committing it all the time for
days before its consummation. It would, per-
haps, be dangerous to relax the obligation—in
cases of such prima, facie premeditation as
Briggs’s and under existing legal definitions—
formally to convict of “wilful murder,” but the
mercv-recommendation has been made and given
effect to in scores of cases where it was less called-
for than in this. Yet, granting all the weight
attaching to premeditation, it is by no means evi-
dence of a sound mind. Nay, more, the repairing
of the revolver, upon which so much stress was
laid, does not necessarily imply premeditation
even of an unsound mind. Briggs took his
weapon to be repaired on 10th Dec. ; but he did
not quarrel with his sweetheart till the 11th.
Does this not suggest that there need be no neces-
sary connection between the two acts ?
* • * *
A number of persons have come forward since
the conviction to attest to a knowledge of Briggs’s
life - long weak - mindedness. Mr. Albert
Gregory, importer, of Market-street, was at
Darlinghurst both days of the trial to give evi-
dence in this connection, but he was ,not called.
He writes, however, that the convicted man was
in his employ for six months of last year ; that
Briggs, a grown man, was capable of doing only
boy’s work, and that he was regarded by his fellow-
workmen as a simpleton. Mr. James Grant, who
knew Briggs for 11 years, states that he regarded
the prisoner as a person of uneven mental balance,
and that two nights before the commission of the
tragedy he had hallucinations about “ men
Elanted” in the neighborhood of Miss Dutt’s
ouse. Mr. J. 0. Myers, manager of Messrs.
Jewell, Davis and Co.’s business, declares that
the convicted man was dismissed from his employ-
ment for unreasonable and passionate conduct, and
that he was regarded as an irresponsible being.
Several fellow employes say he would publicly
talk his love-affairs over with the smallest boy in
the warehouse and had grandiose ideas concerning
his vocal powers. Other friends and acquaint-
ances talk of cerebral disturbances, delirious
attacks, raving, mental weakness, apparent semi-
idiocy, his mother’s anxiety about his mental con-
dition, etc., and one declares that he knew him as
“ Balmy ” Briggs.
* * * •
Briggs’s mental condition is, however, most
strongly attested by his own incriminating and
incoherent letter, written on Dec. 13. It is a
tisshe of incomprehensible and ungrammatical gib-
bering—a perfect marvel of irritable incoherency,
seeing that it was written by one who had attended
a clergyman’s school for over three years, had been
at Remit,/.’ College, Mount Victoria, for four
years, and at Newington College for another four.
In the pathetic language of his mother, this re-
markable production indicates either one of t\yo
things—that Briggs was mentally incapable of
being educated or that his mind had become
utterly unhinged.
* * * *
Briggs’s case is one demanding the attention of
the expert alienist. Two doctors can send a man
to the frigid shades of a lunatic asylum on their
sworn testimony; but their testimony is of small
avail to the man on trial for his life. Either let us
throw open our hospitals for the mentally-afflicted
—and take our chances as a society—or let us
insist upon our legislators embodying in our
criminal laws some need of protection for the
accused person whose actions remove him from the
pale of reason.
• * * *
The points briefly to be considered by the
Executive in Briggs’s favor are : (1) In the case
under consideration, premeditation is no proof of
sanity. The homicidal maniac is always a pos-
sible outrager of human life. (2) The testimony
of employers, friends and acquaintances prove
Briggs to have been a man of weak and violent
temper, subject to fits of long-continued mental
aberration. (3) After 11 years of high-class educa-
tion, the letter written by the prisoner, upon
which the Crown strongly relied m its prosecu-
tion, would disgrace intellectually a third-form
boy. (4) The evidence of Mr. H. Leach, gun-
smith, of Brickfield-hill, attesting that the revolver
brought to him by Briggs for repair was delivered
at his shop on 10th Dec,, whereas the quarrel with
Miss Dutt took place on the 11th, and the tragedy-
on the 14th. 17
Historical Lies of the Freetrade Press.
“ Hermes ” on a large number of things :
The Freetrade press of Sydney has created a supersti-
tion that Cobden found Britain a small, insignificant
European side-show, and with one wave of his magic
Freetrade talisman created her commercial queen
and three-ball lady of the earth ; and that to become
great, prosperous and self-dependent, like Britain, all
countries that are not already rich must be Freetrade and
otherwise consent to conditions that will secure cheap
money by attracting British capital at low rates of in-
terest. This conclusion is reached by a course of argu-
ment something like this t Under present conditions, all
human energy takes the shape of cash, or credit for cash.
There can be no credit unless there is sufficient bullion to
justify it. Britain, by means Of her Freetrade, has
grabbed the bulk of the world’s capital, Therefore the
first need of a poor country that wishes to be prosperous
is British capital. As she has not sufficient capital of her
Own, she MUST have the use of British capital. And it
is better to submit to any conditions than to lose the in-
estimable benefits of British capital. Unfortunately, the
only condition that seems to tickle the British capitalist
sufficiently is an ironclad mortgage over the bodv, soul,
and property of the borrower. And the state of tile com-
munity that works on borrowed capital is little
better than serfdom. The only countries that
have been conspicuously successful with foreign capital
are England and Germany. Ancient Rome was ruined
by the wealth she stole from the rest of the world ; like-
wise Spain was by the cheap wealth she obtained from
America.
* * * *
When Elizabeth of England, or whoever was the in
visible ghost who directed her policy, set to work to
make England the equal of Spain, she established a rigid
system of Protection. Also, she laid herself out to create
a large reserve of bullion by encouraging piracy on the
Spanish Main and by prohibiting the export of'bullion,
and insisting, wherever possible, that imports should be
paid for with exports, The Government did its best to
secure a balance of trade in favor of England, All English
ships had to be built in England— except those Captured
from the Spanish and other enemies of God, If possible,
they were to be built of English material, and, later on,
when ships Were built of teak, the timber had to be taken
to England, and the ships built there. Farmers were
assisted by heavy corn duties, and bounties Were paid
on the export of cereals to ensure that plenty should be
grown in the island, All foreign goods Were subject to
heavy duties, and one law provided that all English sub-
jects should wear home-made head-gear, It is the cus-
tom of Premier Reid to repeat the Weary old lie of the
S. M, Herald and Daily TelEgr Aim that Freetrade made
Britain great and glorious. Which is absurd ! it was
rigid Protection and foreign plunder that made
England a manufacturing and wealthy nation. His-
tory proves it conclusively. When Premier Reid and
the Sydney “ Weight Dailies ” Say that it was
Freetrade they are either ignorant of English his-
tory or they deliberately tell historical lies. The
Corn Laws were not finally repealed till 1846, Eng-
land had nearly 300 years of Protection and grew great
and rich, She has had only 50 years Of Freetrade, and it
is open to question if Freetrade is responsible fbr ahy
improvement in her position. Nothing but the absolute
Smash-Up of the Empire could have instantly arrested the
momentum that her 300 years Of Protective prosperity
had given her,
Undoubtedly the one great cause of British success was
her cheap foreign capital. Between the years of 1500 and
1549 the wholesale eviction of yeomanry left thousands of
men who were born fighters on the verge of starvation.
Their very existence was a menace to the landed aristo-
cracy who had evicted them. And the gentlemen of
England set to work to solve the great unemployed
problem by killing off the unemployed. An Act of Par-
liament (27, Henry VIII., c. 25) provided that vagrants
were to be first mutilated and then hanged as felons.
They were executed by thousands, for the country was a
sheep-walk, and the woolly kings (like the same kidney
of the present day) employed as few hands as possible.
The executions went on apace, and in 1549 the unem-
ployed flashed into rebellion. Badly armed, without
trained leaders, the sons of the men who had carried the
English flag through Palestine and wiped the floor with
the chivalry of France at Poitiers, Crecy, and Agiricourt,
took the field against the Royal troops, stormed Norwich
twice, besieged Exeter long and stubbornly, and fought
pitched battles again and again. They were just ripe for
another struggle that might have ended in the destruc-
tion of the aristocracy when that ghost of Elizabeth’s
launched them against the Spaniards. How much
foreign (chiefly Spanish) capital Drake and his tribe
brought to England will never be known, but it must
have been an enormous sum. Anyhow it was sufficient
to start England as a manufacturing power, and equip
her to smash the Armada, carry on a long war with
Spain, and assist the United Provinces not a little.
* * * *
The poverty of Spain at the time of the Peninsular
War proves that the wealth she extracted from America,
which did not fall into the hands of the English and
other freebooters, did not remain in the Peninsula. It
went down the great treasure-sink that swallowed the
wealth of Rome, Venice, and every other rich empire that
has flashed across the sky of the world’s history—lndia;
which was another source of Britain’s prosperity. Ever
since the dawn of Time the Oriental has been a hoarder of
precious metals and stones. He has sent his manufac-
tures to the uttermost ends of the earth, and taken in
return bullion and gems which he buried in the soil or
great fortress-treasuries. In 1757 Clive, at the head of a
British army, burst into the treasure house of Bengal,
and viewed the accumulated hoards of centuries. For
those who wish to revel in the details of that treasure
house are they not written in Macaulay ? Clive not only
viewed the vast accumulation, but he seized it and sent
it to England. Warren Hastings continued the work
and plundered the princes and provinces that had
escaped Clive. For 20 years that tide of treasure con-
tinued to pour from India to England. Even by 1760 the
plunder of Bengal had started England on that wonder-
lul and hideous career of invention and rapid production
known as the Factory System and the Age of Steam. The
English used their vast plunder to increase their wealth-
producing powers and appliances, and to lend at high
interest to whoever had anything worth mortgaging.
The result is that a large percentage of the industries of
all nations are run by British capital, and Britain of to-

PLAIN ENGLISH.
Turner Will Consider the Matter.
A deputation on behalf of the sweated Melb.
tram employes recently waited on Vic. Premier
Turner. No tram employes were of the num-
ber, for the simple reason that the men are
well aware that any personal attempt to get
justice means prompt “sack.” It was plainly
stated by the deputation that most of those
employes who had given evidence before the
Commission of Enquiry, or who had taken part
in meetings to ventilate their grievances, had
been dismissed upon various pretences, such as
running ahead of time, and so on. The Premier
was much surprised at what he was pleased to
call the “ reprehensible methods of the com-
pany,” and then observed that he was gratified
to learn that the men had not asked for in-
creased wages, as well as reduced hours, as
it would be impracticable for some years to give
them both. Just here The Bulletin would like
to observe that the moderation of the men's
demands is only comparable to that of Premier
Turner’s attitude towards the company. The
men have been retrenched time and again,
until they are now about the most sweated
workers in the province. They are not asking
for increased wages, but simply for their legal
rights, eight hours per day, and the economi-
cal soul of Turner melts with joy at their
moderation. Then the Premier went on to
raise imaginary difficulties. The company
couldn’t carry on business if it granted the
eight hours, and would have to hand back the
trams to the Tramway Trust, and the Trust
wouldn’t be able to give the men better terms,
and so on. Which is bosh ; because, as the
Trust would only have to provide interest and
sinking-fund for the tramway-loans, and would
be under no necessity to pay 12 per cent, divi-
dends or 60 per cent, bonuses, it could not only
carry on the service well and give the
men the eight - hour - day demanded, but
could probably raise their wages and
perhaps lower the fares into the bargain.
Anyhow, all these shadowy possibilities are
apart from the question. The company has
distinctly broken its part of the bargain made
when it got its Act—the part which specifies
that eight hours shall be a working-day, and
anything beyond that shall be paid as over-
time. The penalties recoverable therefor would
make a very handsome surplus in Turner s
next budget. And the careful Turner merely
promised to consult the Cabinet; and said that,
if there were any doubt whether the Act had
carried out the intention of Parliament, he
would let both Houses consider whether or not
the men had been fairly treated. But the ques-
tion is not whether the 48 Incapables of the
Upper House consider the treatment of the
men fair or otherwise, but whether the Supreme
Court considers that same treatment legal—in
other words, whether the company has carried
out its share of the bargain, and, if not, whether
it should not be compelled to do so at
once or the contract be determined forthwith.
In a feeble and irrelevant reply to the state-
ments of the deputation, the Tram Co.
directors assert that they never discharged
a man for giving evidence or for taking part
in meetings. The Bulletin should smile 1
Of course the men who were sacked were sacked
for trivial disciplinary offences, and it was
only an unfortunate coincidence that they
mostly happened to be the men who had given
evidence, etc., and that almost every man who
gives evidence should be discovered shortly
afterwards breaking some rule or other. The
directors further state that it is because the
men are fairly content that they refuse to join
deputations, or take part in agitators meet-
ings, and not from any fear of dismissal ! And
this in face of the fact that over 800, out of
less than 1200 men in the service, recently met
to unanimously demand the eight hours to
which they are entitled. In short, the men
have demanded this right ; the commission of
enquiry has recommended the granting there-
of, and—most important of all—the original
Tram wavs Act, embodying the bargain between
the municipalities and the company, expressly
stipulates for it. In this matter the I ram way
Co. is a law-breaker, and Premier Turner,
who won’t enforce the law, is tacitly its accom-
plice, or at least its apologist.

Pulling Dungey Through.
The last lingering doubt as to whether Detec-
tive Dungey and colleague Dora Bromley
would ever be brought to trial at Melb. Grim.
Court was disposed of last week. They were
tried in a farcical manner, and they were found
not guilty of doing the thing which they un-
doubtedly did, and nobody up to now seems
dumosed to make a healthy demonstration by
smashing the windows of Smellbourne’s new
W Courts. From the outset of the Dungey-
BRdiEY case it was clear that the law had
decked to deny the private prosecutor any
£eJ satisfaction. The controllers of the law
tire the Ct-own Law Dept., the
a much smaller degree the Magistrates and
when the prosecutor of Dungey and BroMleY
applied to the aforesaid authorities for abSl ;
ance in obtaining the conviction of t o
law-breakers, he found b,m«lf
snubbed every time. Lhe law had be
guilty of contemptible conduct by e
ploying two people to commit a felony in the
Alleged interests of public morality, and the Law
decided to put a brazen face on the matter.
For reasons fully explained in The Bulletin,
the prosecutor of the Law’s myrmidons afore-
said was allowed to take his case into the Crimi-
nal Court. Having let him arrive there with
his little load of preliminary costs upon his
presumptuous back, the Law laughed at him.
Judge Hodges, apparently anxious to put the
prisoners at their ease, lost no time in assuring
the prosecution that he didn’t intend to lnnic
any punishment, even though the charge of
criminal conspiracy were proved up to the
hilt ! As a matter of fact, the truth of the
charge could not be disputed on com-
mon - sense lines of argument, . but t e
Law had private reasons for wishing to
preserve its Dungey from a verdict of guilty,
therefore Judge Hodges proceeded to talk
tommy-rot to the jury calculated, if not in-
tended, to procure a verdict against the facts.
“ In our hearts we are all satisfied,” said his
Honor, “ that Dungey and Bromley were
working harmoniously together, and meant to
trap these two people ” (the abortionists now in
prison) “ if possible. But the accused persons
had to be convicted, not upon feeling, but upon
evidence which convinced the understanding,
&c., &c. At the conclusion of this outrage on
common-sense Judge Hodges sent the jury to
consider its verdict, and whilst the jury was
leaving the box the bewigged partisan pointedly
ordered the warder in charge of Dungey
and Bromley to release the precious pair
from custody. Under the circumstances it
was not so very remarkable that the
jury, having wrestled with the sophistries of
Hodges for 90 minutes, came to the conclusion
that it might as well find Dungey and
Bromley not guilty of the legal crime which,
in any case, was to go unpunished. On behalf
of Judge Hodges and the committing magis-
trate (whose tactics have been previously
spoken of in The Bulletin), it will be said
that Dungey and Bromley deserved well of
the community when they plotted to put a
couple of abortionists into prison. People in
general are always ready to echo the Law’s
solemn cant concerning the particular wicked-
ness of the one crime which is invited by its
“ victims,” and which in 99 cases out of 100 is
a matter for all-round congratulation among
the guilty parties. But the fact that the Law
treats abortionists as heinous offenders doesn t
make the trapping of them by Dungey-
Bromley means any the less condemnable,
not to say hateful. The act of procuring
abortion may take, and not infrequently does
take, the form of an act of individual kindness,
perpetrated out of pity for a woman s distress.
Given a soft-hearted doctor, a scheming rival
practitioner, and an eager detective with a
clever female accomplice, and we have the pos-
sibility of a successful crime-trap, ten times
more wicked than the legal crime itself.
Attorney-General Isaacs, deserves censure for
having sanctioned the illegal action of police-
man Dungey and his degraded accomplice.
Panton, P.M., and Judge Hodges are to be
condemned for their indecent treatment of the
friendless prosecutor in this case. Abortion,
for all that hypocritical authorities may say of
its sinfulness, is the most easily procured crime
on the Australian statute-books, and the duty
of Judge Hodges and co. was to study, not
the convenience of policeman Dungey, but the
best interests of the public who pray “Law,
deliver us from temptation.”

Laud for the People.
Ex-Legislator writes: —
In a recent Bulletin the question was asked, “ What is
it that Australia lacks that prevents it being a great
country?” Well, to this deponent, who has seen a big
part of it, it is a great country, but it might be made a
better one if more people were spread over its surface.
To put people on the land has been the great aim of every
real democrat since legislation was brought to bear upon
the subject, and since the Robertson Act of ’6l, there
have been many efforts made by statesmen and others to
make it possible for the “ little man ” to have his bit of
land. In 1884, to quote a personal experience, I assisted
in passing Farnell’s measure, the most striking features
of which were, Ist, the partition of N.S.W. into three
great divisions, each subject to special provisions, and
2nd, the division of the Crown leases, chiefly in the
Central Division, into two parts, the one being secured
to the squatter, as that loud-voiced Boeotian, McEliione,
used to call him, and the other, fixed for a minor term,
to be afterwards cut up into selections. We of the
popular party tried to have the runs cut up so as to allow
the selector of 1898 and onwards to have a fair share
of the frontages, but, despite this, it was manifest that
most of the areas were “peacocked” in the interest of
the larger holder. Now that the latter’s time of giving
up the leases has arrived, he is sending his deputations
down to the Minister for Lands asking for yet another
half of the balance due to the selector, for an indefinite
period—briefly, as long as the Minister will grant it to
him. But I think the lessees need not worry Mr. Carru-
thers, for he is doing their work for them in his own
way. Everywhere there is an outcry for land ; at Dubbo
lately there were 215 applications for 13 blocks ; at
Warren, a month previously, there were 97 applications
for a paltry gamble of three waterless blocks ; and so on
wherever land is put up that is worth the taking. And
this brings me to another point of my grievance. When
the land was to be thrown open for selection, according
to the ’B4 Act, it was intended that the frontages should
go to the selector, but the traditions of the
Lands Department are opposed to any such ar-
rangement, and where the law cannot be evaded
by securing the land to the runholderliy a lease, the same
end is obtained by declaring the frontage a reserve,
which reserve is leased to the runholder, practically in
perpetuity. I enclose vou a map of Willie run, on the
Lower Macquarie, where 80,000 acres were to be thrown
open in 1898. After numberless postponements, 11 selec-
tions, comprising a little over 30,000 acres, have been put
into the market, so to say, to be raffled for next May, and,
as pointed out, the river frontages, with one solitary ex-
ception, are carefully reserved to the manifest and sole
benefit of the station-owners ! Finally, for this screed is
already too long, I have to assert that the action of the
Lands' Department is crippling settlement in the Central
Division, and that the intention of a fairly beneficent
Act, the best points of which are ignored by those set to
administer it, is rendered null by maladministration. If
Mr. Carrutiiers’ words of the other day, in reply to the
deputation spoken of, are true, let him throw open a
million acres of the agricultural land of the Central Divi-
sion for selection in one day. It would, perhaps, surprise
him, but not the writer, to see every acre of it taken up
by men whose good faith is unimpeachable. But who ex-
pects to see Mr. CARRUTHKR* make such a departure 1
The Council’s Reason.
The reason given by N.S.W. Leg. Council for
its amendment providing that N.S.W. shall
not federate unless with the permission and
concurrence of Queensland :
The Legislative Council insists on the amendment in
Clause 7, because, by the inclusion of Queensland, New
South Wales will form the centre, instead of one end, of
the federated States, and because the voting power of
the federated States will be more nearly equalised.
The first reason is very beautiful. Nothing
more about the alleged desire to make the
Federation complete, or about high-tariff
Queensland being the natural ally of Freetrade
N.S.W. The real reason sticks out like the
beak of Israel coming up out of Egypt; if
Queensland comes in there will be a chance to
demand that the capital shall be near Sydney
“soas to be central ”; with Queensland out, no
one could make that argument look feasible.
There is such a yearning in the hearts of the
Sydney provincialists to “consider the just
claims of Queensland.” Not by putting
the capital at Armidale, so as to be
equally distant from Sydney and Bris-
bane, or about Cobar to be equi-distant
from Brisbane and Adelaide, or about Too-
woomba, in Queensland, so as to be half-way
down the east coast, but by planting it just at
Sydney, so as to be as near as possible half-way
between Brisbane and Melbourne. The feeling
that a brutal wrong will be done to Queensland
unless it gets the Federal city planted (at Syd-
ney) just half-way between its own capital and
Melbourne keeps the N.S,W. provincialists
awake most of the night. The rest of it they
can’t sleep through thinking of the wrong that
would be done to Victoria unless the Federal
city was planted (at Sydney) just half-way be-
tween its capital and Brisbane. And they can t
enjoy their food by day when they remember
the irreparable injury that will be inflicted on
Westralia unless the Commonwealth’s political
centre is located at Sydney, so as to be just
half-way between Perth and Fiji—which may
come in some day.
A Broken Joss.
Old, sturdy John Forrest has comparatively
few friends now in the VVestralian press. He
is a fallen idol—a Joss that has burst, in conse-
quence whereof the straw is hanging out of
its forlorn stomach and its glass eye has
dropped in the dust —a shattered drum and a
discredited tin-god generally. If the Opposition
had a really able and vigorous Democrat as
leader, the remaining years of Big John’s Pre-
miership could be counted on the thumb of the
armless man. Since the boom fell through the
papers that used to adore John the Great now
rage about his misgovernment, his lack of fore-
sight, his deficit, his great loan commitments,
his inability to find work for the unemployed,
and his general dunder-headedness. Forrest,
even at his worst, deserves better treatment.
He mistook his boom, as Parkes and Gillies
and M‘ll wraith and Vogel did theirs, for
permanent prosperity, and the local press
sooled him on. He went in for huge loan
undertakings in the cheerful belief that the
population would always keep increasing as it
was doing, and while things went well the local
papers grovelled to their John with a great
grovel. And they mostly left him to hold the
great, shapeless baby all by himself when the
crash came. Hardly one of them told him in
the good days that his boom was only a tem-
porary fraud and that his Coolgardie water*
scheme was too big for a small community to
totter under; but now that the scheme is
started, and it is either necessary to borrow a
disastrous two millions to finish it with or else
drop the money already expended, the windows
of vocabulary are opened, and it rains condem-
nation on Big John for 40 days and 40 nights.
John himself, as a plucky and moderately dis-
honest ass—moderately dishonest in the politi-
cal sense—doesn’t in the least know what to do
in such a strait as the present, for he never was
a politician of much constructive ability ; but
at least he keeps his head to the storm and his
tail up, and puts a brave face on things. And
in some respects he deserves better luck than
he is having.
*
Throssell, M.L.A., Minister for Lands in
W.A., on Federation :
While I am an ardent Fedeirationist, and have given
the subject great attention, my own opinion, as a private
individual, and as the Commissioner for Crown Lands,
who has the responsibility of settling the people on the
land, is that we had better wait awhile. I know that
this view is the unpopular one. You will be addressed in
sentimental language by those in favor of immediate
Federation, but it will be your duty—having to provide
for your boys and girls, and to make arrange-
ments for their future, to look closely into the
matter, and to ask yourselves, “ Will it pay?” We are
only nine years old, so to speak—for it is only that tune
since we were granted Responsible Government—and are
we able, with our puny development, with our 200,000
acres under crop, to compete with people having the
benefit of vast loans, of Protection, of railways, of
mineral discoveries, and of manufactories long estab-
lished? ... I fear the answer will be that we shall have
to wait awhile.
Vast loans! Protection!! Mineral discoveries!!!
Why, these three sum-up the whole faith, con-
dition, and status of W.A.—the Land of Wait
Awhile ! And this at a turnip-show at Kelms-
cott—a rising bush-town peopled by four men
and some chickens !
Melb. Argus showed a feeble disposition to
speak the Truth about Judge Hodges’s ill-
treatment of the Dungey - Bromley case,
whilst the Age affected to regard the pro-
ceedings as a glorious vindication of the
Law, and in a sub-leader jubilated thus:
“It is said that there was some intention
of obtaining a royal pardon (Joe Chamber-
lain refused to entertain the proposal Ed. B.)
for the detective and his assistant; but it is far
more satisfactory that the issue should have
been tried, and thus effectually disposed of, by
a jury, since we are not likely to hear of
another private prosecution on similar grounds.
Yet there was some risk in the course taken,
since it is impossible to foretell what a jury
will do.” Artless prattle ! Judge Hodges,
by ordering the prisoners out of custody before
the jury left the court to consider its verdict^
had reduced the aforesaid “ risk ” to a 50 to I
chance. And, by the way, it seems that the
issue was not effectually disposed of. The
baffled prosecutor is now going for widow
Bromley “ on her own.”

The final polling at Q. elections justifies the
prophets by leaving parties almost as before.
Ministerialists number 40; Labor, 23; and
Opposition, 7 ; with two seats doubtful. The
Labor gain is thus three seats, and the Govt,
still has a substantial majority, as anticipated.
The quality of the electorates—especially in
the metropolitan and agricultural districts—is
still too rotten to make any better result
attainable, especially in view of the Q. pro-
perty-vote and bulcocking systems; but in a
few years, if labor modifies its platform suffi-
ciently to catch the moderate vote, there
should be a change. A Labor Parnell would
hasten the change considerably; but, so far, he
has not risen from the ranks.
♦
Premier Kingston has horrified the stodgy
South Australians who swear by the Constoo-
shun and don’t know what it is by frankly
telling a Methody deputation that he isn’t
going to disturb the present system of educa-
tion, although he personally favors a capitation
errant. Three years ago the province voted the
grant down by three to one; and Premier
Kingston says he is going to respect that ver-
dict until it is reversed. Immediately he is
howled at to “preserve his self-respect” and
resign in order that men whose views coincide
with the majority’s on this one point may step
in. It doesn’t occur to these howlers that in
nine points out of ten Kingston’s policy is the
majority’s policy, and that the other fellows’
isn’t; what they ask is that the province shall
put up with being misrepresented on nine
points in order that it may be fairly repre-
sented on one.
No Australian working men should accept a
situation in Westralia, or go there on any
terms at present. The Government is openly
allied with the most reactionary employers,
and recklessly bent on crushing the mere work-
ing man at every opportunity. In Victoria at
the great strike the Gillies-Deakin Govern-
ment covertly combined with the ship-owners—
but it did not openly enter into active hostili-
ties against unionists. Yet this is what is now
happening in Westralia. The railway depart-
ment is in active treaty against the lumpers,
and all workers connected with shipping. Be
it remembered that the majority of these men
have not averaged 255. a week for a very long
time past, while 355. is a sum almost unheard
of for one week’s work. Then provisions are
dear, house-rents high, and houses uncomfort-
able, the climate trying for men and dangerous
for children. The workman with a wife and
family in Westralia is to be pitied. He has
nothing to look forward to but depression. He
has no vote, no friends, no security, no com-
fort.

“ Alta ”: This letter from an alleged “ white ’’
farmer on the Clarence to a kanaka “boy
throws an interesting light on how the white
farm laborer is being cut out of employment by
the aliens :
Jack Thengli Maclean.— Please Jack,— l write you
this letter on Thursday night 2 March. I want 4 boys
come down on Saturday start pull corn on Monday. It
any more boys there some can come to chip cane by con-
tract. Don’t forget 4 hoys come Saturday. I want to
pull corn now it. is getting too ripe so come now I can t
wait any longer. I want 4 boys pull corn and if some
more got no work plenty work here clean some cane.
When we pull corn and clean cane I want you all strip
cane so if you want the job 4 boys pull corn and some 3
or 4 come clean cane come now or you might lose the
cane strip. If all not ready some boys come first. If all
boys don’t like wages you got plenty boys finish pull corn
quick. You can take contract if you like but 4 hoys
come Saturday with lolanthe if you take contract you
can take boat Sunday or Monday and get what you want.
Don’t forget send boys down Saturday, and you come if
you ready. I want to pull corn, strip cane, clean some
cane and chip some cane, all one job want plenty boys
and if you take contract finish quick. If boys not ready
you come see me Saturday if some ready send them down
Come quick now if you don t come now Indian men
might come and take your job. •
The alternative of being compelled to get In-
dian workers if the nigs, wouldn t take on the
job is a stroke of genius almost equalled by the
anxiety of the writer to get his farm-work
done by black labor and by contract. And
N.S.W. keeps its white men from starving by
painting fences and shifting sand !
Cheerful strain from Brisbane Courier re
recent hurricane :
The vast majority of those drowned were Asiatics and
Polynesians, most of whom were single men. Of over
400 lives lost, not more than 12 or 14 appear to have been
whiles. Those who would have diving carried on wholly
by white men may now see what an awful blow would
have been dealt to" Queensland had 400 families been now
sorrowing for loved ones lost.
If this sort of logic is to hold good, then the
mining industry, which is so eminently fatal to
human life, should be carried on by Asiatics
also, so that no European families may
be left destitute by explosions and kindred
disasters. Also it would be well if the whole
of our mercantile marine were manned by
dirty heathen, so that no white sailor’s wife
may be left sorrowing when the coasting boats
go down. For the same reason it would be
well if our stockmen were Japs., our soldiers
kanakas, our firemen and engineers Malays,
and so on. What the Courier means is this—
“ Only niggers and yellow-boys. Let ’em
drown ! What matter ? ”

Controversy on moral aspects of the payment
of members question in N.S.W. Leg. Assembly
may now be taken as closed. Every man jack
in the present House pockets his wage. When
the Act passed doughty moralists announced
that, being opposed to the principle, they
would not touch the loot with a walking-stick.
For a while they backed up their brave lan-
guage with a show of resistance, but one by
one they slid into the rut and clutched the
monthly £25. It appears from a recent return
that the entire moral valor of that period re-
turned to the State something less than £lOOO.
Adrian Knox gave back £274 3s. 9d. ; Reid
renounced £513 6s. Bd. ; Tom Rose repudiated
£l3 155., and M'Millan scorned £lO6 15s. 4d.;
or, in all, £9oBos. 9d. ! The haughty moralists
raised such a clamor at the time that the
average citizen expected the renunciations te
total £30,000 at least.

Judge Hodges, in influencing the jury to
bring a verdict of not-guilty for Dungey and
Bromley, was reduced to awful extremities of
false argument. For instance—“ These two people
were working together for a certain purpose, but
we are not convinced that Dungey intended to
procure the persons Duvalli and Potter to commit
an offence, but merely to allow them to go so far in
their proceeding as to brine/ themselves within the
criminal law.” The Bulletin would like to pin
Judge Hodges against the office wall and ask him
to explain the difference between procuring per-
sons to commit an off; nee and engaging them to
do something for which they could be convicted
and sent to prison. If the offence wasn’t pro-
cured by Dungey and Bromley there was no
offence at all. The woman Bromley’s share in the
abortion arrangements would rank as part of the
offence under Maoriland law.
At the anti-Federal meeting in Sydney
Town Hall George Dibbs, ex-Republican, ex-
leader of the Protectionist party, ex-Protectionist
Premier, actually stood up and said this (Daily
Telegraph report):—
The Legislative Council harl also protested against the
Braddon blot. That clause was one of the most compli-
cated puzzles he (Sir George) had ever tackled, although
he thought he knew something about figures. The
“ blot ” was practically beyond his comprehension until
he had received light from one who had full time to study
the situation created. The Braddon blot meant that the
people of this colony, supposed to be living under the
blessings of Freetrade and a simple tariff, would forfeit
their individual liberties and be taxed somehow or other
to the extent of over £1,000,000 to carry on the Federal
Government. (Cries of “No ” and “ Yes.”)
The “ Blessings of Freetrade ! ” And the Pro-
tectionist leader and Protectionist Premier crying
to the people not to federate because it may bring
along Protection—the thing he swears he believes
in!! G. ex-Republican Dibbs is “ex” more
things than any other politician in this country;
he is the X-est public man in Australia. And
now it seems he is an ex-Protectionist among the
rest. Also he is an ex-Treasurer who says he
doesn’t understand a simple little clause setting
forth that 75 per cent, of the customs and excise
revenue of each province shall be handed back to
that province to pay interest on its debt, or
applied by the Federal Government for the same
purpose. Truly a financier who can’t comprehend
a trifle like that is liable to become “ ex ” at any
moment.
