George Rignold’s Shakesperian venture
started brilliantly at Sydney Criterion. Nothing
of a more cheerful character has happened to
“Othello” in this country during the last ten
years than Rignold’s revival of Saturday last. It
was splendidly staged; the leading characters
were well done ; anil George Rignold was 25 years
younger than when last he appeared in Sydney,
and he put his whole soul into the old-time
Venetian woe. The years of Rignold’s life that
the cheap melodrama has eaten were truly a
beart-breaking waste. That big actor is no better
than anybody else in melodrama—in fact, he is a
vast amount worse than some ; and when he can
do work like “Othello ” it is sad to reflect on the
time he threw away clearing himself of the
vulgar, sordid aspersion that arose through the
money having been found in the pocket of his long
Sunday coat. The brown warrior who took the
middle of the Criterion stage on Saturday is a
big, impressive, convincing personality, and his
splendid elocution is a surprise to many—he never
got much chance to elocute in the days when the
stolen money was always being found in his
pocket. It is difficult to say that he missed a
point anywhere, or to suggest how anything
could have been done better. Harry Diver’s lago
is equally good. It is certainly the cleverest
thing this clever actor has done in Sydney, and it
is one of the best renderings of lago that the
Society for being Very Tired of Shakespeare can
recall at the present moment. Diver’s lago is a
warm, friendly person, with a persuasive stoop,
and a gentle voice, and soothing hands which
seem always ready to pour holy oil on his enemy.
Nobody matters very much except these two, and
the excellence of Othello, with his square,
brown countenance looming up over his flowing
robes, and the cleverness of his well-dressed
deceiver, make success certain.
• • * •
The balance of the cast consists of reason-
ably good material. Johnson Weir’s Cassio is a
strong character without many fine shades upon it.
Miss Lilian Wheeler’s Desdemona might be a
little less faint and a little better-defined, without
doing herself any injury ; she leaves a memory of
a pretty, fragile being with a saint-like mass of
light, drooping hair —but still there is no serious
objection to her. And the ever-useful Miss Watts-
Phillips, as Emilia, is a thorough artist all through.
* * * *
The “ Othello ” revival ought to have a
good run—if it isn’t killed by the arid newspaper
controversy of the erudite ghouls who always want
to dissect Shakespeare’s characters, and publish
their entrails in two Columns of S.M. Hebald,
and draw learned deductions from nothing at all
about the hero’s digestion, and his dog, and his
wife’s dressmaker. For instance, the fact
that Othello proposes to borrow his wife’s
handkerchief on the ground that a “rheum”
affects him is the sort of point which opens
up space for great wastes of dry and track-
less inference, wherein the ordinary playgoer is
liable to perish for want of water, so that his
bones will be found there after many days. It
suggests that a Venetian general of those days
hadn’t a handkerchief of his own, and that he
possibly hadn’t a pocket to put one in; that he
wasn’t gentlemanly enough to care whose hand-
kerchief he used ; that his wife, who didn’t seem
at all surprised at the request, was quite accus-
tomed to lending the article in question ; and that
when his wife wasn’t about to lend him a hand-
kerchief he would probably have to use a leaf off a
tree. Matters like this, when earnestly looked
into, can be made to fill a good deal of space.
** * #
The new co. which opened Sydney’s long-
deserted Opera House on Saturday night isn’t
noticeably different, after all, from a number of
other companies which have ‘ ‘ done a perish ” at the
same luckless establishment. The house wasn’t
noticeably different either. It was generally
understood that wealth was being poured out like
water to brighten up the antiquated establish-
ment, and the establishment does certainly look
as if it had had a more or less perfunctory wash
behind the ears; but in all essentials it is still
the same quaintly-constructed and ungainly em-
porium. Notably the three painted angels, who
seem to have slept in their hair, still totter at an
obtuse angle on the curtain. The co. opened with
“Round the World in Eighty Days,” but some
unauthorised person has hashed Jules Verne’s
clever story out of all shape and knowledge, and
damaged it badly in the process. Some of his
alterations —notably his duels, and his stuffed
snakes, and his Egyptian Pasha who seems to
spend his time doing police duty on a wharf, and
his Indian cpieen, who is kidnapped and spends
four years in slavery (this within a day or two’s
journey of Calcutta), and his thief who steals two
million francs from the Bank of England (the
British ■£ has been abolished, and even Phileas
Fogg talks in francs) are childish, or thereabout.
The»co. consists principally of unknown people
who appear consumed by a great sorrow. Barring
W. J. Montgomery as P. Fogg, Edmund Duggan
and Miss Minna Phillips, there isn’t one in the
crowd concerning whom this writer can lay his
feverish hand on his chest and say, conscientiously,
that he remembers having previously seen that
person on any stage. “Round the World itt
Eighty Days ” is useful mainly for spectacular
purposes, and there being only a limited amount
of spectacle in the present version, even with the
baggy python thrown in, it is difficult to see why
it should exist at all,

Sydney Lyceum re-opens on Saturday
(April 1) under Charles Holloway’s management
with “ Our Guardian Angel.” Apropos , the pre-
sent writer discovers that in his last notice
of this co., when it played the “Shadows” at
Sydney Royal, he alluded to William Gourlay in
a promiscuous way, sometimes as William and
sometimes as John. He gladly takes this oppor-
tunity to explain that the bright and spidery
gentleman in question is William and not John—
that he is emphatically Bill—that he has never
been anybody else than Bill ; in fact, anybody
J”?o looks at him will see that he was named
William simply because it was impossible to do
hnn adequate justice by calling him any other
as J°hn Gourlay is absolutely unlike
William, and William is still more unlike John—
in fact, John doesn’t resemble William on any
point, and William is at least twice as dis-
similar, and their unlikeness is so phenomenal
that -The Bulletin has often been wakened up in
the middle of the night by complete strangers,
who wanted to point out the utter want of resem-
blance between William and John—this paper
really can’t imagine how the mistake occurred.
Anyhow, it trusts this explanation, however in-
adequate, will satisfy William, who has been
writing on the subject, and that he will consider
his identity as William fully established. If it
isn’t fully established, The Bulletin will estab-
lish it some more if it has to cripple somebody in
doing it. And if the same mistake happens again,
this paper will go out and abolish John altogether.

The reliable old show at the Tivoli exhibits
no sign of disaster on its azure brow. Cora
Caselli goes on doing about the most remarkable
things that any woman with only two legs to aim
at the stars ever accomplished, though sometimes
when Cora is in the middle of her maddest dance
she leaves the impression of being a lady with
eight legs or more. Dante also remains at his old
stand—the Palace —where he seems to have taken
root indefinitely.

The new Brough comedy co. appears at
Sydney Royal on Saturday (April 1) with
“Madame Sans-Gene.” The story of the gilded
wash-lady who rose to be an ornament of Napo-
leon’s Court was favorably commented on in this
paper when she hung her boiled shirt on the
ancestral tree in Melbourne, and The Bulletin
bespeaks her a favorable welcome in Sydney.
*m * *
Speaking as a man who loathes circuses,
the present ink-spiller desires to say that Fitz-
geralds’ present circus is a dashed good circus as
circuses go. In his private opinion the best of
circuses doesn’t go far, but, anyhow, the one under
discussion is as good a one as the writer ever hopes
to see. These remarks, coming from a man who
would rather never see another circus as long as he
lives, may seem like faint praise, but they are not
meant that way. All the Fitzgeralds’ performers
are artists—the horses, the Jappy acrobats, Ajax
(the gentleman who swallows himself and then
goes to sleep inside), the lady on the revolving
globe, the trapeze damsel, and, above all, the
hurdle-racing tiger, who is, probably, the most
astonishing show of the lot. The writer almost
thinks he could enthuse over the hurdle-racing
tiger, and he may possibly do so at great length
—next week.
** # *
The feature of the Broughs’ farewell week
at Melbourne Princess’s was a revival of “Mrs.
Tanqueray,” the one problem play (so-called) that
promises to break out occasionally when all its
gloomy kindred are withered and gone. Pinero’s
masterpiece brought back memories of B. and B.
times, three ladies of the first “ Tanqueray ” cast
resuming their places on the bill, whilst a new
Mrs. Cortelyon (Miss Bessie Thompson) proved to
be even a little brighter than her two predecessors
in the part. The performance on the female side
came fully up to the old mark. Mrs. Brough, as
Paula, doesn’t get the sympathy which Miss
Olliffe wrung from a Melbourne audience,
but she puts in a lot of admirable work,
and baners the furniture and things with
real dramatic force. The actress is impres-
sive, though not convincing. Miss Grace
Noble has strengthened her pretty picture of
Saint Ellean, the somewhat human daughter of a
frigid pietist, the girl who believes that a voice
from Heaven prompted her to leave the convent
and comfort her earthly father; whereas it was
merely the voice of Nature telling her to go forth
into the world and find a cuddlesome young man.
Miss Temple, Miss Thompson aforesaid, Brough
as Cayley Drummle, and Leslie Victor as the
baronet who loses count of his drinks, are always
satisfactory. Joseph Carne’s treatment of the
Aubrey Tanqueray character is tame and monoto-
nous, but a handsome, gentlemanly failure never-
theless. It may be fairly said for Carne that he
surpasses Titheradge in the matter of profile, and
looks more like a chivalrous sentimentalist at the
age of forty-three.

The Princess’s will reopen on Saturday
with “The Belle of New York,”a musical comedy
which boomed in ’Murka and London, and is con-
fidently expected to hit this country hard A new
company from New York has come along to do the
deed, also the co. will be assisted by Albert Whelan,
and a cluster of nice local girls, and the Firm’s
best endeavors for a gorgeous success.

To-night (Thursday) brings “The White
Heather ” to a conclusion at Melbourne Royal.
On Friday the big house will be closed for a sacred
rehearsal of “Woman and Wine,” and on Satur-
day that “ stirring narrative of life in Paris ” will
be played for the first time in Australia on the
usual Blandolt scale of expenditure. A French
tenor has joined the co., and numerous scenes of
foreign dissipation have been painted by Brunton.

The Easter bill at Melb. Bijou includes
the musical Leslie Brothers (who are always
welcome, more especially after a long absence),
beggy Pryde, Fred Poplar, with the tailor’s
dummy and a genial tendency to mooch abowt,
Whitburn and numerous other people of more or
less perceptible merit. Some of the attractions
are perhaps not quite as astounding as they might
be, but taking the thin with the thick of it
Rickards’show is a large return for the money,
and the scribe who gets there gratis comes out
again with a sort of half-conviction that he has
made two-and-sixpence profit.
** * *
Ernest Moffit, the most unassuming of
Melbourne’s artistic young men, allowed himself
to die of peritonitis last week. Moffit was best
known as secretary to Marshall Hall’s Conserva-
torium of Music, and right-hand man to that fiery
particle. His name figured from time to time in
a bottom corner of a newspaper advt., but apart
from his connection with Marshall Hall, the young
sec. had made a little mark as a pen-and-ink
draughtsman and a clever musician and instru-
mentalist. He played many parts—including the
trombone—and did all things modestly. Poor
young Moffit!

Melb. Alexandra has arranged to celebrate
the day after Good Friday by throwing open its
dusty doors to the g.p. at popular prices. Duncan
Macallum takes the management of the forlorn
hope on this occasion, and he will produce “ Lost
in London,” with old friend Augustus Glover as
principal item in the struggle. The Cosgrove
Comedy Co., which made some fitful appearances
in Melbourne last year, will try a further splutter
at _St. George’s Hall on Saturday. Also, some-
thing is going to happen at the Town Hall arid
the Exhibition Building On Sunday, the 31st.
Gounod’s “ Mors et Vita ” will be reverently dealt
with by the Philharmonic Society in the hall
aforesaid, whilst Walter Bentley, at the other
place, recites the “book” of the Oberammergau
Passion-play against a panoramic background.

A curious coincidence in connection with the suicide of
William Miller and Miss Allardyce comes to The Bulle-
tin’s knowledge. The night before Miller arrived at New-
castle, the handsome singer whom he married in the fes-
tive days when he was “ Lord Leamington,” of Hobart,
put up at the very hotel in which the deed was done, but
was not aware of the vicinity of the gay deceiver till she
read of his death after she had reached Sydney.
Brough positively declared in his late Melb. advts.—
“Absolutely the last production of foregoing plays in
Melbourne.” The “foregoing plays” are all favorites
and the most profitable of Brough’s bagful, which seems
to mean absolutely last production of foregoing players.
Brough must be anxious to go again where glory waits
him—to India.
The stupidest criticism (intended for a compliment)
that Titheradge received from any London paper was the
remark that he acted somewhat in the style of Wilson
Barrett. Ye gods! They ought to see Ti'th. in W. B.’s
original character of the Silver King to appreciate the
vast difference between the styles of the two men—and
then see him in a light comedy.
A certain clever musician, who plays Melb. Town Hall
organ occasionally, was up at a police-court last
week, on a charge of insulting some policemen. He
didn’t throw stones, or create a disturbance of any kind—
he merely remarked, in passing, that the policemen were
“ things.” It seems hard for a musical genius, with (or with-
out) a drop of whisky in him, to be fined 30s. for expressing
his opinion about things in general. Also, there is sadness
in the thought that the musician has now promised to
take the pledge, for with the organists of this world
success has usually spelt suction.
The benefit performance for Maccabe realised only
£207, but a number of private subscriptions have been
added to the pool, so it is expected that the fund will
suffice to buy an annuity of £2 a week for poor old Fred.
Better than leaving him to die in a workhouse, anyhow.
Henry Dobson took the chair at hairy, cheeky globe-
trotter “Oliver Bainbridge’s” lecture at Hobart, with
Crisp, M.P., on one side. As the talk went on, Dobson
and Crisp looked as if they were being sentenced to death
by Madden, C.J., and informed that the rest lay with the
Executive. Dobson had told the audience he was there
because Bainbridge was a graduate of a famous University,
and took an enormous interest in education ! But the
absurd little Cornstalk, in his hour-and-a-half’s discourse,
said never a word about the famous University or educa-
tion of any sort; he made a dismal failure, and told jokes
that were old when Joe Miller was young. When the
lecturer sat down, Crisp and Dobson bolted without word
or sign.
“ Weary ” : Your correspondent “ 8.E.” refers you back
to the early ’7o’s for evidence of Clement Scott’s free and
independent theatrical criticisms. The par. that “B.E. ”
quarrels with merely treated of the latter-day Scott, who
threatened to tour Australia on the strength of his long
association with the London D.T. The D.T. notices for
many years past have been of such a character that the
profession —in the words of Beerbohm Tree took
“ critic ” Scott into their sacred circle and treated him as
a dear brother. No man can write reasonably honest
notices and be regarded as a dear brother by mummers
or managers.
A ghastly farce is the average cable concerning
agreements obtained and successes scored by Australian
mummers in England. Such items are usually mere
charity-notices given by the compassionate cable'man to
the poor struggling mummer who must advertise or die.
Present scribe (who himself learned on the spot some-
thing about the system) was t’other day talking to an
Australian lately returned from abroad. “ I see X has
got a good engagement,” this scribe observed. “ Bah ! ”
exclaimed the traveller, “ I was one day in a New York
street on a bitter, cold day, and a man came up to me
holding out his hand with—‘Don’t you recognise me,
Mr. ? I’m X , from Australia.’ He was gaunt
and famine-stricken, and was buttoned up to the chin in
a long over-all—with evidently little or nothing beneath
it bar skin and bone. Told me he had mostly walked
overland all the way from San Francisco, and
had had an awful time. Asked me to procure him
work. I replied I’d speak to (a big’ entrepreneur)
and get him into the chorus; hut, would you believe it,
this spectre of famine wasn’t taking any chorus ; though
many a fine artist has been glad to get such a chance.
Since then he lias, I believe, got a billet —probably at two
or three pounds a week ; that’s what I should expect
from what I know of the crowd he’s with, and it’s been
duly cabled out, conveying the idea that that poor devil
of a starveling has made a perfect triumph. Another
musical mummer, concerning whom I have lately read
cables and paragraphs in ‘Our London Letter,’ met me
in the Strand, London, and asked me, for God’s sake, to
give him a shilling, as his family were starving. The poor
wretch had walked in from a mean suburb four-and-a-
half miles away, on the off-chance of meeting some
acquaintance who would part him a silver coin. God
help the average Australian mummer who goes to Eng-
land to astonish the natives !’’
London D.T. speaks of Ada Reeve as “the nearest
available successor to Nellie Farren,” which is just about
as stupid as a comparison between Peggy Pryde and
Nellie Stewart. Poor Farren was a burlesque “ boy” and
step-dancer, with a neat, old-fashioned method and a
rough, husky voice. Ada Reeve is a petite musical-
comedy girl, her style coquettishiy wicked-one of the
small and spicy sort—-and her voice very fresh and
musical.

The United Australian Mutual Fire Insurance
Company has become surety for the due administration
of the New South Wales portion of the late James Tyson’s
estate. The total net value of that portion is estimated
at over £560,000. The administration has been granted
to a sister and three nephews of the deceased. The bond
into which the United Australian Company has entered
is probably the largest of the kind ever undertaken in
these colonies.*

was bluff, but I guess the e.b. scooted some.*

COLUMN
The NSW. Income-Tax Department has a
ravenous hunger after the saxpence. When The Bulletin
got a verdict with costs in the Atacama case, t
day it found itself £2200 out of pocket after all was
squared-up-in other words, £2200 of the costs were not
recoverable from the defeated party, and they represented
the price of The Bulletins victory. It didn t worry
about that ; that kind of legal mystery is all in the
day’s work. But what is not in the day s work is the de-
mand of the Income-Tax Department for income-tax (£os)
on the money lost. The Department claims that the
±;2200 was not leirally chargeable as business expenses,
and it insists on reckoning that the lost £2200 is still part
of The Bulletin’s income for ’9B. But though it refuses
to recognise that The Bulletin paid away this money to
its legal representatives, and wants this paper to pay tax
on it, the Department also recognises that the money was
received by the legal representatives, and charges them
income-tax on it. The Bulletin is amused. It has
thoughts of writing a pome on the N.S.W. Income-Tax
Department. , • •
The City Council of Sydney met a few days ago
to consider the awful financial position of things, .and to
fix the rate for the ensuing year. The situation is, that
the Council’s income hasn’t met its expenditure tor a
number of years, and it has heaped up an overdraft of
nearly £120,000, solely through lack of courage to face
the situation and levy a big enough rate to cover ex-
penses. The prospect is that, including interest
on the overdraft, it will require a rate of Is. 6d. in
the £, despite all the recent economies, instead of the
present Is. 4d., to meet next year s expenses. If it was
proposed to reduce the overdraft a little and catch up
some arrears of street repairs it would probably require a
rate of Is. Bd. in the £. Anyway, the customary Is. 4d.
in the i', it is estimated, will leave a deficit of £12,657 at
the very lowest computation. After considering which
things, the aldermen blandly made the rate Is. 4d. in the
£, and decided to let the deficiency take care of itself.
Now, it is impossible to imagine that even the
gang of imbeciles at present in municipal office
would rather have a deficit than not. Also, they
don’t look like the sort of men who would care much
whether other people pay Is. 6d. in the £—or even 2s. 6d.
or 6s. Bd. So the only possible assumption is that they are
thinking of their own share of the rates that the
present variety of Sydney aldermen would rather let the
hopeless debt pile up and take its chance than pay an
extra 2d. in the £ them selves and keep the city solvent.
Anyhow, Sydney badly wants a new set of aldermen.
The municipal finance of the second largest city on this
continent isn’t good enough to run a roast-potato can or
a coffee-barrow, unless the front wheel of that barrow is
pointing straight for the Insolvency Court.
Judging by the report of the Sydney Water and
Sewerage Board for the year ended 30th June, 1898,
things are not going well with that body, and if they
don’t improve it will shortly have to sit down and think
hard over its position. In 1891 the revenue covered
working expenses and paid £4 ss. lOd. per cent, on the
money borrowed. Since then there has been a steady
downward movement, till in 1898 the return was only £3
4s. 7d. per cent. Last year the department’s revenue was
£34,077 short of the expenditure ; the profit balance from
previous good years was completely used up, and the
department was left with a debit of about £7OOO. The
revenue was £3OOO more than in ’97, while the expenses
were £BOOO more. A good deal of the trouble is set down
to the fall in property values. In 1889 the value of pro-
perty in the western suburbs was £1,656,000, and it was
fondly anticipated that it would increase by the time the
works were completed to £3,300,000. But instead it has
fallen to £1,478,000, and the Board finds, just like its
brother-Board in Melbourne, that, even after charging
much higher rates than were intended, it is falling into
no small orifice. Still, it isn’t the beginning of a circum-
stance compared with the Melbourne orifice as yet.
Apropos of the above a condensed statement in
the newly-issued “ Abstract of Victorian Statistics” sets
forth the incredible-looking fact that in 1898 the expendi-
ture of the Melb. Metropolitan Board of Works was over
£120,000 more than its revenue. This official publication,
being apparently sober and in its sane intellect, alleges
that the fearfuily-mismanaged institution which Bard
Fitzgibboii bosses and muddles, has progressed like this
in the last six years
Revenue. Expenditure.
1893 .. £178,458 £174,264
1894 .. 174,889 170,487
1895 .. 166,346 195,609
1896 .. 167,096 227,623
1897 .. 163,432 257,913
1898 .. 169,476 291,675
This expenditure has nothing to do with the construction
of new sewers and waterworks; the Board has spent
£2,678,000 of borrowed money on construction in the last
six years (and instead of the new works producing more
revenue to help cover interest, the revenue has actually
declined by £9000), but all this goes into a separate ac-
count. The £291,675 is ordinary expenditure —£25,000
for maintenance of existing works, £24,000 to the multi-
tude of large and small Fitzgibbons who look on and see
it done, and £242,000 for interest on borrowed money.
How long it will take, at this rate of mismanage-
ment, for the Melb. Metropolitan Board of Gibbon to be-
come utterly insolvent is a matter of very simple arith-
metic. The said Board is a sort of stupid and ridiculously
incompetent Legislative Council in a small way. The
people elect the multitudinous municipal councils
on a very narrow property-suffrage, and the councils
elect the Board of Works, so that dreadful
institution is far from any direct popular control.
The first essential reform is to have the Board
elected directly by the people on the one-man-one-vote
principle, somewhat after the fashion of the London
County Council. Then there will be a chance of a few
able men getting on to it. It would be worth the while
of able and ambitious men to try for seats on a body
which represented 460,000 persons when they could get
there by a direct appeal to the people. But to get there
by filtering through the municipal council of Footscray or
Collingwood isn’t quite the same thing.

Ex-Republican Dibbs, splashing wildly at the
anti-Federal meeting, asked his hearers to remember, as
one wildly irrelevant argument against Federation, “ the
state of the other colonies, and how, in the financial
troubles of 1893, those colonies had been sustained by the
gold of New South Wales ” (1893 was the year when
Dibbs’s brother’s bank and the A.J.S. suspended, for want
of gold, and the others were saved by State assistance
and a lavish issue of paper money with a forced cur-
rency). Poor old Dibbs, who is capable of pretty nearly
anything in the way of hideous blundering, here put
both his commodious feet in it even worse than' usual.
According to the banking returns (they can be found on

The Maoriland State Life Insurance Depart-
ment presents a very prosperous report for the year
ended 31st December, 1898. Figures compare with some
previous years as follows :
T i 7T I Total Added to
_ Premiums. Interest. income. Funds.
1891 £234,496 £96,399 £330,895 £132,603
1892 241,965 100,651 342,616 132,501
1893 253,574 108,109 361,683 148,292
1894 254,204 115,274 369,478 135,649
1895 266,032 119,980 386,012 164,009
1896 270,720 124,265 394,985 163,097
1897 269,688 128,433 398,121 133,764
1898 278,948 131.200 410.148 vm ■ -e
The total funds of the Maoriland State Life insurance - e-
partment are now £2,861,534, so with anything like a good
year they should touch £3,000,000 at the end of ’99. It
is the only one of the six large Australasian life offices
whose interest earnings and total income have increased
every year during the last eight years—in fact, most of
the others have shown a tendency to decrease so far as
premium income is concerned. It is the second largest
office in Australasia, and by far the largest among the
institutions of its age. It. has the lowest expense-rate
among the mutual life offices of Australasia, the A. M.P.
only excepted. It charges lower premiums than any other
Australasian office ; it pays good bonuses ; and it is the
only office with State security, at the back of its policies,
and a State audit as a guarantee of the soundness of its
assets. And it has produced these results by working in
one small province only, whereas most of its competitors
gather in business from seven or more.
In 1897 the expenses of the Maoriland State
Life Department were £l4 17s. 2d. per cent, of the total
income. In 1898 they had dropped to £l4 7s. The
interest earnings were £4 13s. lid. per cent., and as the
Department at its last investigation made sufficient
reserves to reduce its estimates of future interest to £3los.
per cent., there is a very large margin of safety. The
assets look remarkably good—about £1,000,000 of them
consist of State and municipal securities and £541,000 are
loans on the Department’s own policies. Such dubious
items as foreclosed properties and arrears of interest are
much lower than the average. Altogether, the only State
Life Insurance office on earth is a remarkably prosperous
institution, and the pity is that Australia is not blessed
with an establishment of the same description under
equally good management.

Melbourne Mutual Store didn’t have nearly so
gaudy a row as usual at its late meeting, when the
balance-sheet for the half-year to 31st January, ’99, was
presented. The news that £2OOO worth of groceries had
taken wings and fled out of the stock, and that no one
could even guess how or where they had gone, paralysed
the meeting. The Mutual Store, which seems to be
always in rows and deep water, has progressed something
like this of late years :

It is supposed that it was about July, 98, that the
groceries absconded, so the £2OOO is deducted there—
otherwise, that half-year’s profits would be £3221. The
poor old Store’s trouble is the same trouble which all the
great Melbourne institutions fell into during the boom.
Instead of renting a humble establishment, it built a vast
stone palace at a cost of £llO,OOO. Its capital is only
£112,600, and when the bricks and mortar swallowed this
practically all up it had to borrow capital for trading pur-
poses. So it has £64,404 overdraft at the Bank of Vic-
toria, and the heavy interest eats up the profits, and
things never get any further forward, and everybody is
soured and hopeless. And it is difficult to see what can
be done except peg away mournfully at reducing the
huge 0.d., and moor the groceries with a kedge anchor to
prevent any further elopements.

A colossal law-suit against a public institution
was very quietly squared last week; alleged pay-
ment in settlement, £35,000. How the lawyers must
have grieved—for, according to all accounts, it would
have taken a team of bullocks to convey the mere state-
ment of claim to the court-house.
* • • *
Gold-dredging, both by bucket-dredges and
pump-dredges, with complete methods attached for gold-
saving, is an industry that has come to stay in Australia.
Maoriland owes much of her present prosperity in the
South Island to recent developments in this branch of
mining. In Vic. excellent results are being obtained, and
investment in gold-dredging co’s is popular. N.S. Wales
possesses areas eminently suitable for successful gold-
dredging, and the famous Araluen Valley is considered
one of the most likely to return good results. What is
claimed to be the pick of the whole valley has lately been
taken up by the Gold-dredging Options Syndicate, with a
view to active work at an early date. From the portions
of this ground already worked, scores of thousands of
ounces have been taken out by diggers in the past, but
many thousands must yet remain in the sand and bed of
the creek, and extremely rich results are looked for.
Araluen Creek is now pegged out for miles, and little
ground above or below the old workings is available.
Those apparently most alive to the possibilities of dredg-
ing for gold both here and on Shoalhaven river are the
Maorilanders, of whom there are several parties.*

* # * •
Some 12 years back Bright District Association,
Vic., started a tunnel into the Oriental Hill, Wandiligong,
to look for a lode worked 900 ft. above for £780,000 in
gold. Tunnel now in 3000 ft., several hundred more to go,
and owners think the time has arrived for Govt, to lend a
hand. Mines Minister agreeable, but co. has nothing to
offer as security for money lent as required by the Act.
A little Bill to remove the embargo is suggested. The
law wisely prevents any co. registered outside Victoria
participating ; still one English co. had the gall to ask for
Victorian State assistance. It was advised to apply to
Premier Salisbury for aid from the British prospecting
vote.
* * * *
British B.H. improves ; a fine body of sulphides
has been exposed in a little-worked section at the Block
14 end. And the carbonate ores, for which there was
never before so good a market, hold out exceedingly well.
The British has had a phenomenal run of hard luck, but
it seems that some sort of a change is working up. The
co. has just made a big sale of concentrates. Block 10,
too,, looks better than usual at present. And the Central
(Sulphide Corporation) has made its first sale of “ zincs,”
25,000 tons of them, delivery to begin at once.
• * * *
B.H. Junction North, it is officially announced,
has mined—and sold—a small quantity of the North Co.’s
ore through mistaking the boundary—a thing not at all
hard to manage where the lode twists and turns as it does
here. The “small quantity” runs into two or three
thousand tons ; and as it is fairly good stuff the im-
poverished Junction North will have to grind a lot of the
poor stuff that alone is left to it in order to pay up. But
that is not the worst of it from shareholders’ point of
view; for if they have worked a greater length of lode
than they have a right to, it follows that their elaborate
calculations of ore available are all wrong, too. Desperate
economies are being made at the mine, one of which—the
letting of the trucking by contract —has caused a little
labor trouble—not a strike ; that word has been blacked
out of the dictionaries at Broken Hill. And as the posi-
tion is worse for the Junction North, it is, of course, just
so much better for the North than it was supposed. Cer-
tainly the latter low-grade proposition can stand con-
siderable improvement.

Mr. William Thow, chief mechanical engineer
of the Railway Department, has recently returned from
an extended trip through the United States and Europe.
It is understood that whilst there Mr. Thow, after making
most exhaustive inquiries as to the latest and most im-
proved pneumatic tools and means of operating same,
purchased for the Eveleigh workshops an Ingersoll-
Sergeant duplex air compressor, with compound air
cylinders and inter-cooler. The local agents for this
company are Parke and Lacy Company Ltd., Sydney.*
* * * *
Gobar notes : Occidental has finished enlarging
its tank, and is still awaiting rain to clear off its bank
overdraft. Cobar Gold-Mines showing a little more
energy. Nearly a year since the co. reconstructed, and
over eight months since the cyanide plant, taken over by
the Gold Recovery Co., was shut down. The old plant
with the Huntington mill adjoining it could have been
made to earn enough money to pay wages and salaries in
Australia all this while, but the management shut right
down and locked the place up. The Chesney people
declare that the new machinery is on the way at last.
The Great Western talks seriously of erecting a cyanide
plant. The idea would be good had it a fair heap of tail-
ings to work on and were the mine opened up so that the
mill, even small as it is, could work continuously and with
profit. As matters stand, there are only about 1500 tons
of tailings, and these so coppery that they cannot be
cyanided to much profit. Hardly any real mining has
been done. Mt. Allen cyanide works have had their
first clean-up and sent in first bar of bullion. Tailings-heap
said to be small.

• * • •
The Bulletin comes across an old balance-
sheet of the disgraceful Shellback mine—described as
being “for the period” (length of period not stated)
“ ending 30th June,” 1898. Its main features are that the
co. spent £2260 during the “period,” of which sum £617
was spent on the Shellback mine, £1250 went in “com-
mission, brokerage, &c.,” to the people who made money
out of the business in Sydney, and £393 was spent in
various ways in or about the Sydney office. The blessed
Shellback lasted about a year altogether, and now
another mine, which looks just like it—the Gulgong Gold-
mining Co.—is being built out of the ruins. A large
moral comes in just here. When a mine is started with
a very small capital, as the Shellback was, and as the
Gulgong Gold-mining Co. is, it is absurd to expect that
any real mining can be effected. There is no money to
do it with, and nobody who understands the business
expects that any real mining will be done. But the fact
that the share liability is very small induces a lot of
people to go in on the ground that they don’t risk much.
Enough money is collected among them to let the people
who mine in dingy offices in Sydney get their £1250, or
whatever it is, and then the show probably closes up for
want of any more cash to pay wages. So far as the
people who start such a co. are concerned, it matters
nothing or very little if it does close up—after they have
got their money. The next thing to do is to float a new
co. and make another little commission.
Apropos of a par. in last week’s Bulletin, re
the Gulgong Gold-Mining Co., which is being formed on
a hopelessly small capital to play at working a part of the
dead-and-gone Shellback Co.’s ground, and, incidentally,
to find salaries and commissions for a few people : this co.
is not identical with, or in any way connected with, the
Gulgong Gold Mines Syndicate (promoted by William
Thompson, of Gulgong), and any obloquy which fell on
the latter through the smellful memories of the Shellback
affair was quite undeserved.

The old - established Wallaroo and Moonta
Copper Co. (S. A.) made last year a profit of £28,554. Of
this, £8000 has been paid in divs., £5000 goes to the de-
benture sinking-fund, and the balance goes forward and
to the liquidation of heavy liabilities incurred to extend
the custom-plant. The directors will not wholly adopt
the general practice of charging the co. with depreciation
of plant only, and not with any of the prime cost; they
will use the extra money coming in from the higher value
of copper to wipe the liability for the plant right out.
Despite the higher price ruling for copper, the
Wallaroo and Moonta co. has had to curtail the pro-
duction from the Moonta. The mine has been worked
continuously since 1860, but the payable ore is now con-
fined to comparatively narrow limits ; last year the total
extraction was 14,600 tons ore, which gave 2491 tons
metal, as against 15,688 tons ore, yielding 2937 tons metal
in ’97. Wallaroo’s figures were 22,792 tons ore for 2615
tons copper in ’98, as against 17,102 tons ore for 2136 tons
copper in ’97. Moonta’s percentage is still the better, of
course ; but, then, Wallaroo was completely dwarfed only
a few years ago. At the two mines together the mining
cost was £35 18s. lid. per ton of metal, smelting cost £12
3s. 7d., and shipping and other charges *1 12s. 3d. The
co., by the way, has promised its miserably-paid hands a
“ rise ” in June ; but, seeing that it is getting the benefit
of the improved copper market all the time, the tardy
concession has caused more dissatisfaction than other-
wise.
* * * *
Mephan Ferguson, engineer, etc., has just com-
pleted at his engineering works, Footscray, Melb., one of
the largest planing machines every made in any part of
the world. It weighs about 100 tons, and is the machine
to be used in planing and thickening up the edges of
28ft. x Jin. plates required in manufacturing the patent
Lockingbar pipes for Coolgardie water supply contract. All
the steel bars for this contract will be supplied from the
Earl of Dudley works, Eng., but in order to get prompt
delivery the order for the steel plates has been divided
amongst four German firms. The first shipment of 500
tons will be dispatched by the Maori King, now loading.*
* * * *
The few who stick to their shows in the rich
country between Coolgardie and the Murchison continue
to beat out some big yields ; but camel-cartage takes all
the cream in one mouthful. The last monthly Lawlers-
Mt. Malcolm escort had 2095oz. under its revolvers. And
returns of from 7 to lOoz. per ton are still announced —
picked stone, of course; for none else could be handled
there.
• * * *
The erection of the sulphide plant at the Aus-
tralia Mine, Kalgoorlie, is nearing completion. The
crushing is to be done by means of four sets of 36” x 16”
Roger Rolls, having a combined capacity of 500 tons per
day, and the whole of the product will subsequently be
fed into four Ropp roasting furnaces. The rolls are slow
speed, and the first of the size manufactured in Aus-
tralasia. The furnaces are well-known, being in use at
Broken Hill and Mount Morgan.
» * * *
From Kalgoorlie Lake View making every
effort with the new sulphide-plant, and expects to be able
to considerably increase the gold-output by June. _ The
Boulder milling contract has been a complete failure.
Konemann did not recover a dwt. to the ton. The co.
has cancelled the contract, and is going to put in eight
large presses which will handle 300 tons per day, and
should increase the production by 3500oz. to 4000oz.
Ivanhoe The filter-presses, as now improved, working
on this mine are a great success, treating slimes and
covering all losses for 6s. a ton, and reducing their
value to ldwt. and ldwt. 6grs. per ton, giving a
high extraction and dealing with 120 tons a day in four
presses.” What a humiliation for all connected with the
running of the Great Boulder mine to have to adopt a
method the success of which was known many months
ago ! At one time it looked as if Konemann’s wonderful
process was going to take more gold than the tailings con-
tained.

“ Decidedly, Cue is busted,” writes a Bulletin
correspondent. “You can add to the previous list of
idle batteriesChampion and Champion Extended (at
Nanine), each of 20 head ; Nanine mine, 10 head ; also, at
Cue, Light of Asia, 20 head ; and others. The only
stampers to be heard at Nanine are those of a three-head
‘njickey.’ Between Abbott’s and Nanine, the Garden
Gully (10 head) is also idle ; and, from the look of the
building, etc., one might be excused for thinking it was
erected by the Phoenicians. Re Tuckanarra Govt, battery,
just opened : Heard a man who had been speechifying at
the opening ceremony, say, two days afterwards, that there
was not enough water by a lot, also that the stone avail-
able would not last three months, after which it would be
coming in at the rate of three to ten tons per week, and
that, in six months, the Govt, would be losing so much
of money on all its batteries that he would be able to
lease the lot cheap.”
Crown Lyell, Tas. : Another disturbed meeting
in Melb. Chairman rushed out of the chair. Meeting
was asked to keep itself cool till May, when a clean sweep
could be made of the Board. Request refused and shrieks
made for instant dismissal. Chairman emphatically :
“ I will not resign—there now !” Devil yet to pay.

SOCIETY
It is not often that an incautious citizen
finds his wrong way into the N.S.W. Leg. Coun-
cil chamber. When he does, he hurries with all
speed out of that abode of solemnity, grey hairs,
bald-heads, spectacles, disjointed remarks, totter-
ing intellect, gout, prejudice, confused ideas,
weird toryism, abdominal immensity, and frock-
coat. Once or twice in a decade, however, the
Assembly sends along a Bill that rouses the
sleepy Council to activity. No hurricane-struck
seaport was ever more agitated than the Council
when the Federal Enabling Bill was fired into it
by the people’s Assembly. An excited Council is
a fine pantomime. Nearly every member rises at
once to catch the President’s eye. They are
laboring under suppressed speech and awful in-
dignation, and hands chop the air like a dis-
ordered calisthenic class in motion. Then they
all sit down but one, and he mumbles a protest in
half-articulate speech ; and consults a slip of paper,
and drops his spectacles on it, forty times in ten
minutes, and each time he is half a minute adjust-
ing the spectacles and fifteen seconds finding the
place on the paper. A forty-minutes’ speech
means thirty-minutes’ grabbing after lost spec-
tacles and ten minutes’ half-articulate mumble and
buzz. This is the House which prides itself on
being a check upon hasty legislation and the
saviour of the country.
• * * *
The extent to which N.S.W. ancients dif-
fer over the site of Sydney’s first Govt. House
shows the fallibility of memory. These old men,
who all swear they were there, locate the house in
half-a-dozen different places at the same time.
What value tradition ?

A par about Governor Beauchamp in a
London weekly offers very little encouragement
to the Sydney girls who have laid in a stock of
blushes and. things for the destruction of his
future happiness. The paragraph states that his
lordship is quite too much in love with himself to
dream of getting married yet awhile. Evidence
as to Beauchamp’s utter respectability and prig-
gishness comes rolling in by every mail, and he
begins to look very like a part of a British
scheme for converting the sassiety heathen
of this distant land. Beauchamp, Brassey and
Tennyson in the places where Carington, Loch
and giddy Bill Wobinson picked their teeth of
yore.! I ederation threatens to come in with a
flourish of crumpets.

Another insult to the mother province :
London Critic, referring to the fact that Governor
Beauchamp will be accompanied to N.S.W. by

* * * *
The first woman-student to enter on the
study of law at Sydney University recently
passed her intermediate exam. Since the news of
her success has become known, several others of
the sex have announced their intention of enter-
ing the Law-school. What their ultimate inten-
tions are no one knows, since the Supreme Courb
regulations will not admit of their admission as
barristers, and an application to be admitted as
convey ancers has also been refused.
* * * *
The Sydney evening papers, The Bulle-
tin observes, are at last giving much-needed atten-
tion, day by day, to the stock-and-share market,
the aggressions of the morning press having forced
them to nose round for new fields. For many
years past you could read Sydney’s evening prints
any afternoon without being one whit the wiser
about, what had been done on the Exchange in the
morning. Also, the Sydney evening press is giv-
ing extra attention—if that were possible—to the
divorce-court ; during last week Mrs. So-and-so’s
alleged “ wet kiss ” was a perfect godsend to it.
* * * *
Divorce-court Justice Simpson : “I place
no reliance on paid witnesses. Other Judges have
said the same. Unless corroborated, I give their
evidence little attention.” This referred to paid
private detectives. Only a couple of weeks before
a witness who followed a couple and testified to
their misconduct, declared he had received no pay-
ment, and expected none. The Judge then re-
marked that the witness’s conduct was inconceiv-
able.
JBe the vagaries
of country hospi-
tal committees:—
The matron of our
local institution
having resigned
(writes a Bulle-
tin correspon-
dent) the commit-
tee met to appoint
her successor and
a proposition to
promote the nurse
to the position
was favorably en-
tertained ; a diffi-
culty in the way,
however, being:
that the nurse,
though certified
competent by the
local medical
officer, was not
fully qualified as
required by the
regulations. The
Govt, medical
officer was com-
municated with on
the subject, and
at the same time
applications for
the position were
called for in the
Sydney and Melb.
dailies. In reply
to the advt. 20
applications were
received, and
when the commit-
tee metto consider
them it was re-
solved (despite a
communication
from the G.M.O.
to effect that the
nurse couldn’t be
appointed) that
the applications
be not opened, and that the appointment he con-
ferred upon the nurse. Next, a telegram was
sent the G.M.O. notifying him of the appointment
and asking for his approval. The answer to this
was, of course, another refusal, and now this not-
to-be-beaten committee has adjourned for a week
to take breath and have another go at the
situation.—F. H. T.

Astonishingly weak have been John Nor-
ton’s open letters to the .Sydney “ Pecksniffian
Press,” considering the many hard things that
might be said on the subject. Much of the little
man’s criticism of S.M. Herald snapped at Gul-
lett, because that good journalist had sense enough
to hang on to his D.T. shares (which, so far as can
be made out, are now really returning about 25
per cent. divs). And Norton’s article on the
D.T. was very like a Pecksniffian puff, at five
guineas a column.

Chalmers—even if one of the crew had to be
whiskied and kidnapped. Borchgrevink, of

In British law a jury, in a criminal case,
is not supposed to know of an accused’s previous
convictions, assuming hfm to have any. In Vic-
toria, however, this
wholesome principle
is subverted by an
ingenious little
dodge on the part
o f the Crown
Law authorities.
Several times
lawyers have
drawn attention
t o this fact,
but without
result. The
dodge con-
sists of pin-
ning the cer-
tificates of a
man’s pre-
vious convic-
tions on to
thepresent-
m e n t -
sheet.
Thus any
juror who
has “ been
there before”
is able, at a
glance, when the
associate stands up
to read the present-
ment to the jury,
to see whether
. there is anything in
addition to the one sheet contain-
ing the presentment. If there are
more sheets than one, and of a dif-
ferent color, the “ fly ” jurors know
just as well as if the associate had
told them that there are previous
convictions against the man they are
about to try.

Certain landlords of Cameron-street, Pad-
dington, Sydney, where a shooting tragedy
lately took place, have had a petition
drawn up, praying that the
name of the street may be
altered, in consequence of
“the detrimental effect that
the recent tragedy was hav-
ing upon properties situated
therein.” They also plain-
tively proclaim that “it is dim-
cult, and at times utterly impos-
sible, to induce tenants to inspect
vacant properties in the ill-fated
street.” Which recalls the cele-
brated deputation to Lord
Palmerston from Rugeley, the
scene of the well-advertised
Palmer poisonings, asking
that the name of tbe village
might be changed. “ Yes,’
replied “Pam.” “call it>
Palmerston.”

A milk - vendor lately fined
in Brisbane for adulterating milk with _
40 per cent, of water, pled in justification
that his patrons had “ rounded on him
for selling them pure milk, for milk that
hasn’t been watered won’t keep. I his is
not so foolish as it looks, for it is an un-
doubted fact that a mixture containing 99
per cent, of milk will turn sour much more
rapidly than a mixture containing 99 per
cent, of water.

A M.L. self-stayer left this behind him, in
a letter to his parents :
The jury may find I committed suicide whilst in low
spirits through being out of work, which is the real cause
as I am as sane as any man.
The jury verdicted as requested. There you have
clearly illustrated the difference between legal
insanity and medical insanity. The man delibe-
rately killed himself, but the impulse was irre-
sistible, all the same.

to your fiancee riding in bloomers?”
HE: “ Well, I think she has a perfect right.”
SHE: “ And, pray, what is wrong with her left?”
The recent Sydney libel-action (in which
an ex-M.L.A. of N.S.W. obtained =£2so damages
against the Australian Star) suggests to The
Bulletin that in some respects sweet are the uses
of the libel. Though, doubtless, nobody who
knows plaintiff would ever have suspected rum of
getting vulgarly drunk and having unlawful silver
spoons in his pocket, it is now, by his. own sworn
testimony, and by defendant s admissions, pro-
claimed abroad unto all men that he has never
thus fallen awav from grace. Blessed are the
traduced, for not only shall their pockets be filled
but their horn shall be exalted.

“Hermes”: There is a fine, promising
crop of fiends growing up round Miller’s Point,
Sydney. Writer heard a bloodthirsty young
wretch of about 14 summers telling an admiring
push how he and another small devil like unto
himself tried to “drownd a dorg.” After about
an hour’s trying they got tired and hauled
the poor animal out and attempted to cut
its throat with a broken
bottle, and that failing
also they finally choked it
with a string. And yet we
send missionaries to China.

What men think when they give
their seats up to ladies.. A crowded
tram : Lady enters, man rises and gives

out. Man, standing, forgets himself,

people no homes ? ”

A M.L. doctor certified that a
man found dead in his garden had died
of apoplexy, so the jury, after examin-
ing the body, brought in a verdict
accordingly. After it was all over some
meddling busybody lifted up the
corpse’s long beard and found the throat
cut from ear to ear.

In recently sentencing a man
convicted of obtaining free drinks and
cash for crooked cheques with the aid of
a persuasive tongue, N.S.W. Judge
Murray said to prisoner, “You are a
humbug—not a criminal ; and I hope
that the eloquence that has got you
free drinks and solid cash will not gain
for you a seat in the lederal Parlia-
ment.” And then he gave the “ non-
criminal ” nine months !

“Micky Moubra”: How about
“ Yowi ” as a name for the Federal
capital ? It’s short, and is aboriginese
for “yes.” Meaning “yes,” it would
remind provincialists of the great ma-
jority who said “ Yes ’ on first referen-
dum-day ; and it may also remind
those very tired Cornstalksthat N.S.
Wales’s majority of “Yes’s’’was 5000
odd on that day, and thereby counter-
act the baleful influence of the same
old lie of Sydney D.T. which tries to
convince them N.S.W. gave a negative
majority vote.
The second Gatton murder inquiry has at
least forced the Law to recognise one thing—that
the local inhabitants in general don t take the
investigation kindly. The attitude of the ad-
jacent population is that it wishes the whole affair
dropped—that it resents inquiry as a personal
injury, and that it wants rather to block investi-
gation than to help it. In fact, it wears the sulky
aspect of a community which knows the criminal
and is tacitly leagued together to shield hitn;
either because it approves of his action for reasons
of its own, or because his discovery would lead
also to the discovery of the state of things which
led up to the crime, and the community, again for
reasons of its own, doesn’t want that matter
stirred up with the long pole of official inquisitive-
ness. The apathy of the Murphy family, its utter
lack of interest in the search for the murderer, the
difficulty of dragging evidence out of witnesses,
the fact that the evidence dragged out at the
second inquiry seems to show that some important
facts were suppressed at the original inquest, the
promptitude and enthusiasm with which the local
crowd trod out all the tracks which might have
revealed the criminal, and various other things,
including the strong curse language thrown at the
investigating magistrate, have convinced that
functionary that the district regards anyone who
shakes up its tragedy as an enemy.
So long as there seemed a chance of hanging an
outsider for the crime, and thereby shifting the
responsibility off the district, said district rolled
up manfully and identified Burgess (who, it now
appears, wasn’t there at all) with beautiful
unanimity. But when the prospect of hanging
the wrong man began to fade away the Murphy
country grew resentful and grumpy, lhe Bris-
bane officials felt themselves in much the same
situation as the British officials do in India, lhe
trouble there is that the white man, living right
alongside the brown man all_ his life, can never
find out what the brown man is thinking of, and
the brown man never tells him. And the city
officials look silently at the clannish turnip-
growers around Gatton, who are mostly brothers
and cousins and half-cousins, and wonder to no
purpose what they are thinking of. I hey
have a vague suspicion that these turnip-
growers know, by a sort of freemasonry, who
did the crime, and what led up to it; and that in
their own silent agricultural way they agree among
themselves, without saying anything, either that
the crime was justifiable, or that it was quite
natural considering the circumstances—whatever
they might be ; or else that the circumstances
were such that it is the less evil of the two to say
nothing about it. And it looks as if the most
promising thing the law could do would be to buy
a farm near Gatton, and let a detective grow tur-
nips there for 20 years till he begins to understand
the local ways of thinking, and then let that tec
report to the department.
It was characteristic of the Gatton tragedy
scandal that magistrate Shand thought himself
called upon to offer a demi-semi-apology for having
hurt anybody’s feelings. “ I have actually been
accused personally of crushing the Murphy family
because the members were called as witnesses.
Such a charge is beyond my comprehension, and
reflects no credit on my accusers, said Shand.
And this after hearing the evidence of an outside
Murphy to the effect that a member of the Murphy
family had taken it for granted that the murder
was a purely family affair ! How many influences
have been at work to prevent the arrest of several
of the clan on suspicion ?

patches if the candle of nationality be lighted before
SOME MISAPPREHENSION.
KING B ILLY: “Mine he know your son Mick this long time,

it. The tragedy was a Boxing Jiight one. The land
the Murphys came from provides such tragedies in
plenty at such a time. The three persons who had
stuck together during the night were those
slaughtered. One life may have fallen by acci-
dent in fight, and the others of the party were
killed to buy silence. Analogues to this view are
numerous. This view rejects the outraging busi-
ness altogether. When the fight was over, the
guilty parties placed the bodies in the cart on a
blanket, drove to the paddock, laid them out,
making pretence that the girls were outraged,
shot the horse, and burnt the blanket. The doc-
tor’s evidence of outrage is vitiated by his evidence
on other points. Put outrage aside, and the whole
case is typically Irish. The bobby losing his
notes, the apathy or reticence of the Murphy
family, the hunt for “strangers” and clinging
with death grip to scapegoat Burgess are all re-
lated incidents. Begin by saying the girls were
not outraged, and most of the mystery is dis-
solved.

From the North The black-trackers
have gone home from Gatton disgusted. They
are quite sure who did it. Free translation, of
their remarks “We have been 10 weeks chasing
nobody and hunting for nothing while the man
who did the murder was walking about laughing
at us.”

“Zoo”: The best “theory” to date
might be commended to the notice of the much-
befogged Q. police. In a tram-smoker, t’other
evening, a scattered-looking man, who owned to
having been rabbiting for the last six months
where “ they didn’t see no noospapers,” was re-
ceiving a very muddled account of the. Gatton
mystery from a friend. When the two girls were
mentioned his weather-wrinkled face took on a
fine expression of scorn. “Then,” said he, “I
don’t believe there was no murder, nor no hout-
rage, nor nothin’ at all. Yer never can believe
anythin where girls is mixed up, they’re sich
blanky liars ! G’arn, I know ! They’ll pretend
almost anythin’! I guess they’re only shamming
dead.”

Coolgardie Exhibition “ opened with
great iclat” —-promises to be the biggest frost
in the whole history of Joubert’s raree shows. So
small a fraction of the alleged exhibits have
arrived that the interior of the building was, at
latest mail-dates, a scene of desolation. The
Forrest Govt, was persuaded into giving .£lO,OOO,
much of which has gone to provide the venerable
showman and a host of officials with billets and
travelling exes. The few exhibitors who have
sent goods to the city of truth and thirst are
raising such a howl about their treatment that
Forrest has promised to appoint a commission to
look into things. Profound apathy is exhibited
respecting the show, as may be guessed by the fact
that the public subscriptions of Geraldton (the
second port of Westralia) to secure a <£2s bay for
the district, only amounted to .£4 10s. 4d. ! It
was amusing to read of the elaborate preparations
made for the opening ceremony—vice-regal pro-
cession, bands, fire brigades, old pioneers, Odd-
fellows, school-children, blackfellows, Afghans;
cantata, fanfare of trumpets and fireworks. Ac-
cording to Joubert, Paris Exhibition was simply
“ not to be in it.”
Half the Coolgardie hotelkeepers hope to make
enough out of the exhibition to clear out, and so
dodge the official-receiver, who is at present even
a bigger man in W.A. than Premier Forrest.
Shilling drinks and ss. meals, with shakedowns
ditto, are among the attractions held out to
visitors.
Now, you of the loyal Austral rout,
Drop a casual tear, if you can—
These queenly ways that we read about
Make better the heart of a man
(And we thank the chap who cabled ’em out
Sheer under the ocean’s span).
She met the corpse on a narrow path,
With its mournful following—
She might have loosened her regal wrath,
But the Queen did no such thing ;
Such wondrous patience Victoria hath
We cannot help marvelling.
"Twas only the corpse of a peasant thrall,
And his grieving womankind ;
But the Queen, she honored the humble pall
With a sympathy refined
(She couldn’t drive over the funeral
So her Majesty lagged behind).

The prophecy in this column that there would
be a rush of female students to the University at
the next academic year has been verified by the
last matriculation results. Numbers of the aspir-
ing bluestockings are of tender years, and have
been much abashed at the decree of those in
authority that their flowing locks or pigtails must
be securely braided to their heads. They are even
compelled to go home with their hair done up.
Rather strange that Prof. MacCallum’s Sydney
University Prize for English in the first year
should have been won by a Frenchman—who was
an evening student at that. Also significant that
the gold medai for Philosophy at the M. A. exam,
should have been carried off by the most earnest
and sincere barracker-for-Eederation at the Uni-
versity.

In a certain Australian province a pretty little
divorcee ha,s lately been taken on as cook by the
J udge who tried her case. A kind friend told the
ex-husband how beautifully the new cook was
turning out the Judge’s dinners. ‘‘ She gives him
the most wonderfuUy-cooked vegetables,” con-
ciuded the friend. The ex-husband smiled a
satisfied smile. “She’ll soon give him onlv
beans,” he said. J
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
~ 1 11 private life, General Booth seems to out-
siders as pompous and puffed-up a martinet as the
most stiff-necked old-style 4 officer and gentleman’
on Her Majesty s half-pay list. Said latter-day
apostle, tis said, takes much thought for what
he eats, issuing his culinary commands, even in
private houses, brusquely enough for Napoleon
ordering his new nobles to divorce their canaille
spouses, preparatory to marrying princesses. The
Salvation Army commander-in-chief is waited
upon hand-and-foot by a devoted aide, who tends
him cleverly as a valet. Likely as not this attache
is a trained valet, though he fights the devil be-
tween times in the uniform of a ‘colonel,’ or sich.
lor that matter, General Booth’s Australian
grand-children are washed and combed by a she-
adjutant. Under her warlike guidance, their
daily walks abroad are thrilling as blood-and-fire
marches, while the very kitten sports its hallelujah
bonnet, praise the Lord !

pocket-beauty, by birth a Bananalander the
motherless only daughter of that old Central
Queensland identity, the late Sydney Sharwood.
Mrs. kairbairn married at seventeen. She has
two small sons, who, like all the ‘handsome Fair-
bairns; will probably be athletes in their day.

Steve Jb airbams will soon be in residence again at
their Wernbee home.

“Mrs. Brough’s new costumes for Paula Tan-
queray make that woman a glorious being. In
the first act, a silk frock, golden as the rising sun
m colour, and fitting as though the actress had
been jioured into it. How many figures are there
which could stand the revealing of splendid lines
and curves that such a dress makes ? It was made
plain, low ill the neck, and drew a sweeping train
alter it. Ihe gomg-out dress was a violet cloth
wffh over-skirt of same color and stuff prettily
braided. Paula palpitated her last before pour-
ing poison into her lovely self, in a dress that was
even lower in the neck, though not more figure-
reveahng than that in the first act. Indeed it
seemed that Mrs. Brough had chosen to do the
gasping business in a dress so built, in order to
exhibit the powerful breast action of the emotional
scenes.

“The shooting-party’s luncheon in ‘The White
-tleather is not such a scene of devouring interest
as the matinee feed aforesaid, although it has its
realistic moments. None of the adults seem
hungry in comparison with the youngster (little
Ida ingersoll), who swoops down upon the sausage-
roils after her elders have gone away to shoot.

second act, where she is served with scones and
milk, or something of that sort, at a table in
Battersea Park. Miss Watson, as the unhappy
mother of the hungry one, can hardly keep a sad
face whilst her alleged offspring is sowing the
seeds of indigestion.

PEOPLE YOU KNOW.
No. 2.—The Postman.
A rural postman writes : “ I desire to add this
unsolicited testimony to the sterling qualities of
Dr. Tibbies’ Vi-Cocoa. I am a rural postman,
and exposed to all kinds of weather. I have used
Dr. Tibbies’ Vi-Cocoa daily this last eleven
months, and I believe that I can do my work with
less fatigue since I have used it. I can honestly
recommend to my fellow postmen Dr. Tibbies’
Vi-Cocoa for its sustaining qualities.”
Of late years many new manufactures, rejoicing
in a multiplicity of names, have been brought
before the public notice, but there are none which
have bounded into favor so readily as has Dr.
Tibbies’ Vi-Cocoa. Throughout the country its
sales have increased with great rapidity, and an
article which only a few years back was unheard
of has now become one of the most popular
beverages of the people.
Merit, and merit alone, is what is claimed for
Dr. Tibbies’ Vi-Cocoa, and the Proprietors are
prepared to send to any reader who names The
Bulletin (a post-card will do) a dainty sample tin
of Dr. Tibbies’ Vi-Cocoa free and post paid.
There is no magic in all this. It is a plain,
honest, straightforward offer. It is done to intro-
duce the merits of Vi-Cocoa into every home
Dr. Tibbies' Vi-Cocoa, as a concentrated form of
nourishment and vitality, is invaluable : nay,
more than this, for to ail who wish to face the
strife and battle of life with greater endurance and
more sustained exertion, it is absolutely indispens-
able.
Dr. Tibbies’ Vi-Cocoa can be obtained from all
chemists, grocers, and stores, or from Dr. Tibbies’
Vi-Cocoa, Limited, 269 G-eorge-street, Sydney.*

Among the varied phenomena of Australia, the
“ Zetz” natural mineral spring at Bullimore, near Dubbo,
N.S.W., takes no unimportant a place. Right from the
heart of Nature’s laboratory this spring pours out daily
an illimitable supply of sparkling mineral water, now-
sold everywhere as Zetz-Spa water. The enormous in-
crease in the demand testifies in a striking manner to the
popularity and good effects of Zetz-Spa water, which is
rightly called “ The Premier Natural Mineral Table
Water.” The best imported waters cannot equal it.*

W. M'Millan is the only N.S.W. poli-
tician of front rank who invariably says grace
before and after all meals.
“Who will get the N.S.W. Society of
Artists’ travelling scholarship?” Well, The
Bulletin could “name it in one.”
Madame Melba has sustained a severe
advertisement by the falling of a Yankee hotel-
parlor statuette, which struck her and rendered
her insensible for some minutes.
Randwick Storey, M.L.A. (a wholesale
hatter —they call him upper-storey), always cir-
culates a Hansard report of his speeches among
his constituents. They deserve it. They twice
returned him.
Primate Smith has been appointed Aus-
tralian representative on the consultative com-
mittee for the Anglican Church throughout the
world. This is taken as an honor even higher
than the Primacy.

week s long walking and hill-climbing, performed
daily from his Adelaide residence.

One M.L. old-age pensioner swore that he
was years old next birthday. The old man
evidently believed it too. He said he had always
been an odd half-year of age on his birthday, and
didn t see why any one should object.
Admiral Pearson’s brusqueness in Maori-
land, and his stand-offishness in Melbourne have
>6en much discussed in the English Service”
papers. It is said that amongst naval people
iearson is known as “Hugh (to) L. Pearson.”
G. C. Hawker, for over 30 years in S.A.
politics, and for whom, despite his wealth and his
pastoralist associations, the Laborists always had
a most kindly feeling, will probably be put into a
book shortly. His widow is in London, with
much of the thorough old gentleman’s political and
pioneering MS.
Old Soldier, the South African corre-
spondent of Sydney E. News, whose letters from
Capetown show such an edge on Oom Paul, took
an active part in the massacre at Rorke’s Drift,
and was with the party under Chelmsford when
the Zulus speared the Prince Imperial. A couple
of years ago “ Old Soldier,” like another able
penman, painted verandah-posts and furniture-
vans at North Sydney.
Astronomer H. 0. Russell celebrated his
63rd birthday March 17. It is 40 years since he
received his first appointment in Sydney Observa-
tory. The only sensational incident in his long
official career was the attempt made to blow him
up with an infernal machine in 1877-
Died at Adelaide, Mrs. Thomas Caterer,
wife of one of S.A.’s best-known pedagogues and
mother of another of them — a lady who, nearly
half-a-century ago, was private secretary to
Elihu Burritt, the “learned blacksmith ’who so
forcefully advocated penny postage.
Edward Trickett, the once-famous Aus-
tralian sculler, later publican in N.S. VV. and
Queensland, and later still Salvation Army
apostle and Customs handy-man at Albury, has
been transferred to an official position in Sydney
where pay will be better and work more cheering.
Perhaps the smartest new man sent to Q.
Assembly is “ Joe ” Lesina, who downed ex-Labor
member Cross at Clermont. Lesina is a rapid,
vitriolic, and coherent speaker, a slashing, devil-
may-care writer, and just the sort of juvenile to
shake up and horrify the long, dismal, dry-as-dust
Premier.
Mr. P. R. Larkin, J.P., who has
joined the directorate of Sydney Con-
secrated Press, has been interviewed
by that paper on his recent visit to the
M.L. hot-spring region, where, he
learnedly says, he was much “struck
by the extensive pumice plains, inter-
sected in various directions by high
ranges of igneous formation, relieved
here and there by enormous trachytic
cones.” “ All the springs,” he is made
to say, “ have not been analysed yet,
but there are saline, alkaline, alkaline-
silicious, hepatic, sulphurous, acidic,
besides saline waters containing iodine,
cold acidulous chalybeates, and saline
acidulous chalybeates.” “Hepatic”
springs is probably a misprint for
“Hepatrick.” After remarking that
you can bathe or enjoy the delightful
views “ad arbitrium,” he proceeds to
descant on the action of the baths, the
salts of which, he points out, are not
absorbed into the system. “ The body,”
remarks Patrick, “cannot absorb any
salts from an aqueous solution. If the
water, or any portion of it, is allowed
to dry on the skin, a minute quantity
of the constituent solids of course re-
mains, which, through the friction of
the clothing, become incorporated with
the fatty elements of the integument,
and is eventually absorbed.” ....
“We should never,” says Mr. Larkin,
“lose sight of the fact that the skin is
the most important emunctory of the
body.” Paddy Larkin, J.P.. geolo-
gist, chemist, physiologist. The world
knows too little of its greatest men.
A house once inhabited by Adam
Lindsay Gordon, with the garden which
surrounded it, is opposite Glenelg
(S.A.) State-school, and, as the pro-
perty has been long unoccupied, is
rapidly falling to bits. Gordon was in
S.A. Assembly from March, '65, to
Nov., ’66, and that was the period
during which he lived in the cottage,
referred to. He only made one ambi-
tious speech in Parliament, although
he spoke on two other occasions. He

The two N.S.W. Leg. Council Heydons—
barrister C.G. and solicitor L.F.—who wildly tear
their hair at mention of the Australian Common-
wealth Bill, are Sydney natives primed to the
chins with King-street legends. To them the hub
of the universe is King-street, with a slight detour
towards St. Mary’s Cathedral and Riverview Col-
lege. C.G. was at one time in the service of a
bank.
Those Federal conventions will yet be the
death of someone. Since his return to Perth,
John Forrest has been laid up with strained
jugular, or something of that sort. He emerged
from strict seclusion the other day, looking much
“off color.” Judging from his puffy face and
figure, a spell of surveying and exploring, with a
diet of tea, damper and mutton, would do Big
John a world of good.
“Melb.”: What became of Mount and
Morris ? I saw them sentenced to 15 years, for
oceanio blackbirding, with Dr. Murray, in the
slaving brig Carl. Not long afterwards I saw
them absolutely released, on a technicality. Their
faces made me think of the man who had been in
hell and got out again.
Capt. Oats, M.L.A. for Yilgarn (W.A.), is
on a health tour to Maoriland. He was the first
man to make low-grade Westralian ore pay, and
claims to be first man who ever gave a sovereign
to a “sandgroper”—whereat the latter fainted.
Capt. Oats is father of Mrs. Finnerty, wife of the
well-known Coolgardie warden.
Superintendent Read and Inspector-
General Fosbery are both entitled to retire on
pensions, but cannot do so because the N.3.W.
police-pension fund is insolvent. Thus the two
highest offices in the force are sealed against men
who have earned promotion but must stay where
they are through no fault of their own.
Kipling has relatives in Australia. A
handful of cousins of the Jingoistic poet live at
Royal Park, Melbourne. One of these, “ Bill ”
Kipling, footballed with St. Kilda club when that
erratic institution first started as a senior organisa-
tion in ’B6, but he lacked the ginger and go of his
kinsman’s verse, and was never a hero to the small
boy of the period.
Pastor Fitchett is off to Europe to inspect
battle-fields. He has received his money for his
“ Bleeds ’’—said to be =8750. The rev. blood-
and-ink splasher’s style has been long missed
from the Argus leading columns. Editor Wil-
loughby does not admire his stuff. In fact, Wil-
loughby has, it is said, never been known to really
admire anybody’s stuff but his own.
Old Dr. Neild, of Melb., dramatic critic
and post-njortemist, never forgets his Shakespeare.
When the body of Mabel Ambrose was being
autopsied at Melb. Morgue, the pressmen and
detectives bad to beat a retreat, and subsequently
one of the former commented to the old man on
bis fortitude. ‘-Use! Use!” said the patholo-
gist, as he washed his hands, “ custom hath made
it in me a property of easiness.”
Some Nemesis. The two men who cut
and carted the timber for Mudgee (N.S.W.)
gallows, were both hanged on their own handi-
work ! One for the murder of Mrs. Martin, at
Guntawang pub., the other for killing his mate in
the same locality. Dick, who was Governor of
Mudgee Gaol when they were hanged, occupied
the condemned cell later on for having, in a fit of
jealousy, shot dead one of his subordinates. He
was reprieved.
W. G. Higgs, who within a month has
found his way into both Brisbane City Council
and Q. Legislature, has made rapid strides into
popularity in Mongrelia. A year ago Higgs and
his “ Socialism in our time ” motto were uni-
versally execrated ; now he represents property-
owners in the City Council and all sorts in
Parliament. Those in the know attribute much
of this sudden success to the fact that Higgs
recently found faith, and blossomed into a solemn
and energetic deacon of the kirk —the kirk, by the
way, being the one with the largest congregation
in his electorate. Anyhow, Higgs is well-meaning,
well-versed in economic subjects, and will quite
hold his own in Leperland Assembly.
Dear Bulletin.—Had occasion to make a
tour through Melbourne cemetery to observe the
relative time-resisting qualities of several building-
stones with which the old bone-yard is bestrewn.
Came across two recumbent sandstone slabs cover-
OfOot The inscriptions,
dated 1856, show that two commissioned officers,
each aged 23, attached to one of the British regi-
ments stationed here, died within 24 hours of each
other. They had met and fought with ordinary
service swords, and their brother officers shouted
the double funeral and paid for the grave fittings.
Wonder who the woman was ?—Architect.

Oliver Bainbridge, long - distance pedestrian, 8.A.,
brother to an earl, author, poet, lecturer, and several
other things, is apparently having a good time in Tas-
mania. Being a “ 8.A.,” he naturally takes the schools
under his special protection, and condescendingly gives
good ad vice to the rising generation. He appears specially
susceptible to “dear hearts,” and has expressed admira-
tion for the samples he has met with in Tasmania. The
funniest thing about Bainbridge, not even overlooking
his flowing locks and Buffalo Bill make-up, is his relation
of certain incidents in his lecture to slow music. “Tor-
tured ”—to slow music—is worth going a long
The Bulletin really believed that the most wild
and remote spot that “ Oliver Bainbridge ” was
ever tortured in was Brisbane. Wasn’t that hairy
youth born of perfectly ordinary and harmless
parents near Maitland, N.S.W. ? Anyhow, on sth
November, 1898, when he got his portrait inserted
in The Bulletin, he was a plain Australian
native, whatever he has become since.

Yic. Agricultural Minister Taverner, who
has just left for England, has little time for any re-
creation but the silliest of practical joking, but J. S.
Gair (who wiped out Harper for Bourke East at
last elections) beat him badly a few weeks ago.
Gair solemnly interviewed Turner, and stated
that Taverner had arranged that he (Gair) should
join the Ministry during the Turnip Department
Administrator’s absence in England. Then fol-
lowed three days of charge, rebuttal and Cabinet
troubles which only ceased on Gair’s explanation.
But since then Taverner jokes less indiscrimin-
ately.
