Murderer Briggs, to be hanged next Wednes-
day at Sydney, when employed at a city ware-
house used to sit down at luncheon-time with
other men and boys in the place, and tell them all
how dearly he loved the girl whom he eventually
slew. Also, this is the letter which he wrote about
the murder (though he had been for four years at
an excellent private school and four years at
Newington College):—
“Carlton Crescent, Summer Hill,
“ December 13, iB9B.
“ This is to state that the girl that has come to grief by
me is one who professed to love me, but is sincerely false.
One time she did act as if true love did exist between us,
but now the tables are turned, and all along she said she
thought I was not in earnest in regards to marriage. And
I must state that I would have married the one I loved,
and that is she. But finding she is acting falsely, I did
the deed, through her own misloving ways, which always
made me think that her love, of which very little she
showed towards me, was false, and only served to act, as
if marriage between her and me would act as a mainstay,
in other words, having me on a string. And no doubt
this will come within hearing of the public, and be of a
very substantial lesson to those who are supposed to be
had or taken in. The nowadays are to be watched,
and I can assure you that this one that I am keeping
company with has pretended to be loving and true, but I
have found out that she is not so. Her sister Minnie was
heard one night to say by me, while the one I love and
who has taken me in said that ‘ if I had a wooden man,
would you not have him on a string?’ But for ail that
my strong and true love to Maggie showed that I
loved her. She could not say otherwise. That
has preyed on my mind, and other words, can say
she thought of £3 a week, which always made me thmk
that true love could not exist on her part as on mine. I
have.thought differently, and this responds to whether
she kept my company for the £3 or true love. My idea is
the £3, and it is very hard for a man to keep company
with a girl for over a year. Just think ! She has often,
in fact, time after time, shown her false love towards a
man who has never thought or breathed of anyone else,
and another thing, cared, and always did have only love
for her, which makes me commit this outrageous deed.
It is to be hoped that her sister will not fall into a true
lover’s hands. I think the public can see why this sad
occurrence has been committed, and it may be men-
tioned that it is one of being true and loving on a man’s
part, and not on a woman’s. So may God have mercy on
each of our souls, Amen.—Yours faithfully (signed)
Stuart Wilson Christopher Briggs. I must also state that
my two store situations have been lost through one who
I have affectionately loved.”

Aboriginalities.
“ Nell Cook ”: Within the last three
months the emus of the N.S.W. side have taken
it into their heads to migrate northwards, but are
baulked by the rabbit-proof fence between N.S.W.
and Q. They have come from out-back in droves,
arid keep patrolling along the fence looking for an
opening. There was feed out-back, so hunger is
not accountable for this conduct. So weak have
they become (they do not seem to think of eating)
that they oan easily be caught by a man on foot.
When the gate of the fence is opened they make a
frantic rush, seeming to have lost all their natural
fear of man in their intense desire to get through.
The blacks say they have never known such a
thing to happen before.

“ W. A. Skinner ” to The Bulletin from
Ulverstone, Tas.
Mr. M‘Gooey (8., 11/3/’99) says, re albinism in Austra-
lia: —“I have heard of albino snakes, but can anyone
produce them ? ” Mr. Turnbull, of Ulverstone, yesterday
caught a snake about 6in. long with perfectly white back,
pink eyes and belly. It is now (preserved in spirits) in
the possession of Dr. M'Oal], of Ulverstone (Tas.). We
examined the mouth microscopically, but could see no
fangs. Mr. Turnbull’s daughters say they have often
seen a 2ft. white snake near where the little one was cap-
tured. Should this larger one be caught, I will let you
know.
(The Bulletin is now waiting to hear from some-
one who has got an albino tank or gum-tree.)

Trooper Dash, a champion thief-catcher of
out-back cattle-lifting days, once came upon two
notorious cattle-stealers driving a small mob of
unbranded calves off a Murrumbidgee run. He
secured only one of them at the time, the other
escaping into the scrub. Being nearly 20 miles
than/he fe f lln & c ® afi( J® nfc
that !f he lost no time he could capture the other
thief, Dash rushed his prisoner a couple of miles
to a wayside blacksmith’s shop,, where, finding
nothing in the shape of rope about the premises,
he shoved the cattle-stealer’s handsinto the black-
smith’s vyce, and screwed it up so as to hold the
uyin without crushing the hands. Then he went
after and captured thief No. 2 inside two hours,
Dash is no,w a senior-sergeant.

“Q.” : Am never surprised to read of
people being poisoned by tinned fish, &c., bought
at tin-pot shops in bush towns. Was once on
tramp at Dalby, and went into small shop en route
to get bread. Woman asked if I’d “ take a tin of
salmon—it was only Is. 9d.” Didn’t care for it.
Then she remarked, with a sigh, that “ There was
no sale for fish, and her own lot didn’t use it.
You see them tins,” said she, pointing to two in
the window with the sun on ’em, “them two
are the last of a dozen I got when I started busi-
ness 18 years ago. They’ve stood there since ! ”
The Anglican and R.C. churches were
built upon adjoining allotments, and a substantial
paling fence divided them. “Old Bill,” the C.E.
the shade, when most wanted, fell into the other
yard, and the faithful used to hitch their nags to
the fence and take advantage of it. “ Bill ” used
to be very wild, and one day writer came upon
the old fellow chopping the trees out, and asked
“what was up.” William explained I didn’t
grow these ere trees for the benefit of a lot of
Roman Catholic horses. Not me, mister. I come
f rom the wrong part for that! ”

For the instruction of “6 x 8,” who appears still
rather young, “ the cobbler ” is old-English slang
f° r “ the last,” and, in shearing parlance, evidently
refers to the last sheep in the pen. And, if not,
what does it refer to? Before “6xB” was no
s j ze at aP > and the British squire began to bring
live mutton out to Australia, the latter’s shed was
nofc muck larger than a henroost. About five
shearers used to draw from one pen, “6xB’s”
dimensions, and the most frantic rush of the day
ensued at knock-off time. The real “cobbler”
was ke Pt i n the pen, perhaps all day, as the
yarder-up used him as a decoy-sheep. Conse-
ffuently, theman to get through the last of the
five sheep (bar one) first, got the “ cobbler.” Any
shearer will coincide. However good, bad, or

“C.C.M.”: Be workers and wasters. It
is often the “good” bush man who brings down
the price of work in these parts, anyhow. Know
a gang of six “ good ” men who will take a con-
tract to fall 100 or 200 acres of bush and make All
or .£4 a week each where others would starve.
Reason : 14 hours per day and no step till the
job's finished. They once had a contract to fall
130 acres bush, at 30s. per acre. Started in be-
ginning of Nov., got out by Xmas. They never
saw their camp in daylight from start to finish,
Sundays included. Away before daylight and
not home until after dark ; whilst one cooked the
others ground their axes by slush-light. Boozers
or even moderate drinkers couldn’t have done
that.
* * * *
“ W.S.” : Have had ocular proof of kan-
garoo’s ability to swim. In spring of ’9B we had
luxuriant herbage, and flies were so bad that fully
25 per cent, of the ’roos were attacked by ophthal-
mia. I ran one, almost blind, into a large tank
18ft. deep, and he struck out in good style,
circling round and round for fully half-an-hour
before he landed, and he was not exhausted then.
He looked not unlike a yearling calf swimming,
and could travel quite as fast.
* * * *
“A.G.”: lie kangaroos swimming. Throw
any old hen and a vigorous ’roo into a deep water-
hole, and the hen will be paddling long after the
’roo has gone under. There is too much of him
behind to be any good at the game. When he gets
into deep water he assumes a vertical position,
same as an empty bottle, and can’t advance worth
speaking of. The ’roo should be able to “tread
water,” but can’t. I know.

“6x8”: Seems to me that for the mere
asking you can procure, in any waybaek Mon-
grelia P. 0., the mail of anybody who isn’t of
much note locally. No questions asked as to
identity or authority. At one P. 0., lately, saw
person receive the lett-rs of others on
his mere assertion, “They’re my mates.”
Also, initials are hardly ever bothered
about. Yell out “Smith” at the counter
and you’ll have a number of letters ad-’
dressed to Bills, Jims, Dicks, Toms, and
Harrys of that clan thrust into your
hand without the least hesita-
tion. High time the Queens-
land P.M.G. dropped his cru-
sade against Southern news-
papers and began to straighten
up things at home.

Apropos the recent Bulletin discussion re
the common pig’s susceptibility to snake-poison.
The matter was settled at Broadford, Vic., the
other day, when a tiger-snake bit a fat pig. The
porker attacked his enemy, killed him, and with
piggish philosophy ate him afterwards, and then
made a nest in the straw —and died in one tableau,
without slow music.

Dear Bul-
Metin. A re-
cent Bulletin
correspondent
wonders at the
hurry displayed
by country re-
sidents (gener-
ally very, very
tired) when in
pursuit of
trains. This is
the only time
they are ever
known to be
hasteful, barr-
ing the great
hurry they evince in not paying debts. I sell wir-
nails, ploughs and other handy articles to this class
of the human race, so can speak with authority.
Our store is not 50yds. from the railway-station,
and yet from 2 to 3 p.in. daily the place is crowded
with them (mostly women)'all scrambling to be
served first, because they want to go somewhere by
the train that starts at five o’clock. Out of the
large number that I know there's not one who’d
dare be later on the platform than an hour before
train-time. —Q. A.H.

“P.T.”: Outside the way-back pub. we
tied our horses to the dilapidated fence, and
Steve tumbled over a fossicking hen as we went
through the bar door. “Bricky” knocked upon
the dirty, beer-stained counter, and a greasv,
blear-eyed man appeared through a doorway,
bringing with him the sound of much caterwauling
in the rear. “ Missus hammerin’ the kids,” he
explained. “Mine’s beer,” said Steve. “Got
none,” said the publican. “ Missus gave the las’,
to a swaggy ’smornin’—it was sour.” “Gin,”
said Steve. Pubjican took a bottle from the shelf,
and carefully poured out a nip of gin. “ Brandy, ”
said “ Bricky.” Publican took down the same
bottle, shook it slightly, and gave “ Bricky ” his
brandy. “ Mine’s rum,” I said. Down came the
same old bottle, received a violent shake this
time, and I got ray rum. “ Strong,” said Steve,
winking at me. “ Yes, drop of good tack, that,”
said the publican, proudly. That bottle would
have produced any spirit under the sun if a skilful
hand shook it so as to mix the sediment properly
—and get the right color.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
No two more opposite characters could be found
than Lane and Bishop, the rivals for the 100 f'i 1 "
ming championship. All the training done bv nisnop,
who is a boiler-maker, is with the hammer and an occa-
sional swim, whilst Lane “lives” at the baths and trains
to the hour. Bishop is a man of tremendous P h s'®VT?®
and iron nerves of the Bill Beach order. At one of tneir
contests Bishop was out first and, squatted on Ins hams,
chaffed with his chums and smoked a cigarette until Lane
came out,-fine drawn and as if his life de-
pended on the result. “ Hallo 1 Freddy,”
laughed Bishop, “ fancy meeting you ! ”
* * * *
Writer witnessed several high-diving
displays in Sydney lately and came away
disgusted. Few of the local swimmers know
how to enter the water properly, and fewer
still undeistand what a “header” really
is. Saw the competition in London some
years ago for the world’s high-diving cham-
pionship. Contestants sprang from a plat-
form 60ft above water. The winner (Golden,
an American) had a flight as graceful as that
of a bird, and he cut the water with a swish
but no splash. “It might be equalled,”
said the Sporting Life next morning, “ but
it can never be excelled—that is impos-
sible.” Poor Peart, erst of Fitzgerald Bros’.
Ciicus, was placed about twelfth in that
competition.
* * * *
The lately-closed series of N.S.W.
Public School district swimming carnivals
introduced thousands of expert boy-swim-
mers to public notice—made it appear as
if Australia is going to become a nation
of swimmers, in fact. A gentleman pro-
minently connected with the movement in-
formed writer, t’other day, that he could
pick out a dozen lads capable of breaking
lmin. lOsec. over 100yds. How many crack
swimmers, bar champions, can do this ?
* * * *
“ MacKell ”: Been reading to an old
Highlander that “Sporting Notions” par
re catching fish by hand. On my expressing
doubt about it, he explained; “You go
along the river bank on a dark night, and
when you come to a likely spot, such as a
quiet, rocky nook where the water is still,
you just- open the lantern a wee, and look
down in the water, and ye’ll see the fish
sleeping there and you reach down your
hand and stroke and tickle him gently, till
you get your fingers up to his head ; then in
with them under his gills and lift him out
on to the bank. Mon, it’s great sport, my
word!” “Don’t the fish get frightened
at the tickling business?” “Frightened !
mon, they’re fond o’it.” This ought to be
a good tip, in a few years, to the denizens
of the Snowy and Bega river country.
Meanwhile, they could be acquiring the
touch. Trout-tickling is already practised
in Tasmania and Maoriland.
Via ” : The somewhat decayed sport of cock-
fighting is indulged in weekly up Towers way, always on
Sunday. Went to one a while back, Up at dawn,
climbed about 14 hills, arid found Cousin Jack, also
Geordie and Taffy, present to the tune of a couple of
hundred ; half-a-dozen trained birds, and a good deal of
money—£4o lost on one main.- After the feathered ones
were done the humans took up the running, and two
slashing goes with the raw-uns came off. All over before
seven. By the way, cock-fighting and dog-fighting are
just now undergoing a revival in Sydney.
♦ * * *
“D.J.”: The little fat gentleman who sat on
the box beside me poked his finger between my ribs and
asked me if I had ever seen cattle in better condition than
were our two ’bus-horses. I agreed that they were un-
commonly well-fed and cared for. “Driver,” said the
little fat man, “ yer harses are very well looked after, it
AN ABSURD QUERY.
COOKIE'S DAUGTHER: “But do you
drink?"
THE WHALER: “Good Lord , miss! what’d I be
lookin' fer work fer if I didn’t?”
gives me plasure to ride behind such cattle. Drink me
health when yoii’ve finished yer run/’ And he dropped
a shilling into the driver’s palm. Just then a lady
inside stopped the ’bus on the steepest part of Christ-
church hill and alighted smiling all over her face, and
the little fat man screwed his head round till he was
purple in the face and exclaimed: “You blanky
to stop the cattle on such a of a hill!” Then the
bus <irove away leaving the petrified woman standing
staring at the little man’s angry face.
* • * *
No fewer than 14 competed in the popular
Ladies’ Match at- M.L. rifle gathering, and, strangely
enough, the first three hailed from Oamaru, the home of
the male champion. The winner (Miss Taylor) made 33
out of a possible 35. Two of the competitors, true to
feminine tradition, missed the target every shot.
* • • *
The neighborhood of Christchurch (M.L.) has
recently been pervaded by a Jack-the-Ripper, whose
operations, however, have been confined to horses. He
had at last advices, destroyed 24 stallions—mostly valu-
able by stabbing them in the neck, and was still as un-
discovered as the Gatton outragers.
“F 0 ” : Re “Tassy’s” remark that the main
idea of’ the aboriginal jockey is to jump away, flog from
LOUISE HEPNER, of the “Belle of New York ” Co.
Photo., Elmer Chickerivg, Boston.
beginning to end, and howl like a steam-siren. Continu-
ous flogging—either for horse or man—is no doubt unde-
sirable, but it appears to me, as a wearied backer of dead-
birds, that if the mannikin at present in use could be
induced to wallop his mount a little more it would con-
siderably improve the backing industry.
• * » •
There are few good cycling roads leading out of
Sydney ; that to La Perouse is one of the best. But
when you have reached La Perouse you cannot for love or
money get a drink of anything stronger than ginger-beer.
As there is no chance of a hotel license being granted, it
is time that somebody established a club at La Perouse.
* * * *
Nigger Walcott, “the Barbadoes terror,” one
of the ugliest and, for his inches, one of the most power-
ful pugs, in the ring, has probably accounted for more
Australians (in America) than any other individual
bruiser. He got verdicts over Tom Trace3 r (twice), Mick
Dunn, Jim Ryan (last month), and now cable tells us
Melbourne Billy Edwards has gone
under to the human gorilla.
* * •
The emphasised downfall in
New York, recently, of Australian
Billy Edwards, who went hence
with the odoriferous title of “ The
Sewerage Champion,” is another
reminder that Australia’-s pug. form
has deteriorated sadly of late years.
The day has gone by when Ameri-
can papers were wont to keep stand-
ing the headline; “Another Aus-
tralian Win.” Peter Jackson, Griffo,
Dan Creedon, Joe Goddard, Paddy
Slavin, and Billy Murphy, the all-
conquering sextette who cut such
a wide swathe in the ranks of Yan-
kee boxers in the past, have fallen
into obscurity, and recent arrivals
from this section have failed to re-
peat the successes of their prede-
cessors.
* * *
Details of the match between
N.S. Wales Jim Richardson and M.L.
Billy Heffernan, in England, recently, go to
show beyond question that the long, red-
haired bruiser is no longer a factor in the
fighting game, and must now go to work
at something else. Richardson, who billed
himself “down under” as “Champion of
Honolulu,” started favorite at 6 to 4, but
at the close of the first round betting was
at even, with the Maorilander for
choice. From the first call of time to the
word “ out,” except at one period during; the sixth round,
Heffernan did nearly all the fighting. It was a thump-
ing match from beginning to end (seventh round), and
all that the N.S.W. middle-weight had to recommend
him was his pluck.

to the N.S. Wales Governors for many years past,
has conferred a blessing upon the citizens of his
country by discovering a wonderful cure for head-
ache. The new remedy cures instantaneously, is
palatable, and perfectly harmless to both
I W F 1 A/r f „„„
ladies and gentlemen. Moreover, it is peifectly
effectual, no matter what the cause ot the head-

experience, can be had in 4 sizes, by post 1/4, 2/6,
3/8, 4/8, in N.S.W. only. To all the other colo-
nies 1/8, 2/8, 3/8, 5/2. Beware of imitations. Only

Answers to Correspondents.
No notice will be taken oj communications insufficiently
stamped. Name and address must appear on MS.
No liability is undertaken in any case re contribu-
tions voluntarily submitted, whether sent by post or
handed in, and in no case will MS. be returned un-
less stamps (of any province) accompany.
Back Creeks : Well, Federation with Federal
control of rivers would prevent the Queenslanders annex-
ing the whole of the Upper Darling. And Federation (as
G. Reid demanded and as all the provincialists want)
with each province controlling its “own” rivers would
let Queensland cut off the N.S.W. Upper Darling supply
as it pleased .... J.S.C. : Jack Want, who is mostly
away fishing, and Brunker, who is always on the spot
and utterly usejess, get £lB2O a year. Premier Reid and
all other .Ministers are put down at £1370 F.D. : The
£812,000 of W.A. gold dividends which go out of W.A.
mean £812,000. The £3,000,000 which you estimate
is earned by local inhabitants probably doesn’t mean
£300,000. The people of N.S.W. earn about £50,000,000a
year wages, and get it, and yet there is only about
£2,000,000 or £3,000,000 in circulation. A bank cashes all
the wages cheques on Saturday, and the money has almost
all filtered back through the grocer,. &c., by next Thurs-
day. If an average working man gets £1 a week he might
bs paid practically all his life with three sovereigns

HAYNES GREASES THE EAT PIG.
Mere is John Haynes, the erstwhile Apostle of Decentralisation, greasing Sydney, the fat and ever -hnngry pig . Haynes, having buried his famous Decen tralisation policy, which was to relieve the country fron the oppression of the metropoltan octopus, is now
helping Dibbs, Ross, M'Laurin, Kohen, Heydon, Tommy Dood and Co. to maintain that the welfare of New South Wales depends upon Sydney being allowed to rob the country of the Federal capital.

That boys who are , allowed by their
parents plenty of liberty to play about
the streets or parks, are curious and in-
quisitive, and that they are no respecter,
of persons, was borne in upon the mem-
bers of the Ministr>\ yesterday, while the
Cabinet was engaged inspecting the
proposed route of the Collingwood line,
along Wellington Parade, and the sug-
gested deviations. About Highett
street, as the party was standing around
the Engineer-in-Chief, examining a map,
several of the juveniles elbowed tlfcir
way towards the map, and proceeded to •
ask questions that amounted to an in-
. vitation to Sif George Turner to explain
his presence there. “Are you goin’ to
* build the railway, mister?" asked one
1 youngster, addressing the head of the
Government;” it’s about time you did
something, you know.” The Premier
made some evasive reply, when his in-
terrogator said, “Oh, we kribw you;
you're Sir George Turner,; you jist got '
to say ‘Start,' and the railway starts:
,oh, we kpow.” ♦ _•

COUNTRY TRIAL ORDER RESPECTFULLY SOLICITED.


A MAMMOTH HOCK TOR SYDNEY
For Mort’s Dock and Engineering Co. Ltd., Sydney, N.S.W.
- Proposed Woolwich Dockyard, New Graven a Dock and Encineerind Works. __
—» lT ■■■ H I ■■•■■■■* « r ■"! tf ' ■ --■■■■■■» «»%....» •■.■■■■■* -* - ‘
ONE of the most noticeable things about recent
developments of maritime commerce is the
rapidity with which marine architects have got
ahead of the dock accommodation of the world.
Improvements in mechanical construction, but especially
in marine engines, have enabled steamship owners to
reach, and— in one instance even to eclipse— the dimen-
sions of the mammoth Great Eastern, and instead of the
result being unweildv monsters always breaking down
and coming to grief, we have great craft resembling float-
ing cities and with hold capacity to carry the produce of
a countryside, and which are yet as easily handled as a
ferry-boat and consume no more coal than the compara-
tively small intercolonial and coastal traders of a few
years back. To accommodate these leviathans the world
has but few great docks. Here in Sydney we were
thought to be exceptionally well provided, but the
trade of Port Jackson and the size of the ocean-going
steamers frequenting it have increased so enormously
within the last year or two that it has actaally become a
commercial necessity to provide another great dock,
which will rank among the very largest in the world. To
meet the current need of the intercolonial trade the
directors of Mort’s Dock and Engineering Company
decided about a year since to lengthen their graving dock
at Balmain to 640 ft. on the floor, divided by a centre
caisson at various positions in the dock, so that repairs
could be carried on to a vessel at one end while another
vessel could be cleaning and painting at the other end
without interfering with the one under repairs, or dock
two vessels of 3500 tons each at the same time, thus
avoiding any detention. The advantage of this has been
proved during the wool season, as the company has been
able to fully cope with all requirements without
causing delay with any of the shipping com-
panies with the assistance of the Government docks
for the few large vessels that have required to
dock for cleaning and painting. The Government has
two large graving docks capable of docking vessels up to
15,000 tons. Mort’s Dock Company has already a dock
640 ft. long, as well as two patent slips, power, 1000 and
2000 tons respectively ; two floating docks, each capable
of taking 1400 tons. In spite of all this accommodation
there is still further need, and the directors of the com-
pany now foresee that if Sydney is to maintain her posi-
tion as the principal port in the East private accommoda-
tien must be provided for docking large vessels now
trading here, and due allowance must be made for the
probable increase in size in the near future. They have,
consequently, commenced to construct a large graving
dock at their recently-acquired engineering establish-
ment at Woolwich. This is an exceptionally good posi-
tion, as it is well sheltered and close to the city and.
enjoys a good half-hourly steam ferry service. The
company intends that the great bulk of the docking
will be done as heretofore at Balmain, but the new dock
will relieve any congestion during the busy season,
besides being able to accommodate the large vessels which
at the present time are docked at the Government dock.
It is not anticipated that the new dock, for some time to
come, will be employed for more than three months in
the year, so that the docking accommodating in Sydney
will provide for a large expansion in shipping, and will,
in all probability, be ample for very many years to come.
To enable the new work to be carried out Mort’s Dock
and Engineering Company, Limited, is being recon-
structed, and the capital increased to provide funds to
carry out so great art undertaking.
The new dock when finished will be able to accommo-
date any merchant steamer up to 20,000 tons, the prin-
cipal dimensions being 660 ft. long on the floor (it is so
designed that it may be lengthened to 750 ft. at some
future time), the width of entrance will be 83ft., and the
depth of water on the sill at ordinary spring tides 28ft.,
so that vessels may be floated out at any state of the tide.
There will be a dividing caisson in the centre, so as to
enable both ends to be used independently. It w'U be
the largest dock outside Europe, and, therefore, Sydney
will be the only place in which some of the leviathan
battleships now being constructed can be docked should
they be sent to the China Seas.
The dock basin will hold about 48,( 00 tons, or lOf
million gallons, and this immense quantity of water will
be operated on by three centrifugal pumping engines,
capable of discharging 15,000 tons per hour, and of
emptying the dock in about two and a-half to three hours.
It will lie frilly equipped with steam cranes, capstans,
electric light, and all the necessary tools and appli-
ances for cleaning and repairing vessels expeditiously
and at most reasonable rates. The large workshops
already erected, comprising engineers’, boilermakers’,
blacksmiths’, founders’, coppersmiths’, joiners’, ship-
wrights’, and painters’ shops will be equipped with all the
most modern appliances and conveniences for doing re-
pairs well, expeditiously and economically.
The berthing accommodation will be about 1800 ft., and
will enable vessels of 15,000 tons to berth alongside the
works at any state of the tide.
Our illustration, from the engineer’s drawing, gives a
good idea of the appearance the dock and its surround-
ings will have when completed. The property has a front-
age to the Parramatta River of about 1000 ft. and 800 ft. to
the Lane Cove River.
Every exertion is to be made by the company to open
the first section of the dock in 18 months from date, so as
to accommodate the intercolonial steamers and the wool
ships in the wool season of 1900.

WHAT SEVERAL WOMEN WHO SAW
DARK SHADOWS HAVE TO SAY.
“In every shadow she saw a ghost.”
These words described the condition of a woman
driven almost to hysteria by worry. Not by some
overwhelming shock, but by the endless little
irritations of life which wear out the body as the
constant friction of a pulley wears out the stoutest
rope.
There is nothing more firmly settled in medicine
than that the fretting inseparable from the home-
keeping life of woman strikes at every important
part of the physical machinery; particularly at
the kidneys and adjacent organs.
When these organs go wrong the heart-beats are
irregular, and there is pain in the breast. A bad
taste in the mouth and heaviness in the stomach
tell of indigestion. Dots dance before the eyes,
and the hands and feet are like lumps of ice.
Small wonder it is that women so afflicted get
nervous and fanciful, and start at shadows. What
is to be done ? Let these women answer.

and I am pleased to state that I was entirely cured
by the same.
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems. *

Q. Ministerial motto : “ Sugar at any

N. S. W. Legislative Council—Dr. M ‘Laurin
and his patients.
The N.S.W. anti-Federalists’ difficulty in
securing a leader is just as great as in finding
supporters for him.
-Eighty per cent, of the N.S.W. country
press were pro-Bill at last referendum; 95 per
cent. pro-Bill is the estimate now.
“J. 0.” : Two-thirds of the N.S.W. runs
are said to be in pledge. The Federal capital then
should be either Uncleton or Popopolis.
The DIRTY BACK-DOOR.
This stamp-design was late-
ly submitted to the Tasma-
nian Government but was
not accepted.
A testimonial to
M'Farlane, M.P., is
being got up by the Cla-
rence sugar-growers as
a token of gratitude for
his share in the “sav-
ing ” of the N.S.W.
sugar-industry. It is ex-
pected to realise about
.£250, and will be pre-
sented during Premier
Reid’s jaunt to the
river in April. There
is much local newspaper
war as to whom be-
longs the credit of the
duty being retained.
No doubt the Pre-
mier will take along
his little version with
him—and it will not be
political necessity.
Westralia tia>s disrolled the two Northern
J’s.P. (Brockman and de Pledge) found guilty of
gross cruelty to aboriginals indentured to them.
But there is no intimation that the indentures of
the wretched blacks have been cancelled.
All that has been said about the secret
provincial tendencies of N.S.W. Assembly seems
to have been uufounded. The As-
sembly proves to represent N.S. Wale..,
and not merely Sydney. The first
time that body got a chance to secretly
injure Federation—to injure it with-
out compromising itself with its con-
stituents—was at the secret ballot for
the appointment of 10 M'sL.A. to
fight out the question of the amend-
ments in conference with the Council.
The Assembly might have appointed
10 provincialists, who would give way
on every point, and the secret ballot
would have prevented the appointment
being sheeted home to anyone. And
it put Barton, the boss Federalist ot
the crowd, at the head of the 10 with
67 votes —more than half the entire
Assembly, and apparently almost a
block vote of the members present,
seeing that the Telegraph puts them
down at something “over 70.” A
day or two before, Cotton, M.L.A.,
had declared that a ballot would show
there were not 20 Federalists in the
Assembly. Also, the wild, sprawling
G. Dibbs had been prophesying that
the Assembly would probably accept
the Council’s amendments bodily.
“If N.S.W. be so Billite, wny
growl at the Leg. Council’s amendment
making it indispensable that a quarter
of the electors should vote ? ” is a
question asked every half-hour, and
expected to ‘' knock ” all and sundry
in the Federal camp. But first, it is
just possible that the weather may go
wrong on polling-day; second, the ab-
sence of a personal issue may make
N.S.W. men even sleepier and lazier
than usual; third, N.S.W. has vir-
tually promised the other provinces to
take the test of an actual majority;
and fourth—and probably a stronger
reason for dissent than all the others
put together —Federalists object to
nave any handicap placed on them
by anti-Federalists, and especially by
the anti-Federalists of a nominee and
otherwise undemocratic body.
E. B. Forrest, who topped the poll at N.
Brisbane, is a pugnacious politician who must be
given a portfolio or otherwise disposed of. He is
just the sort of man to floor the Govt, if not
placated.
The achievement of Q. elections was the
magnificent poll in favor of Independent-Labor
candidate Walker at Moreton. This gentleman
found himself at the bottom of the list with
exactly four votes.
The capitation-grant struggle in South
Australia is severely straining the loyalty of some
Laborites; and the dying message of their old
leader, J. A. M'Pherson—“ Tell the boys to stick
together is being used for all it is worth as a
rallying-cry.
Elections approach, and the unemployed
are being adroitly stirred in Adelaide. They are
being invited to be particularly contemptuous of
the Govt.’s new Labor Bureau. But the Adelaide
unemployed are agitated with difficulty; city and
suburbs are too scattered.
Louis F. Heydon, of N.S.W. Upper
House, complains, in one of his bovine gusts of
passion that Victoria built a tariff wall around
her borders in order to keep N.S. Wales’ products
out, and also reduced her railway-rates 66 per
cent. s° as to draw N.S. W. products in. And L.
. Heydon is against Federation for both reasons.
R. Archbishop O’Reilly is counselling
every member of his flock in S.A. to vote for the
candidate, regardless of other opinions, who
favors a capitation grant, and the Anglicans, who
have private schools everywhere, are quietly
talcing the same position. The other churches are
ranged in opposition, and a lovely sectarian war
has already begun.
S. Labor-party has resolved to attack
two more country seats, and, by a coincidence,
the present senior member is in each case a Minis-
ter. Healy is to run for the Burra, Treas. Holder’s
district; ; and Kirkpatrick for Frome, which re-
turns Lands-Minister O’Loughlin. The avowed
intention in both instances is to win the second
seats, which are now held by Conservatives.
Labor-member Hughes was one of the
few members of N.S.W. Assembly who spoke in
favor of requiring a minimum affirmative vote of
one-fourth of the electors before Federation could
be accepted. Yet Hughes himself never got the
support of one-fourth of the electors of his own
constituency, and if that rule had been applied to
him he might never have been in politics at all.
Politicians out of place : O’Sullivan, Cot-
ton, and M‘Gowen, of N.S.W. Assembly, sup-
ported the Leg. Council’s “ three months ” amend-
ment re the date of the referendum ; Haynes and
egan supported the minimum vote of one-
tourth ; and Cotton, Hughes, Fegan, and Haynes
supported the compulsory inclusion of Queensland.
Holman (!) paired for the Council on all its amend-
ments.
Question in N.S.W. Assembly the other
: l< Does the Government propose to bring in
a Bill for the reform of the Council ?” Premier
Keid (who has fought two elections largely on the
promise of Council-reform), looking out with a
glazed and dreamy eye over his barrel-shaped
weskit: “ I have some slight recollection of a
matter of that kind having been brought under
my notice.”
The position of Glassey in the new Q. As-
sembly will be a big take-down to his ineffable
vanity. Hardly one of his personal friends has
been returned to Parliament. He will be sur-
rounded by the. Northern and Central Democrats,
who are heartily sick of his leadership, and by
the supporters of the Worker policy, who dislike
him intensely. Quite time they had a leader with
a head on his shoulders.
FEDERATED AUSTRALIA—THE DIBBSIAN IDEA.
If the electors of N.S.W. wanted an in-
ducement to vote unanimously for the Federal
Bill they got it when the papers published the
minority-iist in the Assembly.
Twelve burly policemen guarded Premier
Dickson when he went on the platform to assist
Charles Powers against a hostile Bundaberg audi-
ence. No wonder the spectators stormed; no
wonder they howled ! If Dickson had but taken
his historical kanaka with him, the procession
would have been complete and beautiful.
Strange fossils that even the veteran
parliamentary reporters of the dailies couldn’t
identify at first, rose up in N.S.W. Council on the
night of the great debate to talk against Feder-
ation Even old man Macintosh was hardly known
by sight, though he is by no means the most obscure
of the crowd, and half the paper scribes had only
a vague idea that his name was Waterproof, or
something like that.
The Bulletin has received a number of
free pie-passes from an all-night Sydney-street
saveloy-vendor, who also sends a letter pointing
out that the majority of his present customers con-
sist of M.P’s., cabmen, burglars, dirt-box-rakers,
and belated upper-class drunks, and that should
the Federal capital be fixed elsewhere he will
necessarily lose many customers, and perhaps be
compelled to shift his potato-can hundreds of
miles. What is he to do ? Bulletin advises him
to consult G. Republican Dibbs, Tom Slattery,
and ex-Mayor Riley on the subject; also G. Pile
and dog, Grimley, and Eagar.
When the N.S.W. provincialists (Cotton,
Dibbs, and others) started their theory that the
Cornstalk Assembly, if a secret ballot were taken,
would be found largely against the Federal Bill,
they made a horrible bungle of their own argu-
ment. If members vote openly for the Bill but, as
the provincialists allege, would like to vote secretly
against it, the only reason can be that they know
the country is for it, and that if they openly
opposed it they would be thrown out first chance.
The anti-Billious crowd asserts that the oountry
is against the Bill, and the same anti-Billious
crowd also asserts that members are dishonestly
voting for it to keep square with the country.
Poor old Duncan Gillies has fallen so low
in Vic. politics that he is now forced to bid for
the “football vote.” Hitherto, all the pandering
to the calves of the local twenty and their bar-
rackers has been done by the aspirants of the
A.N.A. debating-olass, but last week witnessed
the old pride of the Vic. Tories orating largely on
the advisableness of re-establishing a senior foot-
ball club at Prahran.
The present Q. elections emphasise the cry
that Separation is now a Rockhampton question,
pure and simple. In no other town in Central or
Northern Queensland was the least attention paid
to the matter, and in the Central District (outside
Rockhampton) the indifference was the most pro-
nounced of all. As for the North — it calculates
on getting all the portfolios soon and transferring
the seat of Government to Townsville.
“ Br’er Alec’s ” Perth Morning Herald,
which came into the field to shake up the old
Sandgroper press, seems to have at last quite made
up its mind that there is most grass on the Oppo-
sition side of the fence. It has, therefore, dared
to say that Bigjohn has been talking all twaddle
lately, and it comes to the conclusion that his
Govt.’s “ chief accomplishment seems to be that of
bungling everything which it takes in hand ”!! !
The only Hoolan (just before the elec-
tion) : “ After they (the Labor - party) have
squeezed the orange dry, they seek to throw it on
the dunghill; but lam not one of that sort.”
The election has squeezed J. Hoolan dry enough—
and Cairns is the dunghill whereon he reposes. . . .
To make matters worse, the Burke has gone and
rejected John’s independent Labor-nominee in
favor of a supporter of the accursed 40-plank plat-
form. Sad!
Q. Labor-party has been greatly strength-
ened, not by the new blood it has taken in, but
by the impure stuff it has cast out. The rejection
of the shuffling members by important constitu-
encies is bound to have an important disciplinary
effect —an effect emphasised by the fact that
straight Democrats were returned in all cases bub
Maranoa) in place of the ratting
Apostles of Freedom,” as the Govt, press
grandiloquently calls them.
The present Federal scrimmage is getting
well on in its fifth year. It was somewhere about
hept., 94, that Premier Reid asked the other
Premiers to meet him at Hobart and discuss
means of bringing Federation about. They met
in Jan., 95, and drafted the Enabling Bill, and
after much hazy dreaming over it the election of
delegates came off in March, ’97. Then two years
were spent fixing up the details of the scheme,
and now, at the beginning of April, ’99, the anti-
Federalists are still grieved over the “ undue pre-
cipitancy ’’ that has been shown.
“ HIS TROUBLES."
"Please do not walk in front of pavilion during
an over !”
The French move in the Bahr-el-Ghazal
seems to have been largely a ruse to obtain an
acknowledged right to the unallotted country
about Lake Tsad, and the vast sphere extending
up to Tripoli. This means that the whole
of Africa, except Abyssinia and one or two little
isolated states is now subject more or less to
Europe. It is significant, by the way, that the
majority of cable-editors ignored Egypt altogether
as a party to the new agreement between Britain
and France.
Curious what tireless roads-and-bridges
men several of the best Speakers have been.
Abbott, of N.S.W., for instance; and Coles, of
o.A. It is Abbott’s punctilious attention to
local correspondence and his apparent intense
interest in the bridge over Dead-dog Creek that
make his hold on Wentworth so firm; and
Jenkin Coles will trip off to his Light electorate
if more than two or three constituents write
putting the same question to him about current
legislation.
Ex-Premier Dibbs, in the Sunday Shell-
back
I would not be the least surprised if the Assembly com-
pels Mr. Reid to accept every one of the Council's
amendments. From conversations I have had with
members of the Assembly, I believe the amendments will
be accepted. The Melbourne people are very sore at the
opposition. 1 met one of them to-day, and he was grossly
abusive and insulting regarding Sydney and its people,
i reminded him that Melbourne was settled and estab-
lished by Sydneyites. (Beautifully irrelevant.) But
one can stand all that.
Poor old Dibbs is as bad at prophecy as at history.
For N.S.W. Assembly threw the Council’s amend-
mentsout on their ear, and Melbourne was first
settled in an unofficial sort of way by expeditions
from Tasmania. The site of the city was after-
wards decided on by an Englishman (Capt. Lons-
dale), and another Englishman (Hoddle) laid it
out. Also, the bulk of the settlers came direot
from England - the most that the Sydney Govern-
ment did in that bygone time being to annex the
land-fund of Port 2?hillip and to discourage all
immigration from the older parts of the province
to the new district. The real beginning of Vic-
toria, however, was not Melbourne, but the old,
sleepy western town of Portland, and it was also
settled by Tasmanians—the Hentys—one of whose
descendants figures in a firm which has of late
years had the insolence to set up a branch busi-
ness in Sydney.

The Bulletin has always had a kind of tender-
ness for John Norton—that streaky mixture of ail sorts of
instincts—but he’s uncommonly like a wild-cat: you
never know whether he’ll slaver you or scratch you ; and
Norton doesn’t know either. Last week he aimed at this
paper with a dab of the venomed claw, when the Truth
editor told “ Tattersall ” Adams that—
“ You have managed to practically bribe the most in-
fluential of the Australian newspapers (including the Syd-
ney Bulletin) into silence by coin paid for big “ fat " ad-
vertisements.”
Which made The Bulletin smile and turn up its file of
Jan. 22, 1898, where, at the head of “ Sporting Notions,”
this par. was printed :
“So far, nothing has been heard from George Adams
in the matter of protecting his sweep-patrons from rac-
ing blackmailers. ‘ Tattersall ’ draws a large income
from the Australian sporting public, and, hitherto, his
name has been synonymous with probity and honor. But
the ‘ information’-leakages in his office —most of them
must be from his office—are disgusting many of his best
friends and supporters. If you draw a horse in ‘ Tatter-
sail’s’ sweep nowadays, you risk receiving a demand
from the owner for half the prize, accompanied with the
significant threat that, if you don’t agree, the horse
won't run. That sort of thing stinks. You pay your
money down in the faith that you will receive the adver-
tised prize, and it is disgraceful that you should be bled
by the horse-owner—acting on information which, as
already remarked, must, in the great majority of cases,
be received from some of * Tattersali’s ’ officials. George
Adams has so far dealt honestly with the Australian
public, and no one grudges him the handsome commission
on his enterprise. Let him continue to see that his
clients are dealt by squarely, and draw his sweeps on the
day of the race, or there will presently be room in his
business for someone else with better methods.”
There is more point in that par. than in columns of
Norton’s crusading blatherskite and grotesque self-
praise, which it preceded by over a year. In fact, The
Bulletin believes this was about the first pointed
criticism of “ Tattersall” that appeared ; and it has been
followed up in these columns (which have, and had all
along, been printing Tatt.’s “fat advts.”) on many
occasions since, long before the magniloquent John
flamed into adjectival fury. And The BuLLETiN’scriticism,
besides being early, was just; much of Norton’s isn’t just,
in addition to the fact that John comes in very late in the
day. The “Tattersall” business has been mismanaged, and
run on bad methods; but if it comes to a question of
personal honesty between George Adams and John
Norton, this paper believes in George Adams, and so does
everybody else who knows “ the old man.” There is only
one tangible statement in Norton’s last three-column
assault, and that points out that George Adams collared
an allegedly dishonest official at Brisbane sweep-drawing,
and stopped his game. That, singularly enough, is appa-
rently given as a proof of Adams’s dishonesty !
There can be no question that, if “ Tattersall’s ”
sweeps are to go on, the vicious practices connected with
them must be promptly ended. Whatever foolish ad-
visers tell him, The Bulletin asks, and has many times
asked, Mr. Adams to break with the past and start a
better system for the future. All sweeps to be drawn on
the day of the race. No quarter (and no third—nothing)
to blackmailing horse-owners. Unclaimed moneys to be
advertised at once and continuously. If not claimed, the
money to be held in a public fund for a definite period
before distribution among charities.
* * • •
As most people guessed, it was not long after
Sydney D.T. took to publishing “Tatt.’s" results that
the same journal roped in a big double-column advt. pro-
claiming 1 the virtues of “ Tattersall.” Does this step of
the Govt.-respecting D.T. presage the early return of
“ Tatt.” from his island exile to—say. the Federal
capital ?
* * ’ * *
Year after year the best three-year-old is hailed
by Australian sporting writers as “the colt of the cen-
tury,” and “ better than Carbine.” Dreadnought, New-
haven, Aurum, and now Bobadil have each been gushed
over. In the case of the first three, they did nothing
after three years, and though Bobadil has done well to
date, he has still to equal the old horse’s two Sydney Cups
under 9st. and 9.9, and his second and first in Melb. Cups
under lOst. and 10.5 Besides, they were racehorses then.
To be champion amongst horses' like Abercorn, Melos,
Marvel, Lochiel, &c., is different to being champion now.
Carbinier and Iramoo, from the Miller stable,
are both of them likely to distinguish themselves at
Onkaparinga. The Carbine horse was short of condition
when he ran at Flemington, the other week, but he did
sufficiently well, and may snap the Onkaparinga Cup.
• • • »
A deputation approached the Bible Christian
Conference at Adelaide the other day on the well-worn
subject of the tote and its horrors, and asked the confer-
ence to put its big flat blucher on the evil thing. One
member stated, by way of evidence, that ten small boys
were recently found playing pitch-and-toss, and eight of
them were Sunday-school scholars. If this means any-
thing, it means that it is necessary to immediately sup-
press Sunday-schools.
* * * *
“ We will want a slow bowler !” is the chorus
of the departed Australian team from selectors down-
ward, but they left Giffen and M‘Kibbin behind, and
there is no Harry Trott. It certainly looks as if—Giffen
being, apparently, impossible for “certain reasons”—
M‘Kibbin might have been taken and the chance of
being no-balled for throwing risked. As it is, there is not
a slow bowler in the team. M‘Leod and Noble are
medium-pacers. Who does not remember that Harry
Trott’s “googlers” won the second test in England last
time?
* * * *
The Adelaide match tended to confirm the
opinion that the Australian XI. is weak in bowling.
Howell, Trumble, and Jones were comparatively harm-
less on a wieket that helped them much ; Noble only-
bowled well for a few overs. M‘Leod, who took 8 wickets
for 94 in the two innings, was the only one who bowled
well. In the face of this the decision to pick Laver and
Trumper and ignore Giffeu and M‘Kibbin is a very risky
one.
• * • *
Bad luck for the Australian team if Kelly’s
illness proves serious. He has suffered from an ear-
trouble for weeks, and in Adelaide it became so serious
that an ear-specialist performed an operation. It is
expected that the voyage ’Ome will finish the cure.
• * * *
At Adelaide, Iredale shone out as the sound
batsman of the Australian XI., but his batting- was pain-
fully slow ; in fact, it looks as if the team will tire the
crowd once Darling and Hill are disposed of. Atone
time, in Adelaide, Iredale and Gregory took 70min. to
make 35 runs on a perfect wicket. The only other bats
who starred in the match were the erratic Fred. Jarvis,
Jack Lyons and Trumper. The latter’s batting was
beautiful, but his intense nervousness must be conquered
if he is to succeed in England.
* * * *
Geo. Giffen is the most popular cricketer in
S.A. just now, but feeling against Darling is so strong
that when he went out to bat on the Saturday he was
greeted with a storm of hoots. The S.A. public attribute
Giffen’s exclusion to Darling and Trumble; Gregory is
believed to have strongly advocated his inclusion.
* * * •
Giffen never bowled better than he did last
week against the flower of Australian batting. In the
first innings he secured four for 99 and had four chances
missed off him, and in the second his bowling was nigh
unplayable.
* • * *
The idiosyncraeies of the Adelaide crowd : —
At the recent cricket-match they wildly cheered Jarvis
when making his score of 75, and hooted him an hour
later when he missed a hard catch. Giffen was cheered
every time he got a ball past a batsman, but when he was
hit to the boundary there was stony silence. Each of
the selectors was received with groans, and if Major
Wardill had had to go in to bat there would have been a
gory riot.
* * * *
One of the most pathetic sights ever seen was
the spectacle of George Giffen shaking hands with and
saying good-bye to his deadly enemy, Major Wardill, on
board the Ormuz at Largs Bay. What their real feelings
were is another question, but one spectator, a prominent
member of S.A. Cricket Association, was so overwhelmed
at the spectacle that he went and shook hands with a
parrot which was pathetically shrieking “ mother 1” in the
steerage.
* » * •
The “deep-thinking expert ” cricket editor for
Melb. Age has committed himself to the opinion that
Laver will be a failure in England.
* • * •
Giller’s claims to a place in the Australian XI
were never seriously thought of in N.S.W., yet he is
eighth in batting and third in bowling in first-class
cricket for the season.
* * * *
There are many conflicting rumors as to the
terms on which Trumper has been taken to England with
the Australian XI. One that he receives f'2 per week
and his expenses ; another £2OO, his exes, and a bonus if
he does anything remarkable ; and Major Wardill states
that, “so far as he knows, Trumper is to receive the
same share as other members of the team.” There should
be no doubts on such a matter. It appears Kelly only
had a half-share last trip, and a rotten precedent was
thus established. As three players form the selection
committee, and cash may — The Bulletin does not say it
now does—hereafter outweigh the desire to see Austra-
lian cricket on top, there is apparently nothing to pre-
vent the selectors from picking any second-rater who has
an ambition to make the trip and will go cheap. If
th ngs are not altered we may see something like this :
“Tenders are invited from cricketers willing to form an
Australian team for England. Tenderers to state whether
they are prepared to pay their own expenses ; also
what salary is expected, if any. The highest, or any
ability, not necessarily accepted.” By that time cricket
will probably be as dead as Julius Caesar.
* • » *
Victoria, as premier province for the year,
grumbled at the small representation awarded it in the
“ test ” matches-only 7 to N.S. W.’slO. The averages-
table, compiled to include only matches for the Sheffield
Shield and test-matches, and not bolstered up with those
against Tasmania and M.L., show the grievance to be
well founded. In batting Victoria claims sof the first 10
and 4 out of 8 bowlers, Trumble, M‘Leod, and Giller being
actually the first three.
* * * *
“ Newhaven ” Jackson took the double at Vic.
League sports on Saturday—the mile Vic. championship
and the five-mile Australasian championship both falling
to him. Walne’s tyre punctured in the heat of the mile,
and he failed to show up at all in the long race. The
slow-run final of the mile caused the odds of 5 to 6 on
Jackson to veer round to 2 to 1 on Walker during the
running, but the Canadian lurched half a wheel in the
lightning lap at the end.
The crowd that infested the “ outside ” ring at
Melb. Exhibition on Saturday provided a fair index to the
present position of cycling’in Australia. The “Rats”
push and former members of that esteemed organisation,
promoted to big cigars and shiny belltoppers, occupied
nearly half the space, and writer counted no less than
10 convicted men (mind you, men he has seen convicted—
picking pockets, welshing, confidence, and assault and
robbery) swelling it where betting-tickets were thickest.
The elaborate cycling club promoted by Vic.
League, and opened with a glorious flourish of trumpets
by Chief Justice Madden, is now a thing of the past, and
only awaits the auctioneer and the vanman. It has ever
been a huge white elephant. To oblige about a dozen
cycling hangers-on who loafed round the bar, the League
has been losing money steadily ever since it took the
premises. Not one out of 20 members ever set foot in
the place.
M.L. bikisfc Reynolds, paced by triplet and
tandem, skipped over a mile on Auckland Domain in
ziuin. io o-osec. —a world's grass record.
• * * *
Five hundred people howled themselves hoarse
in favor of one side or the other—you could hear them
streets away —when Martin was declared winner of
oolden Gate Club (Sydney) 9st. 41b. tournament. Satur-
day night last Martin and Webster had fought four
savage, gruelling rounds —Martin rushing, head-down,
most of the tune, till at last, with a full head of steam
shfiLilder fetched up good and hard against
Webster s victualling-department, sending him beyond
recall during the allotted lOsec. Referee explained that
he did not think the foul intentional, and awarded
Martin the money—hence the uproar. On this reasoning
a man might—accidentally, of course—kick another into
oblivion and still win. “ Push” rules, surely.
» * * *
Jack M'Gowan, the pocket Adonis who has in
his time knocked out so many Australian featherweights,
but who was outpointed by the heavier, lengthier, and
reachier” Tot Higgins at Melb. the other night, after
15 rattling rounds, is a marvel as pugs, go, and stands as
a reproach to the Yankee and British artists who live on
the reputation of one fight for a year or two. The father
of two rising lightweights, Jack still looks a mere lad at
28, and, though he fights on an average twice a month
.' s evei Y wee k, he bears not a mark of either.
M Cowan has probably fought more fights in the past two
years than all the pugdom of the Yewnited States during
the same period. But, instead of talking over his last
scrap until he is shamed into the next, John seems to
regard fighting simply as the day’s work, and goes on
with it just as another man punches clay or writes para-
graphs.
* • * *
Champion Harry Dunn, after coming out top
dog in a dozen or more matches against the world’s
‘ rasthng” wonders, met his Waterloo last Saturday at
Agri. Society’s Ground (Sydney) in a very simple manner.
Deplorably off in condition and recognising nobody of
note among the names of the entrants, Dunn went out to
“pick up” the money offered. Bad and all as he was,
Harry easily accounted for one, Martin, but ran against a
snag in German strong man Pagel, competing under the
name of Williams. Pagel, an absolute novice at the
game but fit as a fiddle, grasped the situation imme-
diately and went purely on the defensive—making Dunn
do all the work, until latter became exhausted, which
soon happened. Dunn secured one fair collar-and-elbow
style, and Pagel got the other two. In catch-as-catch-
can Harry went to the bridge early to have a spell, but
received such a severe mauling from the Teutonic Samson
that he had perforce to cry go. Another instance of a
strong, well-trained comparative novice at the business
settling a champion who, in train, could beat a paddock-
ful of chumps.
* * * *
A.U.S.N. team won the tug-of-war champion-
ship in connection with St. Patrick’s Day sports, finished
at Agri. Society’s grounds (Sydney) Saturday last. Ten
teams of ten men each competed. Winners were all
brawny, deep-chested, hard-fisted wharf - laborers—
average weight, 15st. (J|lb. Dalton (18st. 61b), Sherry
(17.2), and Cummings (16.4) hung on to the back end of
the hemp as if fixed there. Police team made the best
showing against the horny-handed lumpers ; won one
pull, and might have got away with the other but their
hands were too soft for the business. A boom in tug-of-
war contests is imminent—writer knows of 20 teams in
active training.
* * * *
Champion-swimmer “Mud” Bishop and Fred
Lane meet for the fifth time this season at Fitzroy Dock,
Cockatoo Island, to-day(Saturday)over a 100yds. straight-
away course. Each has two wins to his credit. Provided
nothing interferes with arrangements made at time of
writing, Lane will leave for Melb. immediately after the
race so as to catch the England-bound Orient-liner
Omrah. Both men have been training hard for to-day’s
contest. Most good judges think Bishop will win after a
hot struggle, but writer’s dollar is going on Lane.
* * * *
Vic. eight-oar championship fell to Ballarat
club on Saturday, when the goldfields’ crew, full of
stamina, easily put down the three competing metro-
politan clubs. This season, the championship was
decided on points, instead of falling to the winners of the
eight-oared race, but, as things fell out, it was all the
same. Dawson, the sculling crack, stroked the winning
crew. The race was pulled against a strong tide and
heavy wind, and was by far the slowest on record—2lmin.
55sec.
« * • *
Rupert Clarke, a titled Vic. personage, by no
means “on his uppers,” drew' the w'inner in “Tatt.’s”
Ballarat Cup sweep. Some years ago, a woman, whose
house was mortgaged, borrowed 55., got a ticket, and
drew £9OO. The mortgage was cleared, and her husband
promptly drank himself to death. Some people have
luck.
• • • •
Notable features about N.S. W. bowling-rink
championship, concluded last week, were that the last
three rinks left in came from the one club (City), and
were all that body was represented by. One of the two
teams that contested the final was got together privately
and in opposition to the wishes of the committee which
had selected two representative quartets ; and yet those
who went in “ on their own ” were only beaten by three
points after leading most of the journey. Bookmaker
Bill Lyons was one of them, and he played a perfect
game. Muir, Hoskings, Hale, and Harris were the
winners. City Club now has the individual champion
and the champion four for this season among its mem-
bers, while its prospects of getting away with the club
supremacy are not by any means bad.
* * * *
W. B. Bell, on a Swift cycle and Dunlop tyres,
has just lowered his own Maoriland 50 miles road-
record by 14min. 405e0., covering the distance in 2hrs.
49sec., beating the Australian record by 12min. llsec.
On Monday night Sydney Centennial Hall
was packed for the anti-Federal meeting—plat-
form, galleries, and floor. At the doors late
comers climbed on the shoulders of those in front,
and cursed loudly at not being able to get in to
see the fun. When G. Republican Dibbs stood up
and began to speak this writer felt sorry for the
old man—he looked so old, so tired, and so hun-
gry for bed. He wore a little round cap on the
back of his head, and, although he expanded his
onoe-noble chest to its uttermost, his voice could
not be heard half-way down the northern gallery.
For the first five minutes there was perfect silence
and every promise of a model meeting. Then an
awful voice yelled, “What about Montgomery's
widow?” At 8.20 the crowd was howling
for John Norton, and that erratic fire-eater
waved his pocket-handkerchief at them and
made cabalistic signs. This amused some
and angered others, who told John to put
his nose-rag in his pocket. By 8.25 Dibbs was not
speaking as far as the audience was concerned ; he
was simply gesticulating, expanding his chest,
bending himself double, and training the point of
his beard on various parts of the hall. Suddenly,
without warning, he sat down. Then Norton
arose, and there was a tremendous demonstration.
He opened by pouring vitriol upon some indi-
vidual for “interrupting all the evening.” This
was unfair, as half the hall had been interrupting.
Thence for a while, right down to the man who
wanted to know about Montgomery’s widow,
everybody maintained sullen silence. Norton felt
uncomfortable ; he was like the good little boy
who, in company with a lot of very low
mates, suddenly meets his maiden church-going
aunt, just as the dog-fight is starting. He
began to tell the audience that he was in good
company, that Dibbs was not half a bad chap,
he’d done a lot of good at different times, stopping
land sales when somebody was selling public estate
at the rate of £2,000,000 per annum. And he’d
saved the credit of all Australia at Bank-Smash
time. Oh, yes ! his new pal George was not too
bad. As for Dr. M‘Laurin (hoots, howls, tramp
of mastodons,)—“ callous somethings like Reid
had sneered at him for his care of the fatherless
and the widow, but it was the grandest”—and
then even John’s awful clang was swallowed up
in tumult. Dr. M‘Laurin was Chancellor of the
University, and how many of them were poor,
toiling working-men with sons, good sons
that they hoped to see in the University?
What John was driving at was not
very clear, and as he didn’t stop to explain
it, it must remain a mystery, but it looked
very like a “ smooge.” When John spoke of the
Council, a man asked him why he couldn’t trust
the people to squelch the bill if it was unfit for
consumption ? and John couldn’t answer him ; he
ignored him. Thenceforward his speech, as far
as it could be heard and understood, wild
abuse of Melbourne and appeals to the work-
ing men not to allow themselves to be cajoled
into a union in which a hide-bound, impregnable
central government would collect three times as
much money from them as it actually needed
and give it to the “lame ducks.” (Dibbs
had called the other provinces “lame ducks.”)
When Norton had grown so hoarse that he could
speak no more, a tall man in a black coat
got up and gibbered at the people. Miss
Rose Scott stood to say “a few words”
with a block of paper in her hand.
The audience grew restive. Miss Rose Scott
is not a good elocutionist, her style is sing-
song, and when it was seen that she was
bent on going right through the block of
MS. an intense feeling ran through the crowd
that something would break. The people didn’t
want to be rude to a lady, especially to' one who
looked so kindly, but they seemed to feel that
Miss Rose Scott must be stopped somehow.
They clapped their hands gently but firmly
and tramped softly with their feet, and made a
subdued roaring noise. But Miss Scott still held
on and placed sheet after sheet on the table till
her hands were empty. Dibbs rose up "and bent
himself double, held the candle in one hand and a
paper in the other, and the people on the plat-
form began to wave their handkerchiefs and
shout, and those in the body of the hall did like-
wise, and there was an awful uproar, and sud-
denly the hands went down and another lot went
up again, but not nearly so many, and John Nor-
ton bellowed something and waved his Butler cap,
and the audience howled, and cheered, and hooted,
and started to file out.

The essence of Justice Hodges’ summing-
up in the Dungey-Bromley case was that there
was no evidence of “ a wicked mind ” in the act
of the accused. To demand evidence of “ a
wicked mind ” when crime is charged is good law
and good morality. But the phrase, “ a wicked
mind,” means an intention to do the evil charged.
Now why in cases of murder per misadventure is
not evidence of this primary intention as emphati-
cally demanded ?
A CLEVER GIRL
Will always speak with enthusiasm of “ D.C.L.’’
Whisky after her “ boy ” has tried it once. Most
whiskies, she finds, tend to make him dull and
morose. A nip of “ D.C.L.” makes him sparkle.*
A humorous item in connection with the
current mobilisation of N.S. Wales forces on
“ strictly active service conditions,” is the action
of the authorities in supplying straw bedding for
the men in camp. History has yet to tell of the
army that carted straw-beds about with it in its
war-manoeuvres.

Concerning the Melbourne larrikin :
There has of late been an active revival of larrikinism in
Melbourne and suburbs, and the slouching young man
with the ominous jaw has again pushed himself into a
prominent position in the columns of the great dailies,
and is re-elevated to th» dignity of a social problem.
About eight years back the familiar “ stiff-rimmer and
the flowing “ bell-bots.” suddenly disappeared from Melb.
streets. The revolution was not simply sartorial, the
larrikin had really gone under, and he remained in a
comparative state of subjection for years. What in-
duced this unexpected subsidence is not obvious. It was
certainly not brought about by any extraordinary effort
on the part of the Law. That learned hass had done
nothing unusual. It seems reasonable to conclude that
the larrikin was, in fact, a victim of the hard times.
Certainly his decay was contemporaneous with the pass-
ing of the sensational speculator in building-lots, the
wild-cat financial society, and the predatory bank-director
who gave a tithe of his neighbor’s income to the Lord.
Yet it isn’t quite the same larrikin. The old stiff-rimmer
has not survived the depression, and bell-bots. are re-
tained only in a modified form. The rehabilitated larri-
kin of Carlton or Footscray wears either a peculiar little
round boxer with no rim worth mentioning, or a high-
crowned, black felt cady, such as is much affected by
jockeys. His coat is short and his trousers are light, and
only sufficiently belled to modify the outlines of his feet,
always a tender vanity with the dressy larrikin. About,
his neck he likes best a large, striped handkerchief—black
and white is most in favor now. This is donned with
artful carelessness and tucked back under the arms with-
out being crossed. The above is the “ leary one ” who
dresses the part. There is also another type who, whilst
instinctively preserving certain characteristic features, is
blandly indifferent to appearances. He is by far the
most dangerous of the suburban banditti, the first in
stoushing, the most vicious and most persistent in a
scrum ; butitisthe “bloke” proclaimed by his apparel who
falls into the hands of the police, and is gently admonished
by a merciful magistracy. His clothes give him away.
The woman element is singularly lacking at the larrikin
conversazione, and the peb’s customary public attitude
towards the sex is casual and contemptuous. But the
most marked characteristic of the push on its pitch is its
extraordinary lack of humor. The larrikin has his sets of
cxtch phrases and peculiar idioms, but he uses them per-
sistently without any sense of the ludicrous or absurd,
and his laugh—when he does laugh—is not wholesome.
There is no playfulness in the composition of _ Micky, or
Ginger, or Nipper, and none ever made an intentional
joke in the whole course of his street-corner career. He
cultivates an implacable expression, and horseplay is in-
compatible with the push’s reputation for slugging and
plugging and dull villainy. Sometimes you see an
exuberant “ bloke ” attempt a waltz on the footpath,
moving with the cold deliberation of a dancing bear, but
his movements excite no interest in the push bosom, and
the studied ferocity of the crowd is not impaired thereby.
It never lends itself to unseemly frivolity.
“ Gobung ” : Recently saw a rat leading a
blind mate by the ear, presumably skirmishing for
tucker. Sooled Thomas (family oat) on to them.
Leading rat ran, leaving his blind companion in
the lurch. Did Thomas go for blind rat? Not he.
Caught retreating rat first and squelched it; then
killed blind rat.

Bayswater, Sydney, October, 1898.
W. H. Malyon, Esq., Man. Chemist, Ballarat.
Dear Sir, —For years past (about ten), I have been
troubled with “ Acne ” in its worst form, and have sought
advice from the best Australian and London doctors, with
only fair results. A Victorian friend of mine, some four
or five weeks ago, sent me a tin of your “Ligmolliene,”
and speaking very highly of its wonderful curative powers.
I naturally took same “ Cum Grano Salis,” and was in-
clined not to use it ; eventually I tried your ointment
with truly marvellous results, and am now entirely cured ;
and think people who suffer from skin diseases are greatly
indebted to you for your wonderful remedy. I shall
advise all my friends with skin troubles to use same.
Wishing ‘ ‘ Ligomolliene ” all the success it richly deserves,
Yours faithfully, GEORGE J. O’REGAN.

Statutory Declaration.
I, THOS, MUDIMAN, Master Tailor, of 327 Elizabeth-
street, Sydney, in the Colony of New South Wales, do
hereby solemnly and sincerely declare as follows
Whereas I suffered severely from a very troublesome
affection of the NERVES, which affected me so much that
I was quite unable to attend to my business. I tried
several doctors and many remedies, but all of no avail.
Whereas I, as a last hope, obtained some of Herr Rass-
mussen’s Purely Herbal ALFALINE VITALITY PILLS,
which I hereby solemnly and sincerely declare COM-
PLETELY CURED ME within a few weeks, and although
this is over five years ago I have remained well ever since.
The ALFALINE PILLS also cured my wife, and I make
this solemn declaration conscientiously believing the same
to be true.
Declared at Sydney this 29th day of April, 1898.
THOMAS MUDIMAN.

"P CLARKE, Esq., a merchant, of Geelong, writes to
Mr. S. A. PALMER, agent-general for VITA-
DATIO, 45-47 Bourke-street, Melbourne, as follows :
“ Ryrie-street, Geelong, March 16,1899.
“I have suffered for years from that terrible disease
ECZEMA. I was persuaded (after considerable hesi-
tancy) by a friend of mine, who had heard of the
wonderful cures in New Zealand by Vitadatio, to give
‘Webber’s VITADATIO’ atrial. I sent to Melbourne
for six large bottles, and, when I had finished them,
thought I was cured, and discontinued its use, but the
trouble returned, and I thought * VITADATIO ’ is not a
cure for my trouble anyway. I explained my case to
Mr. Palmer, and he urged me to take some more ‘ VITA-
DATIO.’ I made a fresh start, took about another 12
bottles (large size), and am thankful to say I am
thoroughly cured, and have been so for fully eight
months, and think VITADATIO is a wonderful remedy
for blood diseases. I am 70 YEARS of AGE, and a
medicine that will cure a man at my time of life is
surely a wonderful discovery. I was speaking to a
gentleman—a friend —who had suffered from ‘ Piles ’
(Hemorrhoids) for over 14 years. He joyfully told me
he was quite cured after taking 12 large bottles of VITA-
DATIO, but he is unwilling his name should appear,
but said I was at liberty to refer any sufferer to him.
He is well known and highly respected in this town.
“ I am, yours sincerely, R. CLARKE, J.P.

Weak & Miserable.
Lack of Vital Force.
ENERGY ALL GONE;
Makes Inquiries, Gets Satisfied, & Cured.
THE OABE OF
Mr, Reginald Owen.
{From, the “ Liverpool Herald " August 6th.
1898.)
(by our special reporter).
Anyone arriving at Liverpool by train can-
not fail to observe, near the railway station
at the foot of the bridge that here spans
uhe river, the picturesque residence and
boat-houses of Mr. Reginald Owen, boat
proprietor and builder. There is not a man,
woman or child in the district but what
knows Mr. Owen. A reporter from the
Liverpool Herald office paid this gentleman
a visit.
“ I have come,” said the newspaper man,
‘‘ to learn all about your recovery and how
it was effected.”
“ Welcome,” replied Mr. Owen. “ I give
you a complete
History of IVIy Case.
*‘Some time ago I found myself getting
weak and unable to do even the light work
whieh my bus : ness demands. Often I
should be forced to sit down and take a
rest after being but a few minutes at work.
This puzzled me, for ordinarily I could do a
hard day’s work and not feel the least
fatigued afterwards ; but now all day long I
Felt languid and dissatisfied
with everything. Then, again, I found my
appetite gradually waning, till I didn’t
trouble about coming to my meals till I was
called. Then I would sit down, turn my
food over once or twice, perhaps eat a
mouthful or two, and then
Could eat no more."
" You wanted an appetiser.”
"‘So my people said, and I got two or
three bottles of stuff I saw advertised,
which was guaranteed to string a man to-
gether and make him eat. I remember I
took a couple of doses before dinner.”
** What result ? ”
Why, I couldn’t eat a mouthful. The
stuff completely turned me up, and I went
outside and threw the rest into (he river
there (pointing to George’s River). I slowly
got thin, and no wonder, as I was not
eating anything, and then, to make matters
worse, my nerves began to go wrong. I
couldn’t stand any strain whatever ; even
the trains that passed my home, to which
I had long been accustomed, worried me a
great deal.
My Energy was Gone.
If anyone came to see me on a matter that I
could make a profit of, I was afraid to see
them. I lost the money rather than under-
go the trial of an interview. As time went
©n my complaint obtained a deeper hold
upon me, and I lost my sleep. I used to be
awake and long for morning so that I could
get away from my bed, and when morning
came I felt
Too Weak and Miserable
to get up. My weakness became general,
and affected every portion of my body. I
got thinner and thinner. I had to throw
up my work altogether. I became terribly
irritable, and at length I got into a state
which I can only describe as general de-
bility.”
“ Did you feel any pain ?”
“ No, I did not. 1 should have been far
better pleased if I oouiri bsvelrested a good
ache somewhere. But here I was. with no
organic trouble the matter with tn» that I
could make —with nc apscial dicasj —
and yet falling away, th. jv.-gh, i s>"ppos*, a
Lack of Vital Force
in my nervous system.”
“ There must have been something wrong
Mr. Owen.”
“ Yes, I knew that, but it was verysubtle
and elusive. There must have been a cause
for my illness, but there were no par-
ticular signs by which we could tell the
nature of the disease* The doctors said
they could do nothing except recommend a
change of air and scene, and give me certain
rules as to living and diet. Their treatment
did me no good, and I could have once
showed you a goodly collection of
The Remedies that Failed.’*
“ You had a bad time ? ”
“Yes, and I looked bad, too. I was deeply
despondent. I didn’t know which way to
turn for assistance-. My nerves were shat-
tered and my physical strength was very
low, and it was only through reading a book
containing a case which in most details was
similar to mine that I am alive to tell you
the story of my sufferingsr. This book
described the effects of Clements Tonic.
If it cured him, why not me? Our cases
were alike. I first of all satisfied myself
that the report of
The Case was Genuine,
and then I sent for some of the remedy.”
“ What effect had it?”.
“ I could not perceive any immediate
change, but after a few doses I began to feel
better, I thought, and I suppose it supplied
my system with something that was missing!
for I began to shake off some of the des-
pondency and nervousness then with every
dose. A more
Hopeful feeling set in.
I got up and about, began to eat again, and
I needed no second call after my long period
of starvation. The dawn of every day saw
me better. I could sleep like a rock, and I
don’t believe a railway collision would have
awakened me. In time I put on the flesh
I had lost and obtained my usual robust and
healthy appearance. My recovery was so
perfect > that people who knew me were
astounded ; they evidently did not expect,
me to pull through.” Here Mr. Owenlaughed
heartily, and continued.-
“ They wouldn’t believe I was the same
man that a few weeks before
Was a Helpless Invalicf,
but Clements Tonic alone did it, and for it
to cure a man reduced in vitality as I was
is simply wonderful. I don't believe, after
my case, that there is any curable disease
a man can have but what Clements Tonic
will eradicate.
