Dear Bulletin. —You sneer at the mili-
tary commander who ordered the body of the
Mahdi to be flung into the Nile, with the object
of knocking the stuffing out of the dervishes’
superstitious reverence for their previously un-
washed prophet. You also remark that the
Britain of old could have “ held its own ” against
badly-armed spearmen without committing “out-
rages on the dead,” but unless you wish to suggest
that General Kitchener and co. are physically
afraid of Arab fanaticism, your reference to. the
Britain that “ used to be ” has nothing to do with
the case. The Soudan trouble is a religious trou-
ble. As long as the dervishes are open to be
deluded by any masterful new Prophet who may
come along, there will always be a probability of
Britain having to slaughter the pious cranks right
and left. Of Britain’s ability to hold its own
against badly-arined spearmen there is no doubt;
then why quarrel with the musters of the situation
for wishing to maintain a bloodless ascendancy,
instead of a gory despotism ? Better to kill the
Mahdi legend than to slaughter the. fanatical
believers therein. The chucking of the Mahdi’s
corpse into the Nile may save thousands of lives
in the future, and in a region which is 2000 years
behind the times the outraging of a dead Mahdi
seems as politic as the Early Christian destruction
of pagan gods.—F.
[And if Britain got into a religious war with the
Catholic powers of Europe it might gain a point by dig-
ging up some dead Popes (assuming a British army to be
in possession of Rome) and throwing them into the
Tiber. But it wouldn’t dare to do it. The Soudan, as
“ F.” remarks, is 2000 years behind the time, so the
British went back for a moment to the savagery of 2000
years ago to be in keeping with the place. The throwing
of the Mahdi’s body into the Nile doesn’t necessarily
destroy the Mahdi legend. If his followers believe he is
in Heaven they will still believe it. If they believe he
was a saint, the outrage on his dust is no evidence to the
contrary—though it may convince them that the British
have nothing saintly about them. If they believe he will
rise again—well, he can rise out of the Nile as easily as
out of the earth. Also, if he does allegedly rise, the
British will have thrown away the only evidence that the
new man isn’t the old one arisen. Where would the
story about the Resurrection have been if the Pharisees
had kept a tight enough hold on the original body and
could have shown that it hadn’t risen ? Of course, if the
things that were done to the body of Christ had killed
Christianity, or if the knocking about of the dust of Peter
and Paul had destroyed the popular reverence for Peter
and Paul, or if the scattering of the ashes of the early
Protestants had killed Protestantism and proved to its
adherents that the holy Romans of the Inquisition days
were correct in their theories, or if even the murder of
Joe Smith had killed Mormonism, then there would be
some logic in the proceedings. But in view of the fact
that no sect was ever convinced of its error by having its
founder's body dug up and dragged around, the act looks
like senseless barbarism.—Ed. B.j

operations.*

The Taming of Belinda.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Mr. Michael Tottles put up his sign on his
shop-window, and went on stitching and hammer-
ing saddles into shape, until one morning he died,
in a meek kind of way, in the arms of his weeping
wife. And then his son, born and christened for
the sake of the old sign, took up his father’s
needle and wax-end, whilst the poor old mother,
amidst her grief, thanked God on her knees for
the grace that had made a saddler of her son.
The business was a good one, and it lost nothing
by the old man’s death. The son was his father s
child in every respect, and Mickey Tottles, with
his meek, simple nature, was quite content to be
imposed upon by the customers who had imposed
upon his father before him.
Belinda Thompson stepped into his life one day.
She was a big, bouncing girl, with a dark, wicked
eye that fell upon poor Mickey like a sabre-stroke.
After that, his one ambition was to take her to
Sunday-school to read the Bible together, but
Belinda wouldn’t go. She asked him, instead,
how much he had in the bank, and all about it.
Mickey told her.
“ Ah, well,” she said, “ I think it will do. We
will go to church, instead.”
So they went, and returned man and wife, and
presently old Mrs. Tottles was observed, with tears
in her eyes, to tip-toe her way into her neighbor’s
house, and tell Mrs. Jackson, ’midst her grief, that
Mickey didn’t want her any more, and had put a
bad, wicked woman in her place.
Mickey, however, was misunderstood. He came
round to Mrs. Jackson’s, looking for his mother,
seeming sorely troubled. Finding her weeping,
he put his arms around her neck and tried to say,
“Mother, come home.” But his blue eyes filled,
and he fell to crying himself, just like any other
child might do. The Tottles had the reputation
of being soft-hearted.
Mickey brought his mother home at last. Be-
linda was upstairs ransacking her husband’s
boxes—an old-fashioned bonnet of Mrs. Tottles
upon her head.
* * m *
Twelve months of married life, and Mickey was
a father. His baby boy, the apple of his eye, was
to be baptised.
“We’ll call him Michael, of course,” said
Mickey, “and he shall have the old shop with
the old sign, and •”
“No, we won’t,” interrupted Belinda. “It’s
too vulgar.”
So the child was- christened Gregory Claude
Tottles. The old lady went into hysterics over
the name, and Mickey for the first time felt glad
his father was dead.
“After all,’’snapped Belinda, “ it don’t matter
much to you what we call him. You’ll know why
in a week.”
Mickey waited patiently for the end of that
week. It canm, and the news was all over the
town that Mrs. Tottles had cleared out with Alec.
Chicksee. They had travelled to Sydney, rumor
said, as man and wife.
For a week Mickey went silly. His faithless
wife had taken the baby with her, and Mickey’s
love for that child was a terrible thing. And,
besides, the spell of those black eyes was over him
still. Shei was the mother of his child.
“ Good-bye, mother,” he said, at the end of the
“ What, Mickey - ' •
“ I’m a-goin’ to look for them. I’m a-goin' to
Sydney. I can’t live without them. I’m a-goin’
to fetch them home, mother. Good-bye.”
He was gone. This time, however, Mrs. Tottles
didn’t tip toe her way into Mrs. Jackson’s house.
She merely stood where he had left her, staring at
the door through which he had gone. And half-
an-hour afterwards, for the first time in the
memory of Mrs. Jackson, she was heard to sing a
joyful stave, as out in the yard she splintered the
firewood with fierce blows of the axe. , ■
* * * * >c
Alec. Chicksee and the woman he was living
with both woke at the striking of a match in
their bedroom, and gazed in alarm at the intruder,
who, having lit the gas, stood with a pistol pointed
dead at the man’s head.
“Heavens, it’s Mickey!” cried the woman.
“ Mickey, please, Mickey, don’t shoot.”
“Send that man away,” said Michael Tottles.
Alec. Chicksee had often been told his act would
send the saddler mad. And a madman with
firearms is dangerous. He obeyed with alac-
rity, and dressed and went out of the roofh,
without a word, in shivering fear of the man he
had wronged.
“Now, Belinda, I am going to wait here till
morning.” > ?
And he did, with the light burning all the
time. There was a fierce determination in his
manner, and a gleam in his eyes the woman had 1
never seen before. She was cowed, and the man
she had bullied and betrayed she feared at last.
In the daylight Mickey dressed the baby him-
self, and told his wife to get ready.
“ What for ? ” she asked.
“ We are all going home to mother again,” he )
said. “ Hurry, for the boat will leave soon.”
“Home!” cried the woman, with the old-time
scorn in her voice, “ with you—never ! ”
“ Then we will die here together—the three of
us.”
The woman shuddered.
“No, no ; not that—not that!” <
“ Then home. Hurry ! ”
* * • •
It was a very quiet party that got out of the
cab that drew up outside the saddler’s shop.
Mickey, carrying the baby, went in first; his wife '
followed.
“ Mother, Belinda has come home again.”
The old woman said nothing, but stood, with
arms folded, gazing bitterly at her son’s wife,
who quailed beneath the look.
“Mother, why don’t you speak? Baby has
come home again.”
Her eyes softened. But her arms were still
folded.
“It is her child,” she said.
“No, no, mother ;it is our child boy.”
Belinda grew agitated. A look of terror came
into her eyes, and for a moment she gazed wildly
around. Then, with a passionate outburst of
tears, she threw herself at the other woman’s feet,
and cried—
“ Forgive me—forgive me; but the child is :
not ”
“ Stop ! in God’s name, stop !” the elder woman
interrupted, throwing her hand over Belinda’s
mouth ; “ or I will kill you ! ”
Mickey did not understand.
“No, no, mother ; she only wants to say the
child is not hers—but ours. Baby belongs to both
of us, and to you, too, mother. Forgive her.”
“ I forgive her, then.”
And Mickey took the child up to his room, to
fondle it, and to cry over it.
“ Yes, I forgive you,” she continued, when they
were alone, “ but you know at what price. Be
careful what you say in your sleep, or else ! ”
But Mickey was back again, insisting that his
mother should kiss his wife.
“ For baby’s 9ake, ” he said.
The women kissed.
Sydney.
J.J.OM.
The Death Sentence.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
His Honor, richly garbed in a spacious robe, the
voluminous folds of which lent a statuesque dig-
nity to his short and fleshy figure, leaned forward
in his place on the bench under its richly-carven
canopy. His eyes were riveted upon the prisoner
cowering in the dock clinging to its spiked bars.
Lawyers in the court, listening with keen, pro-
fessional interest, but without a human heart-
throb, said the Judge had never before been so
eloquent, so searching, so touching as in his sum-
ming-up that day, and his Honor’s penchant for
improving the occasion had given a proverb to the
people. It was said his Honor should have been a
parson. One barrister declared that he never
heard the Judge deliver a judgment without feel-
ing an almost irresistible desire to go round with
the hat.
The jury had come in with its verdict—
“ Guilty! ” and now His Honor, who had never
taken his eyes from the face of the wretch in the
dock, was talking again, almost beside himself
with emotion. The lawyers whispered and won-
dered, and .the cold pressmen recorded in neat,
carefully-rounded phrases the opinion that the
duties of a criminal-court Judge must be an abid-
ing torture to a man of such a highly-strung and
extremely sensitive organism as Judge Blinco.
But none knew that the Judge, gazing intently
into the coarse, brutal, lowering face in the dock,
had seen grow in it a vivid and terrifying likeness
to himself, and, as he gazed, the likeness became
more accurate in form, and line, and shade. The
Judge remembered the promptings of his hot
youth, ere money and matrimony won him back
to the path of respectability and preferment ; and
horror gripped his soul with an icy clamp as the
last dread word fell from his blue lips, and that
man, who was himself, was condemned to death.
As the word was spoken the Judge was seen to
catch at his side with both hands and fall back in
his chair. He was helped from the Bench and
taken home in a cab, and for weeks he lay upon
his bed, silent and dreadful, intent on some hor-
rible thing of which the watchers had no cogni-
sance, waiting with ghastly-faced, stupid terror
that drove his wife shrieking from his bedside,
and made men think of a lost soul at the gate of
Hell.
At five minutes past ten one beautiful Spring
morning, the slouching hangman at the city prison
pulled the gallows-bolt, and—-
The Judge far away flung himself forward in hia
bed, remained rigid for a moment, tearing at hia
neck with hungry fingers, his eyes starting from
his head, his mouth gaping blackly. Then he
died.

An English schoolboy’s essay on Lord
Kitchener:—
Lord Kitchener ov Surder is a Irish man, but hia
parents lived in Suffolk when he was born. He went to
Egipt to find Gordon corps. Vengens, vengens he cried
and he had so much vengens that he killed all the kar-
tooms and made a frenchman <ro home quickly, a war. of
terrible blood will now come with france and pretty soon
there will be no french maps in school as no country will
be left, the ladies all love Kitchener and my mother says
she wishes she could get hold of him it is nice to be brave
as you can go to feasts and eat awful like Kitchener.

Sir Evelyn Oakeley, late chief inspector of
schools in England, and lately on a visit to Aus-
tralia, throws interesting sidelights on the struggle
for existence in London. “ The enormous increase
of the population requires one new school for 1000
children to be opened every month throughout the
year. The percentage of school-children who are
upwards of 12 years old is very small. The Edu-
cation Department would be glad to increase the
short school-life, but parents are opposed to any
extension, because they require the wages which a
child of 12 or 13 can pick up.”
When Melbourne was sick of the boom,
the young man was told to go on the land, and
there was much gush about the grand prospects
of scent-farining ; also Govt, was induced to show
how the quite-superior industry should proceed.
And it has proceeded so fast that last year the ex-
penses were <£464 and the receipts <£4s; also,
there is no hope for the future, for everything in
connection with the farm is declared to be as bad
as bad can be. So Turner closes the purse with a
snap ; and Melb. has once more to be content with
the Yarra as a national scent-bottle.

Autumn Medicine.
\
Change of Season often affects the health, more or less perceptibly Prudent persons take
“ Spring medicine ; but Autumn medicine is just as needful. The effect of hot summer
weather on the blood makes itself felt, now that the weather is changing; you feel bilious,
dyspeptic, and tired; and there may be pimples or an eruption on the skin ; the damp nights
bring little twinges of rheumatism or neuralgia, that give warning of the winter that is coming.
If you want to be brisk and active, well set-up for the winter, it is NOW that you should build
up the blood and give the nerves a little tonic.

The Sliprails and the Spur.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The colors of the setting sun
Withdrew across the Western land—
He raised the sliprails, one by one,
And shot them home with trembling hand ;
Her brown hands clung—her face grew pale—
Ah ! quivering chin and eyes that brim !
One quick, fierce kiss across the rail,
And, “Good-bye, Mary ! ” “ Good-bye, Jim !”
Oh ! he rides hard to race the pain
. Who rides from love, ivho rides from home ;
But he rides slowly home ai/ain
Whose heart has learnt to love and roam.
A hand upon the horse’s mane,
And one foot in the stirrups set,
And, stooping back to kiss again,
With “Good-bye, Mary ! don’t you fret !
When I come back ” —he laughed for her—
“ We do not know how soon ’twill be ;
I’ll whistle as I round the ‘ spur ’ —
You let the sliprails down for me.”
She gasped for sudden loss of hope,
As, with a backward wave to her,
He cantered down the grassy slope
And swiftly round the dark’ning spur.
Black-pencilled panels standing high,
And darkness fading into stars,
And blurring fast against the sky,
A faint white form beside the bars.
And often at the set of sun,
In winter bleak and summer brown,
She’d steal across the little run,
And shyly let the sliprails down.
And listen there when darkness shut
The. nearer spur in silence deep ;
And when they called her from the hut
Steal home and cry herself to sleep.
A great white gate where sliprails were,
A brick house ’lieath the mountain brow,
The “ mad girl ” buried by the spur
So long ago, forgotten now.
And he rides hard to dull the pain
Who rides from one that loves him best ;
And he rides slowly back ayain
Whose restless heart must rove for rest.
HENRY LAWSON.
From Cold Storage :
If the shipper of kangaroo-tails were to see the exces-
sively clean and attractive methods adopted by the
Americans in sending their smaller portions of pigs and
cattle to London for sale in Smithfield Market, he would
realise the enormous advantage thov had over himself
The kidneys, tails, loins of pork, tongues, &c., are care-
fully packed in most scrupulously clean boxes, and the
nice white cloths that accompany or surround these
ai tides make them look as nice and clean as a piece of
fresh beef on one’s own kitchen table. It is this tempt-
ing, cleanly mode of putting its food-products before the
British public that has caused America to have so domi-
nant a position in our markets.
“Tripang.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Of course Nangethe promised Ruma he would not
speak- Roberts would never learn of his wife’s
infidelity—and, of course, he went down to the
beach when the boats returned and told all. Who
was this half-caste, this woman, that she should
deceive a white man—a man who had steered a
boat clear of the reefs in the teeth of a cyclone!
Lifu men worship good boatmen —they are born
sailors themselves.
“Boss, the‘copra man ’ came while you were
in the shallows—l saw him.” Roberts was stag-
gered. He had bought the girl from a recruiter,
who was afraid to take her to Maryborough, when
she was a youngster, and she had always lived
with him—lately as his wife. Proud of her con-
nection with a white man, she regarded the natives
with disdain, and all went well until Wilson came
along.
“ Curse him ! Only two months on the island,
and he’s beaten me.” The trader’s hand wandered
round to the revolver-butt.
“Shoot it?” questioned Nangethe, compla-
cently.
“No,” answered Roberts, slowly, “the Danc-
ing Water will be here in a day or so—l’ll wait.”
He turned to his men, and cried, savagely,
“ Damn yon, hurry up ; light the fires and throw
in the slugs ! ”
Ruma greeted him cheerfully and confidently at
the door. “ I can see you had no luck; you look
so angry.”
Roberts brushed past her. “ Quick with my
dinner, or those fools will overcook the slugs.”
Humming to herself a native air, the girl flew
round, and in a few minutes the trader was down
at the boilers again.
Wilson strolled over afterwards and stood
watching the men slitting tlie beche-de mer:.
“Look here, Roberts ; if the Dancing Water
brings word that copra is down again, I reckon
I’ll chuck over the cocoanuts and take to tripang.
I’ve got one boiler, and you can lend me another.”
“ Yes, you can’t beat working, for the Chows—
‘Hollythurries,’ as a doctor-cove in the Straits-
used to call them, pay best,” answered Roberts,
gaily.
“ I don't know much about collecting them,”'
said Wilson, doubtfully.
“ Neither do I, except that you can pick them
up off the reefs, spear them, in the shallows, and
dive for them in deep, water. You send them to-
China to be sorted.”
“ I’ve got no divers.”
“ What rot! I brought my two- Malays and!
some Lifu men when I started,, but the natives-
are learning, and! now that the women come down
to the beach, and: cheer the pluckiest and luckiest
divers, all the boys on the island, want to jpin in.”
“ But the sharks *”
“ That’s, their risk,, and! they take it, for the
girls hoot, the men who work the reefs and.
shallows., i’ve only lost three mem”
“ Well,, I’ll see.”
Tjhp-boat from, the recruiting-vessel called! next
day, and* left a few “ returns ” and a note for
Wilson. Copra was down thirty shillings.
“It’s got to be slugs or starvation—the copra
won’t pay its freight. I’ll come to the beach to-
day and look on.”
All right there’s enough for both of us.
You d better fix up your boilers and smoke-shed
straight away.”
When the boats had gone out Wilson went over
to Ruma.
, Dome to the beach: I want to watch them
slugging, and, anyway, that Lifu man is hang-
ing about here, and he is not to be trusted.”
Ruma agreed ; she had heard the women in the
village talking of the divers, but had been too
proud to join the audience.
The circle of onlookers was almost hidden in a
shower of shell sand with which the maidens were
pelting the timid youths who, afraid to dive, were
wading near the shore. When they came in with
their baskets full the air was filled with cries of
4 Coward ! ” “ Stupid ! ” —and more sand. Then
attention was fixed on the boats. Tripang fishing
was still a novelty—a sensational novelty, now
that their own fathers and brothers shared in it ;
therefore the women loved it.
Ruma sat a little apart and watched the per-
formance. She saw the boat gliding along, and
the men peering over the side; then it stopped,
and a man dived into the water. Coming to the
surface he lifted his hands and displayed his
catch. A yell of joy burst from the women as he
clambered into the boat again.
Time after time the same thing was repeated,
except that in deep water the men in the boat
splashed their paddles to frighten the sharks while
their companion dived.
Wilson began to tire and said, “ Come home
now, Ruma.”
She did not answer, but with wide-open eyes
was staring at the fishermen. Just then one of
the Malays, after a long dive, came up with his
arms full. Ruma was conquered, and springing
to her feet joined the yelling crowd. She was
mad with excitement and her plaudits rang out
above the others.
“Bhe is still a savage,’’ muttered Wilson, walk-
ing away, for the girl no longer took any notice of
him.
He came down when the fires were lighted.
“ Those fellows are good divers.”
“Yes—if they didn’t make such a damned fuss
about it. But it’s new to them yet,” answered
Roberts.
“ I’ll go out in your boat to-morrow.”
“ If you like.”
* * » *
“Are you going to dive?” whispered Ruma,
excitedly, as Wilson passed her on his way to the
beach.
“No.”
She was evidently disappointed, and turned
away—rather contemptuously, Wilson thought.
“ Come along,” called Roberts.
Wilson took his seat in the stern, and soon
becoming infected with the prevailing excitement
—as great in the boats as on shore —he forgot for
a time his sweetheart’s perverseness. Yet her
voice could be heard, less and less distinctly, as
Roberts steered into deeper water, keeping a
course inside the reef.
Wilson gazed lazily over the side, admiring,
through the clear water, the bright colors of the
living corals, broken every now and then by a
patch of white sand. It was on these patches the
black bodies of the slugs were seen.
Suddenly he cried out, “ There’s one—over 2ft.
long, I’ll swear. Who’s going for it ? ”
“ No ! No ! ” screamed the natives. “ Look —
the ‘ Moving Pillar ! ”
In a line with them, and near at hand, a tall
rock rose from the reef. A native legend told
how it was transferred from an inland mountai
by Bokor, whose spirit, in the form of a snar ,
still haunted it, and no line must be dropped, n
arrow shot at fish, within the radius of its longes
shadow.
“ What about the stone, Roberts ? ”
“ Nothing—a silly superstition. Here’s a chance
to distinguish yourself—see the women ? ”
Wilson looked across, and saw that all trie
women had run to the water’s edge, Ruma in
front.
“ What ? Risk my life for a slug ? ”
“ No—for a woman’s favor.”
Wilson smiled at the man’s ignorance.
“ It’s worth while. Steady the boat, and splash
when I give the word.”
He stood up in the stern, and the natives, with
trembling hands, lifted their paddles —it was
Bokor’s water around them and they feared to
strike it.
“Are you ready?” asked Roberts, feeling at
his belt.
Wilson looked again towards the shore, and
answered :
“ Right ! Splash ! ”
“Don’t!” roared Roberts, raising his hand.
The revolver confirmed their hesitation, and the
paddles remained aloft.
The yams were bigger than ever the next
season, and the natives knew that Bokor had ac-
cepted the sacrifice.
